THE RED PA GE.
The Bridle-Track,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The bridle-track goes up and down—
A winding scar across the peak
The twilight gray has turned to brown;
Below I mark the darkening town —
The old, old town beside the creek
Breaks out in lights. Upon my cheek
The evening wind leans cool and weak.
Dusk silence swoons from starry heights,
In sackcloth dies the golden day.
Far up I hear the feathered flights—•
That loitered late amid the lights
Of bursting spring, and snowy spray—
Rush, streaming homeward thwart the gray
That looms about ine every way.
Up here amid the dying fern
I lie beside the bridle-track,
With all the olden days that turn
Back from the past, and thoughts that burn,
To blind me till the stars are black.
What though the heartstrings thrum and rack ?
“Come back, O, sweet days dead, come back I ”
Come back, that face, those eyes, that hair,
Wlicre love was hid in safety deep,
Those curls that floated like a vow
Above her soul’s enchanting brow,
Her snowy shoulders soft as sleep.
Ah ! Thou hadst heard my vow to keep,
Who stilled the warm heart’s latest leap.
He gives His gifts, and even so,
He takes again. A shadow-hand
Of silent mystery stoops, and 10,
The beauty falls and flies ; and woe,
Looms down dark wings across the land.
Beneath the wind the shiftless sand
Knows not the way its lord hath planned.
Around me crowd those daring days
Of life and love and tender things;
Dear heart beyond the mystic ways,
I dream of nights and moonlit haze,
We rode the bridle-track that clings
About the hill. The clatter rings—-
Still in my soul its eehoings.
The stars were red ; the peeping moon
Broke gloriously to drown the night.
The clamor of the long lagoon—
I heard it not above the tune
Of passion pure and infinite,
That time we rode the swimming height,
Up, up into the eastern light.
Clang, clang, the hoof-beats hit the road.
The night-wind kissed us as we fled;
As side by side the horses strode,
Unloosed, the hoof-sparks sharply glowed
And flashed, and fell, a sudden red
That leaped to live,; and 10, were dead.
And she and I raced on ahead.
Come back, oh night of long ago !
Dear heart that knows the great surcease.
I watched the wind uprushing flow
About your cheeks until a glow
Crept like the dawn behind the trees ;
Your eyes looked back, alight with peace,
Soft sown with golden witcheries.
A checking hand. Her upturned face
Shone lustrously with tender eyes,
Beneath the moony boughs that trace
Frail shadows thro’ our hiding place,
She leant her head in shy surprise,
And Love swooned down; and stars and skies,
And midnight earth were Paradise !
Since God is fate, and fate is God,
The gold days die, the roses fade;
So life was death ; her soul a clod.
Beneath the feet that on it trod,
Below the Hand, below its blade,
Bravely she smiled, all unafraid,
And kissed the oaths I hotly made.
Up here the evening wind is cold,
Beside the bridle-track I lie ;
My eyes are weak, my heart is old.
In youthful days was hope o’er bold,
Flashed like a leaping star to die !
So, so ! I turn my frail hands high,
To her, in her eternity !
VIC.
C. L. RIVERS.
Some Curious Rock-Paintings.
WHEN Lieutenant Grey made his first
impetuous and ill-considered rush into
the unknown wilderness of north-
western Australia, although he made
no important geographical discovery, he at least
found something that has puzzled ethnological
students ever since. This was the pourtrayal on
the sides of a rocky cave, by an aboriginal artist,
of human figures clothed in garments (unknown
to the Australian natives), and embellished by
haloes. Since 1838 these cave paintings, situated
in the most secluded and inaccessible nook of
Australia, were unvisited, until last year by F. S.
Brookman. But they have the while been dis-
cussed, with some curiosity.. They have been held
to show—(l) The visit of Paulmier de Gonneville
to Australia; (2) that the forefathers of the abo-
rigines landed on the north-west coast, and were
a superior race to their degenerate ancestors ; (3)
the advent of the Chinese and other Eastern
people long before the coming of the whites.
Although these theories may be considered to
have been confounded by Brookman’s visit last
year, the explanation of the origin of the paintings
is by no means reached. Brookman found the
figure seen by Grey repeated on the rocks in seve-
ral places, and over a comparatively large area.
After sketches by George Grey, 1828.
All other drawings were of the ordinary aboriginal
kind. This one robed presence, with its encircling
halo, stood out as an exception. One sketch, by
Grey, shows the halo developed into an aureole,
and figures with this peculiarity were also photo-
graphed by Brookman. The figures are drawn in
colours—red, yellow, black and white —in shel-
tered positions under overhanging rocks, and,
although tlie figure is used by more than one
tribe, it is strictly limited to one district. Where
Hid the naked aborigines obtain their notion of a
garment with sleeves, and of a halo with rays of
glory issuing from it ? The idea must have come
from outside this continent, and naturally one
looks to it as having been brought to these shores by
some mariner, whose first drawing lias been since
rudely reproduced by the natives.
De Gonneville says that on the land where he
sojourned for a while and made friends with King
Arosca, lie planted a gigantiq cross 35ft. high and
instructed the natives to treat it with reverence,
but the details of his narrative do not suggest that
After photo, by F. S.
Brookman, 1901.
Arosca’s land was Aus-
tralia. Pelsart marooned
two mutineers at the
Abrolhos only a few
hundred miles north of
Perth, but it is not
credible that these men
could have covered the
distance between there
and the locality of these
cave drawings. Yet the
resemblance in this
figure to a rude portrait
of the Madonna must
strike many people, and
the solution of the
mystery of its origin
seems to rest in the fact
that the first drawing
was made by a cast-
away-sailor. A ship-
wrecked mariner of Spanish or Portuguese
nationality might have been the sole survivor of
some old catastrophe, and lived and died amongst
the natives, as other white men have since done.
As an act of gratitude for his escape, or a votive
offering for his final deliverance, he might have
drawn a representation of the Madonna with such
rude skill as
he had, and
impressed up-
on the natives
its sacred
character. In
time, those
amongst them
who possessed
the artistic
nature which
expends itself
on drawings
and paintings
After photo, by F. S.
Brookman, 1901.
on rocks and stones, might have taken to copying
it, in a still ruder mode, carefully preserving the
distinctive details which had taken their fancy,
those of the dress and halo. This theory is borne
out by the often repeated figure and the apparent
care taken in its preservation. If this theory is true
the original, or the remains of the original, may
still exist in some cavern on the Glenelg River, and
the settlement of this portion of Australia may
soon lead to its discovery, and some further
memorials.
Grey saw other strange things, a head with
Caucasian profile carved in bas-relief on a rock,
and, w 1 at appeared to be two graves covered with
stones and placed east and west.
No Dutchman would hav.e drawn either a
Madonna or a saint, so that it must have been
before the coming of the Dutch that this haloed
picture was introduced to Australia. Always it
must be borne in mind that what have as yet been
brought to light are merely imperfect reproduc-
tions of the original; if once the original rock-
painting could be found, the mystery might be
solved and much strange knowledge added to our
too brief Australian history.
ERNEST FAVENG.
The Literary Woman.
OPENING a discussion (which threatens to be
interesting if confined to women writers,
and confined to truthful opinions by
them —not extended to embrace opinions
which they think will be attractive), Maggie
Sullivan writes anent “ The Literary Woman ”
(B. 6/8/’O2) :
“Constance Clyde” remarks, en passant, that the
literary man and the literary woman don’t generally
attract each other. Well, since almost every literary-
person believes that he or she is the particularly-destined
child of Fame, it isn’t surprising that the man doesn’t care
to have a rival for the favors of the same goddess in his
wife. The matrimonial co-venture of the literary man spid
woman is not likely to be happy, except in those cases
where the woman comes under the class defined by
Laura Marholm, in “ Modern Women,” as the
grande amoureuse. The average literary woman
may yield a fine and delicate stimulus, but the
average literary man cares little for stimulus ; he
wants, for the most part, from the woman, comfort, con-
solation, satisfaction, repose. The literary woman living
in her sensations, craving a constant emotional interest,
does she not take more than she gives ? Again there
arise, very frequently, from the continued stimulation
of the female brain, hysteria, inversion and aberrations of
sex, and other phenomena of sex-pathology, with which
Havelock Ellis, Charcot, Kraffd-Ebing, and kindred
writers deal.
The grande amaureuse is, perhaps, the only woman
genius, perfect in body and mind, that exists. Hers is a
love without the haughtiness or satiety Of possession ; a
love without repletion, a love of unlimited surrender,
and Anally a love of long duration.
Literary people dependent on their brains for their
bread and butter live out of harmony with natural
schemes of life ; the brain is in continual excitement, and
heart-failure and nerve-disease are common. The literary
woman in time verges towards masculinity under the
pressure of her surroundings, the restraint of her natural
impulses, the lack of romantic emotion.
I cannot agree with Constance Clyde that a literary
woman’s invented love-affair is more real and brilliant
than fact, though I am much in accord with her remarks
as to the benison, from a “copy” point of view, of a
disreputable husband. The woman writer with such an
asse makes many'discoveries in emotions, which are
for ever hidden mysteries to women not so cursed (or
dowered). A bad “ live ” husband makes so, much more
interesting “copy” than Ihe ghost of any woman’s
imagination. The authoress who has to depend on her
imagination for the limning of a man’s character is
usually the product of repressed sex, and rarely compre-
hends the true basis of the relation between man and
woman.
I differentiate between the literary and the “ intellec-
tual ” woman. The latter is usually the cool, clever,
irreproachable wife, with an atmosphere of distinction
and fresh clean linen about her ; the woman who, outside
of her intellectual pursuits,, is completely engrossed with
the education of her children, and who considers her
husband chiefly as their father and the holder of a
position.
Life and its End.
INDESTRUCTIBLE matter and energy cease-
less, from finite elements build infinite
forms; these compose systems, life, and the
universe.
Quickened by energy, matter changes in form ;
breathed through by life, matter grows, many
parts making one whole. Wonderful growth,
from symmetrical crystals to sentient beings!
Death links with life, combining through forms
distinct, but allied.
Amorphous masses by magical power attach arid
cohere in minerals, crystals, having shape, grow-
ing ; these, brpkeh to dust bv strange metamor-
phosis live in the plant. The crystal and plant
destroyed and reformed, feel in the beast; these
three broken, remodelled, think in the man. And
God ? Is in and through all,
There seems to exist a paradox here : the lower
the life the longer it bears its image on earth.
The crystal for ages lies bright and symmetrical;
the tree may for centuries weatlier the seasons ;
the beast may endure half a score generations ;
but the higher the organism, the sooner its form
returns to its dust. Then is God non-apparent
(so deemed non-existent) through being eternal
and highly developed ?
Water and heat in all living tissue create or
destroy ; that which has life grows as it lives ; that
which has lost life turns to its dust, rising again
in strange resurrection : plant becomes beast ;
beast becomes plant; unto the ancients groping
for knowledge, this foretold dimly soul-transmi-
gration. Water and heat created the gods.
Turned to their dust, all living forms become
interchangeable. Thus in the ages, ever ascend-
ing, plant turns to beast, beast to man, man to
God—moulded by life,
’Tis life that makes plastic all things inorganic ;
’tis life that uplifts a crystal of earth to a crystal
of thought; from a shape, nothing more, to a
shape that both lives and produces its kind till
time have an end.
What is life? We see its results, we feel its
effects ; a power exists that governs all growth ;
itself we know not, though we know its existence.
' Indestructible is matter, never extinct; chang-
ing for ever, moulded by life to wonderful
beauties. And which is greater—the cause or
result? No matter perishes ; life is eternal, but
is not present only iu matter. Meteors exist, but
are only revealed when a medium resists, retard-
ing their progress. The body retards the course
of the spirit and manifests life ; we see life exists
when matter opposes its course and grows
animate.
Life is eternal, ever revolving: we are resistants
to its swift current, 1 heated to brightness, heated
to life. Life and death are the poles between
which the current of life vivifies matter.
Energy, ceaseless, moving in waves, moves on for
aeons: sets up in matter kindred vibrations ;
these to the senses represent motion. Motion
increases ; and seeming quiescent, merges in sound.
Sound as it rises, piercing in pitch, suddenly
ceases : rather the senses fail to discern it. Soon
it is heat, seen not nor heard, but sensibly felt.
When the vibrations, shorter and swifter in
recurring waves, agitate matter, light on us dawns :
that which we heard, again we can see : sounds
have their counterpart, sound has its counterpoint,
beating in light. Shorter and swifter move the
vibrations ; light from the eye passes away, but
matter reveals it on sensitised film. And after?
Thoughts are vibrations : can it then be that the
communion of spirits is but the movement of
palpitant ether? Color, warmth, music—the senses
of the flesh know and admire. Through them the
grossness lifts from its lowness, perceiving their
beauties. Then s/Wri? senses, feeling an impulse
flesh comprehends not, feel an existence flesh can
explain not. Seeing and hearing (senses of sub-
stance) show an existence replete with marvels —
what of the splendor still lying hidden ? Sounds
too keen, and so lost to the ear; colors too delicate,
lost to the eye : and 'spirit too subtle for feeling
to fathom or thought to imagine ; but which still
exist.
Mind, at the apex of living Creation, has latent
vast powers that come but in flashes. The seer,
the poet, live in a region where others dream,
sleeping. A few words, a song, from the , lips of
a man (from vivified dust) can move a world’s
heart to laughter or tears : this is the voice of the
mind universal. The star-gazer, reading the signs
of the heavens, can foretell the advent of planets
and comets, and more; can make matter, to
certain shapes moulded, reveal in the sky what
the eye cannot see ; can make it disclose vast
systems a-building. Thus the seer and poet
reveal, half unconsciously, fragmemt* •£ marvel*
that pass comprehension; and even ia man caa
the glimpses be seen of what may be God.
But what can an atom tell of a world ? or what
can a mortal reveal of a God ? Studied, an atom
gives a vague feeling of millions in kind : two
atoms blended bewilder the thought; three, and
their potency baffles conception. Studied, a Being
may give suggestions potently vast; but, as from
atoms, the ablest of fancy could not induct palpi-
tant worlds, nor from tlie elements predicate
suns, so from a Being, or from humanity, no
thought can faintly predicate God.
We who know worlds, subtly reduce them and
come to the ultimate atom and molecule. Taking
as unit man at his highest, who can with surety
predicate God ?
M.b.
AHAU.
Russian writer Maxim Gorki’s real name «
Maximovitch Peshkoff. Gorki is Russian for
“bitter.” He has worked as a baker (at 6s. a
mouth !), as a sawyer, a lawyer’s clerk, a lighter-
man, but never stayed long at anything, and was
more likely to be assisting a ship’s cook on the
Volga, or doing straight-out tramping. Now, at
33, lie is writing about the workers, from his own
experience, and his name is already mentioned
all round the world. “Bitter ”is a good enough
name. He is a dreary pessimist, apparently too
tired to preach the gospel of revolt, except by
occasional snatches. “The .Orloff Couple” is an
infinitely petty and sordid tale of a cobbler and
his wife living in a cellar. She “nags” at him,
and lie beats her, simply from want of other
interest or variety in life. Presently they get a
change for the better—to a cholera-hospital, of all
places. But it means good pay, and work which
interests them; they are together, and are still
young, and fond of each other. The man goes to
the devil none the less. Probably his final
apologia is about half Gorki’s own self" talking:
My wife ? Oh, she is nothing to me now. Let her go
to the devil ! What does a man like me want with a
wife ? How can Ibe tied to a wife when I feel as if I
were constantly being attracted towards the four quarters
of the earth? I was horn with the spirit of unrest in my
heart, and fate marked me out for a tramp, for a wan-
derer over the face of the earth. It’s the best life there
is, after all. It’s free, though it’s not without its dis-
comforts. I have tramped all over the place, and never
yet have found comfort for my soul. I drink, you say.
Well, I suppose that’s true ; but what else is there to do?
That is just Kipling’s :
For to admire and for to see,
For to behold this world so wide —
It never done no good to me,
But I can’t drop it if I tried !
At present Gorki seems to be under the influ-
ence of Zola. He does not go out of his way to
find the unpleasant, but neither does he shirk
it. The Orloffs discuss the question why
they have no children, and she makes it quite
clear that it is his fault for kicking her in a
careless and unscientific manner. People “eat.
without talking, sucking the fish-bones, noisily,
and spitting them onto the sand . . . great sun-
burnt jaws chewing, cheeks distending, thick,
moist lips moving.” It is not pleasant reading
by any means, but it is the work of a man who
has something new to say, and is not afraid of
saying it. To how many millions of human
beings does the cobbler’s querulous complaint
apply to-day ?
Why do we need daily bread ? In order to be able to
work : And why do we work, but to obtain daily bread ?
What's the sense of that ?
TO CORRESPONDENTS.
N.J.R. : Cryptogram “ proof” that Shakespeare’s plays
were written in Tue Bulletin office amusing. Of course,
in dealing with a great mass of writing such as Shake-
speare’s works, with a little ingenuity in fashioning
cryptogram schemes, you could “ prove ” anything.
Carl ton ian writes : Your correspondent was wrong (B.
C/9/U2) in stating that Alex. Sutherland, when head-master
of Carlton College, had to take his boarders to the Scots’
Church. In my day, the boys were shepherded on the
Sabbath by “Daddy” Dales, the home-master to St.
John’s Church of England. Sutherland did pot live at
the school, but took up his quarters somewhere near his
well-beloved University. It was during my time at Carl-
ton. that Alexander and George Sutherland published
their History of Australia. The hook was brought into
use at the school with the characteristic modesty of the
Sutherlands. That the work was an achievement is evi-
denced by the enconiunl of Blair. But, for anything the
boys were told, it might have been written by a combina-
tion of brothers miles away. In those days, Sutherland
was one of the quietest and most undemonstrative of
men. Yet his popularity was undoubted, and this
although the school sports might have starved for .the
want of his patronage. He was not an athlete, and
simply did not care. His temper was that of a recluse
and a scholar.

WE PUBLISH EVIDENCE LIKE THIS* I
The Case of Mr. A. LYES.
(fob the “standard.”)
The requirements of the Australian popu-
lace can only be arrived at by reference to
the statistics which are compiled at various
periods, and as evidence is necessary for the
purpose of gauging popular opinion, it may
here be interesting to listen to Mr. Albert
Lyes, of No. 7 Frazer-street, Leichhardt.
“ When living in the North Coast dis-
trict,” said Mr. Lyes, “ I experienced a
vast amount of trouble with my liver and
kidneys, the consequence being that I was
put to a lot of inconvenience owing to the
acute pains across the small of my back.
As I had heavy work to do at the time, my
afflictions came as a great hardship, more so
if there was any lifting to be done, when my
back pains were intensified to a degree that
I could scarcely endure.”
Reporter: “ Were you much affected in
any other part ? ”
Mr. Lyes : “It would be quicker for me
to tell you where I was not affected, for I
had aches and pains all over me. There
was a cold, painful sensation in the spine
which felt as if somebody had shot a ball of
ice along my back, and that horrible shoot-
ing pain extended right to my eyes, which
also gave me the impression of being
swollen. I was sluggish, drowsy, and had
no energy whatever, the only thing I felt
fit for being to lie down—it did not matter
where. It is a serious matter for a married
man to get into that condition, but I held
out as long as I could, and then I had
to give in, for all the grit was taken out
of me. Being without any appetite, which
had been on the wane for a long time, the
chances of stopping a further leakage of my
vitality were very remote, and as I could not
sleep at all well at nights, there was no
doubt that I was in a very grave state. With
my tongue covered with a nasty substance
and a vile taste in my mouth, there is
nothing remarkable in the fact that I could
not enjoy any food, and in the natural
course of events I got a long way thinner
than I ought to have done. I was tor-
mented with very severe headaches which
made me feel so glooipy and wretched that
there seemed to be nothing in the world
worth living for, and with my weakness
daily increasing, my limbs ached so persis-
tently that I could not even take a walk in
comfort. Palpitation of the heart and dizzi-
ness always followed any exertion, my
heart being particularly bad. It used to
make a thumping sound like the noise from
a horse’s hoof heard in the distance, and
many an anxious hour have I passed in
thinking what might be wrong with that
organ. Like most people who suffer like
this for the first time, I consulted a ,
doctor, who said that my liver and kidneys '
were diseased. He-wrote a prescription,
which I had made up, and said I would
have to keep as quiet as possible, and not
exert myself any more than was absolutely
necessary.”
Reporter: “How did you-feel after
taking his medicine? ”
Mr. Lyes : “Just about the same as
before, and when I saw it was only so much
waste to take any more I dropped it off.
The oppression in my chest and the flatu-
lence in my stomach were still there, and
the urine remained of a cloudy and scanty
nature, so what was the use of throwing
any more money away I thought ? Besides,
my nervous system was completely out of
order, as any kind of noise had a startling
effect which kept my nerves at extreme
tension, as if they had been suddenly un-
hinged, and then, again, I felt agitated and
irritable at the slightest provocation. The
long-continuance of these sufferings had
made me very morose and low-spirited, for
there others to think of besides my-
self ; but, thank goodness! that trying
ordeal is all over now,-and it is not likely
to bo repeated, because I discovered a
remedy which cured my ailments then, and
I am sure it could do so again if ever I
needed it.”
Reporter: “ May I learn what the remedy
was ? ”
Mr. Lyes : “ I have no desire to keep the
matter secret, because I might do other
poor sufferers a good turn by letting you
know. It was Clements Tonic—a medicine
that I had been reading about—that made
a new man of me, and I always regard it as
my only friend amongst all medicines. I
had only taken a couple of bottles of
Clements Tonic when I felt ever so much
better. In fact, the change was so great
that I could scarcely believe it myself,
especially as I had lost confidence in
physics generally. . But Clements Tonic
asserted itself as something uncommonly
good by rapidly giving me a desire for food,
and in the matter of making me sleep well
I thought it a most marvellous medicine.
The health-giving properties of Clements
Tonic were not confined to those improve-
ments, as it amended the defects in my
internal organs which had gone against the
digestion of my food, and by those means
the pains in my chest and shoulder’s were
relieved. The fermentation of my food
was done away with in like manner, so that
I was getting the full benefit of my meals,
and how well I knew it! for the return of
my strength was quite noticeable. I no
longer desired to lie about in a state of
careless languor, for Clements Tonic gave
me the eiiergy and ambition to do some
work, which was becoming easier to per-
form each day, because the pains iii my
back were less troublesome as time moved
on. My heart gave me no occasion to
worry myself, as it was beating with a
regular pulsation, and with the departure
of my headaches my sight became so clear
that I could sit down and read for hours
without the least inconvenience. l I was
greatly pleased with Clements Tonic for
the effect it had on my nervous system,
for it invigorated my nerves so thoroughly
that they became perfectly steady, while
such a thing as becoming excited or annoyed
over trifling incidents did not happen. A
good indication to go by as to the efficacy of
Clements Tonic was the entire removal of
my deranged kidney symptoms, and it was
then that I was altogether free from pain.”
Reporter : “ Did you stop taking your
medicine immediately that happened ? ”
Mr. Lyes: “Not exactly, for I wanted
to be quite sure that all disease was effaced,
so I took another couple of bottles of
Clements Tonic, and when they were
finished it was not necessary to take any
more, as I was as strong and thoroughly
restored as I could wish to be. It is several
years ago since Clements Tonic put an end
to my sufferings, yet my work has never
since interfered with my health—a fact
which shows that Clements Tonic made me
as sound as a bell; and if these statements
are likely to le perused by others, you may
publish them as you like.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.

five folios and consecutively numbered from

Tonic, and also contains iny full permission tp pub-

voluntarily, without receiving any payment; and
I make this solemn declaration conscientiously believ-
ing the same to be true, aiid by virtue of the pro-
visions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Leichhardt, tnia
fifteenth day of Maroh, one thousand nine hundred
and two, before me, "

“ON OUR SELECTION.”
BY
Steele Rudd.'
Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Love is Not Happy.
Love is not happy through and through,
Without a smart, without a rue,
Or thought, with fret and plaintiveness,
“It might have been more sweet than this,
Knew we that sweetness —how to woo.”
Do as the spirit bids us do;
Then naught that comes shall come amiss;
And yet, though skies,are,cloudless blue,
Love is not happy.
Joys are too many, woes too few,
To make the bliss a perfect' bliss ;
When naught but sweet is every kiss
Is it the sweetness palls at last ?—yet who
Would change therefore?—so through and through
Love is not happy.
MAQRILAND.
AHAU.
Honeyeaters.
Honeyeaters—hear them calling
, As the red rose, Morning,' blooms,
And the pearls of night are falling
From the.leaves. .0 hear them calling—
Flitting from the soft green glooms !
Honeyeaters in the heather—
Oh, the darlings, it is Spring !
Flashing eye and burnished feather,
And a crimson sea of heather,
And a fluttering of wing.
Honeyeaters—ah, the sadness
When no winter hears them sing !
Honeyeaters—O the gladness,
And the sweetness, and the madness
Of their singing in the Spring !
TAS.
BARKIS.
Fort Desire!
Oh ! Port Desire’s a haven
Down in the Argentine,
Washed and swept and laven
By seas of star-salt brine !
And out! Souls out!—let’s thunder
Round by the cold Cape Horn !
Laying the seas asunder
For it in the early morn !
Down by the dark Hanovers,
Past by the Isle of Fire,
Swing out, ye true-built lovers.
For sweet old Port Desire !
Crash / Hear the screws a-jumpin'!
Boom ! How the shafting sings !
On ! with the red blood pumpin’,
On to the sport of Kings !
Round by the Desolation —
On with the coal! —On—On !
Ho ! for the soul’s vibration—
That’s how the hearts are won ;
See, thro’ the mist a-gleamin’,
Love’s sweet old temple-spire !
Steer for it, all ye seamen,
Steer out for Port Desire !
Down thro’ the gray Atlantic-
Past, by the square, grim Cape !
Hear how the swingin’, frantic
Old bunker-shovels scrape !
Down by the lonely Tristan
With crosshead-gui les a-clang !
Hark ! how the heart’s hot piston
Bids all the seas go hang !
“lam,” it says, “a-drivm’
Unto the lips of fire !
Get off my track ! —l’m strivin’
For Love and Port Desire ! ”
Oh, Port Desire’s a haven
Down in tfie Argentine !
No soul-ship weak or craven
Sees its sweet glories shine !
None save the hearts a-roarin’
With blood drive safely thro’,
And see great stars go soarin’
From lips of those they woo !—■
Heave on the coal, you Lovers,
Set all the screws a-choir ;
Full steam ! —fire up ! you rovers.
An you’d reach Port Desire !
GRANT HERVEY.
The Alderman.
My door he passes every day
Twice to his work, and twice returns ;
And in between, I cannot say
How many pounds he earns.
At times the happy lot is mine
To walk with him a furlong’s span;
He is my public Social Sign—
My friend the Alderman.
He is of proud and portly mien,
Austere but smooth, and full in girth ;
His sober face has never been
Awry with howling mirth.
The motion of his lordly hand,
His creaseless cloth, his brow unlined,
His very gait—reflect a grand
Serenity of mind.
He never swayed from This to That;
He never yearned to touch a star;
He is as solid and as fat
As his own ledgers are.
I love to note his placid chin
Toward the Sabbath pulpit raised,
Hear threats rolled out to thriftless sin,
And “ civic virtue ” praised ;
Till lazily I seem to see
A vision of the Heavenly town,
Where burgesses of high degree
Stalk slowly up and down.
And my imagination paints,
Throned in the very inmost hall
Of that great Borough of the Saints.
The plumpest of them all.
We scourge ourselves with many rods,
And different blessings claim our kin ;
I bow the knee to heathen gods;
But his —are Aldermen.
PAGAN.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 20, 1902.
The Irvine Government’s Treachery.
The Victorian Government of Irvine, Bent
and Shiels —the iceberg, the bear, and the
old woman—has justified the worst opinions
entertained concerning it. It has gone back
on its democratic professions and protesta-
tions, and it has done this with a clumsiness
which robs the performance even of the
interest which attaches to well managed
hypocrisy. It has always been difficult to
suppose that a Government led by the cold,
bloodless Irvine could be democratic, or
that a Government to which Bent belonged
could be anything but Tory, ignorant,
foolish, and objectionable. On the other
hand, a Government to which Shiels
belonged could be anything that was good
or anything that was bad, Shiels being
a colorless kind of picture which could
go into any sort of frame, and which
was so used to standing neglected in a
corner that it was glad to be framed on
any terms. The other members of the
Ministry are practically nonentities, frozen
by Irvine, trodden on by the hind feet of
the plantigrade Bent, and overwhelmed by
the purple patches of eloquence in Shiels’s
immensely verbose orations. Shiels is the
kind of man who can hold forth brilliantly
for six hours about the reasons why the large
end of an egg isn’t the small end, and about
how it doesn’t matter which end it is.
* » * *
The Irvine Government came into office
just as the Victorian Factories Act, which
also includes the famous Minimum Wage
clauses, was on the point of expiring. How
theTuRNER Government succeeded in driving
that exceedingly wise, liberal and progressive
measure— about the most progressive thing of
its kind on earth—through the bigoted House
of 48 Rich Landlords, will always remain a
mystery. Turner, the patient, unobtrusive,
beaming little man with the spectacles and
the brown suit, certainly looked like the last
man who would coerce 48 of the worst bigots
in Australasia, but there was great force at
the back of his unobtrusive exterior, and
seemingly impossible things happened while
Turner was shoving behind. But, even
with all his success, he only succeeded in
getting the Minimum Wage law passed for a
certain number of years and applied to cer-
tain districts. When it came up for renewal
the case called for a strong, earnest man to
engineer the renewal measure through the
hostile House of Landlords, and the irony of
fate left the job to Irvine. Peacock, who
was associated with Turner in passing
the original measure, and who, with all his
follies and mistakes, and his ungainly laugh,
and his tendency to trim on other matters,
was in deadly earnest over this one, had
just gone out of office. So had Trenwith.
Turner and Isaacs had gone into the Federal
Legislature. In the backwash of State politics
strange, cheap things began to come to the
surface, and the name of one of them was
Irvine, while another was Bent.
* * * *
Irvine came into office pledged to three
things—the renewal of the Factories Act,
Parliamentary reform, and the restoration of
the sorelv-disorganised finances. He was
pledged to all three as solemnly as he and
Bent could be, yet no one felt even reason-
ably sure of his sincerity on any one of them.
His Tory following loathed the splendidly
democratic Factories Act. Parliamentary
reform was hateful to his friends in the
Legislative Council; in that weird body the
property qualification for membership is so
high that only from 1200 to 1900 men in all
Victoria can sit in it, and when those who
are absent, or too old, or who don’t care for
politics, or who are too busy, or are in the
Assembly or the Federal Parliament, are
deducted, the number of available qualified
men sinks so low that it is impossible to even
raise a contest at a Council election. And if
the restoration of the finances involved a
general land-tax, the whole party which kept
Irvine in office was against it—to say
nothing of the fact that Treasurer Shiels is
a miserable kind of man who wouldn’t be
happy without a financial disaster to weep
over. As the law stood, the Factories
Act would expire at the close of the
session, unless previously renewed, and
if anything or anybody happened to close
the session before the renewal Act was
passed, the whole law, with its Wages
Boards, its Minimum Wage clauses, its anti-
sweating provisions, and all the rest, passed
away as utterly as if it had never been.
Irvine propounded his scheme for Parlia-
mentary reform with an air of reasonable
probability. He passed the extension of
the Factories Act through the Assembly,
and assured the country, with his unreliable
hand on the whited sepulchre of his shirt-
front, that he was in solemn earnest about
it. But the House of Landlords adjourned
it from week to week, neither accepting
nor rejecting it, but acting as men who
were waiting for something to happen,
and who had been privately informed, by a
person who called round surreptitiously
after dark, that it would happen. And
last week the Ministry of whited sepul-
chres justified the worst expectations.
It closed Parliament with a bang, and with
the end of the session the democratic Fac-
tories Act passed away, and the sweater
came back to sweat as before.
* * * *
For weeks the Irvine Government had
been clumsily and obtrusively riding for a
fall, and the fall not coming, it deliberately
got off its horse and lay down. It almost
-brought about the desired quarrel over the
threatened railway strike, and the choicest
flowers of Bent’s vocabulary were not
wanting to turn the disagreement into in-
dustrial war. The men yielded, in great
measure, it is commonly reported, because
ex-Premier Peacock pointed out to them
that an upheaval of some kind, and an excuse
for the dissolution of Parliament, was exactly
what the Irvine Cabinet was in search of.
Failing this the Ministry had to satisfy itself
with about the thinnest pretence that ever
served a thin politician’s purpose. Its Re-
trenchment Bill provided that all civil service
salaries exceeding £125 should be subjected
to a per-centage reduction. A member pro-
posed that the reduction should begin at
salaries of £175, to which the Government
demurred, on the ground that it would
thereby lose some £40,000 of its anticipated
savings. The matter was not pressed. But
by a small majority, in a comparatively thin
House, the £125 limit was struck out, and a
blank was left in the clause so that the
matter might be further discussed. The
Irvine Ministry made no serious attempt to
rally up its followers in order to prevent this
episode. It didn’t warn the Assembly in ad-
vance that it would regard the vote, if passed,
as a serious matter. It didn’t wait for the
further discussion of the clause, and demand
the reinstatement of the original figures, or
wait to see if the House would reinstate
them voluntarily, or call on its party to roll
up strongly to force the restoration of the
clause, or tell the Assembly that it must
either reverse its vote or take the conse-
quences. Having managed to secure the
smallest semblance of a defeat, it adjourned
on the instant, and fled off the premises,
and that was the last sitting of the
session. No Government was ever in
greater haste to prevent Parliament thinking
better of its action.
* • • •
The Tory press hails Irvine as the Strong
Man of politics who refuses to allow his
policy to be cub and hacked and altered by
Parliament. In reality, the Irvine Ministry
can only be regarded in one of two lights.
The better and less probable alternative is
that it is a paltry, peevish, unstable com-
bination which let its new Factories Act be
destroyed, and dropped its Parliamentary
Reform scheme, and its financial proposals,
and left the 1)600,000 deficit, which Treasurer
Shiels foresees, to go on accumulating in-
definitely, all on a miserable point of personal
dignity—the dignity of a Thomas Bent
Ministry ! The other alternative is that it
deliberately left the finances to go to wreck,
and hung up the urgent Parliamentary Re-
form matter indefinitely, for the sake of
snatching at the chance to destroy secretly
the Factories Act which it dared not destroy
openly —the Act which, above all others, it
was pledged to support. It doesn’t even
save its alleged dignity by the course
it has taken. George Turner, a greater
Parliamentarian than the present scrag-end
Premier will ever be, when he struck an
exactly similar difficulty, took a more manly
and dignified course. He informed the
Assembly that the vote which had just been
carried would seriously disarrange his
finances, and hinted that the Government
would resign if it was not rescinded. It was
rescinded. Turner dominated the House
instead of bolting from it at the first sign of
trouble. It is a theory of Party Govern-
ment that the Assembly must accept the
Ministry’s policy or find a new Ministry, and
Turner made it accept his policy. Irvine
didn’t want to fight, but dropped his policy
and ran. He said that the renewal of the
Factories Act was the essential feature of his
policy, and seeing that the Factories Act is
now, by his action, extinct, then he lost his
policy in his flight. If the Irvine Govern-
ment wanted to secure the perpetuation of the
Factories Act its sudden retreat stamps it as
a coward and a failure. If it didn’t, then it
appears as a coward and a liar. There isn’t
much to choose between the alternatives,
after all.
• • • *
Two duties now lie conspicuously before
the Victorian people. One is to insist that
the control of Labor legislation shall be
handed over to the Federal Parliament, and
to ally themselves with the democratic
elements in the other States, with that end
in view. The other, and the more immediate
duty, is to wipe the Irvine-Bent-Shiels
Ministry off the face of the earth. It goes
to the country, on the most charitable esti-
mate, as the Ministry which didn’t pass its
new Factories Act, which didn’t pass its Par-
liamentary Reform Bill, which did nothing
to square the finances, which nearly brought
about a huge railway strike, which hadn’t the
sense to keep its party together for a division
on what it now declares was an essential
item of its policy, which hadn’t the courage
to face the Assembly after a very small
defeat and command it to reverse its decision,
which ran away at the first time of asking
and dropped its baggage as it ran. If it
comes back from the country, finding that it
can only hold its billet by offering a demo-
cratic programme, there is little doubt 'that
it will come back with a democratic pro-
gramme ; it would probably come back with
almost anything rather than lose its billet.
But, judged by all the evidence, it doesn’t
want to come back with a democratic pro-
gramme ; and, if it is driven to do so, it will
almost certainly miss no reasonably safe
chance of dropping the burden a second
time. And it is vastly safer for Victoria to
take the initiative and drop Irvine. As for
Bent and Shiels, no community can possibly
make a mistake in dropping them.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Bushveldt Carbineers.
J. 0. L. Fitzpatrick, M.L A., had the courage to
bring the Bushveldt Carbineers scandal before
N.S.W. Parliament last week. With the news
brought by every batch of returned troopers, and
with every mail from Pretoria, there has come
clearer proof that the Australians, shot and other-
wise punished by the British army authorities in
the Transvaal, were scapegoats for the sins of
many others. But the daily press has been
studiously anxious that no knowledge of the cor-
rect facts in regard to the ill-fated corps should
get to the public, and efforts at ventilation
in Parliament have been frequently smothered
by the N.S.W. Government. One member,
who was anxious to have the facts discussed,
was besought to remember his young political
career and not to blast his fair prospects by taking
action-“ which might turn the guns of the world
on to the British Empire.” Despite these fren-
zied efforts to hush the scandal up, Mr. Fitz-
patrick, on Sept. 9th, moved the adjournment
of N.S.W. Assembly to call attention to the
matter. The keen desire of those in authority
to smother the discussion was shown by the state
of the House. There was a marked absence of
Government supporters in the Chamber, though
there were understood to be great masses of them
in various retiring rooms, or under the billiard-
tables, or in other places to which the sound of the
quorum bell did not penetrate. .Then frequent
attention was called to “ the state of the House ’
by a lonesome Govt, supporter, who appeared
to have remained for that purpose only. If the
discussion could have ended with a ‘ count-out
that would have been construed into a contemptu-
ous shelving of the matter. But all efforts at a
count-out failed. Members from all sides of the
House spoke in favor of a full inquiry, and finally,
after Holman, M.P., had spoken, and the growing
feeling had been made clear, the Premier promised
to lav all the papers regarding the Bushveldt
Carbineers on the table and to urge the federal
Government to institute a full inquiry. Mi. 1 itz-
P.vtrick and most of the other speakers wisely put
forward as the main point of the scandal the treat-
ment of Major Lenehan, the officer commanding
the Bushveldt Carbineers, who, though he
had no complicity at. all with any outrages,
was arbitrarily imprisoned, both before and
after trial, apparently with the sole object of
preventing him communicating with the Aus-
tralian Government in regard to the other Aus-
tralians. But if an inquiry is instituted by the
Federal Government —and it is difficult to see how
the request of N.S.W. in this regard can be
refused—it should be made to cover the whole
ground of the operations of the Bushveldt Car-
bineers. The allegations made as to serious in-
justice to Australian citizens are supported by a
great mass of testimony—much of it given on
oath and coming from good sources. A full
investigation will either clear away suspicions
which are now causing the gravest discontent, or
establish the right of Australia, to ask for sub-
stantial redress from Great Britain.
The Non-Existent Tea Duty.
The abolition of the duty on tea is one of the
achievements on which the Federal Labor party
is disposed to pride itself. Yet it was the cham-
pion folly of the tariff amendments. What man
m all Australia can now truthfully say that he
gets his favorite mixture at 3d. a lb. less, or that
his Is. 6d. a pound blend has jumped up in quality
since the abolition of the duty ? The money lost
to the revenue has been diverted, not to
the consumer, but to the importer and the
middleman. And there will be this addi-
tional public loss that, whilst with a tea-duty
the Customs House takes care to inspect all
tea-imports, when tea is duty-free there is a ten
dency to let it enter without inspection. What-
ever the reason for it, the fact is undoubted that
tea is a most immoral and dissipated grocery,
about the preparation and sale of which there
gathers a tremendous amount of fraud, and of
chicanery which comes close to fraud. A com-
munity which lets shipments of tea in without
inspection is bound to get with its “choice
blends ” and its “ Souchong buds ” a very big pro-
portion of dung, willow-leaves, mud, metal-
filings, chopped straw colored brown, ex-
hausted leaves gathered from the rubbish
heaps and given an attractive color and curl by
soaking in tannery juice and frying in a pan. All
these things come to a nation which does not
watch with a keen detective eye its tea imports.
And when there is no customs duty the tendency
is to let tea go without examination; the
Customs Department makes no money out of
it, and can’t be bothered inspecting it. So
far, there seems to be an exception to this ten-
dency in Australia, for C. C. Kingston, to
the great grief of S’Malkum M'Eacharn and the
Cobdenites, has been keeping an eye on Aus-
tralia’s tea, though it is an unrernunerative job,
and has stopped a certain amount of Chinese
mud and other nauseating stuff on its way to the
family teapot. But KinGstons are rare, and the
chances are that, without a tea-duty, customs
inspection of tea imports will become in time
quite perfunctory. Then the ordure, &c., will not
be stopped, and can be retailed at Is. 6d. a lb., to
the great glory of the free breakfast table and to
the satisfaction of the Labor party. It won’t even
fall to Is. 3d. a lb. Tea is a disreputable
incident of the grocery trade which defies all the
ordinary conventions of shop-keeping—an item of
consumption which sinks to the depth of being
retailed at the kitchen door by a shabby and
hungry-looking man, who pleads to you to buy a
pound at 25., partly because it is the best tea
in the world, partly because he is brutally
hard-up and it would be a charity to him.
The retail tea-canvasser gets sixpence out of every
shilling that he gathers in ; out of the balance the
tea merchant finds the tea, the package, the cost
of advertising, and runs the risk of his woe-begone
canvasser not coming back with the cash. Figuring
it out, it is easily to be calculated that the article
for which 2s is paid costs not more than 4|d.
wholesale ; and tea is about the only article of
common consumption which can put on 433 per
cent, in the process of getting from the warehouse
to the consumer. Of course, all tea is not quite
so gorgeous a swindle as that; but the fact that
some tea firms will dump down a big parcel of tea
in a cottage whose tenant they don’t know from
Adam, on the distinct understanding that it is not
to be paid for for three months, throws much light
on tea profits. It would have been the middle-
men only whom the tea duty would have affected
—the consumer wouldn’t have paid more than 2s.
for the pound of tea bought for charity, and the
vendor couldn't have given a more worthless
quality for the money. The tea middlemen are
the class for whom the Labor party struck away
from the Commonwealth revenue the big tea-duty
item, and thus put in extra jeopardy the minimum
wage and the old age pensions. It wasn’t good
politics.
Giving the Flag Away!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
«• During our Great Remnant Sale of this week we give
away to every customer the National Flag.” —(Drapery
advt. in West Australian, Perth, of a recent date.)
Though the curtain be dropped and the cable tells
of a dreary war no more;
Though the nation dreams of permanent peace on
a burned and desolate shore ;
The loyal and true on Australian soil seem loth to
forget the fray,
And still, though in novel and limited form, are
Giving the Flag Away !
They gave it away on a wholesale plan when our
swaddies used to write:
“I enclose two teeth that I took from the mouth
of a slaughtered Boer last night,
And some dead men’s whiskers, and curious things
that were robbed in the open day; ”
Each one of these notes was another success at
Giving the Flag Away.
They gave it away in lavish style when a blood-
drunk Jingo press
Published its sneers at the “cowardly” foe, and
gibed at their wives and dress.
At Nicholson’s Nek, too, and other fields, after
the hard-fought fray,
Some muddled commander laid down his arms,
and the Flag was Given Away.
We would fain forget these sorry tales; they are
things of a doleful past.
But the draper man must needs stir the bones that
we thought were buried at last;
And the spectres rise from their dismal tombs, and
the ghosts from their bed of clay
When the news is published that once again we
are Giving the Flag Away.
W. E. C.


The Tariff.
The weary old tariff is through at last. It isn’t
an ideal Protective tariff, but it is an approach
thereto, and it will be improved in years to come.
Such as it is, it was pushed through in the face of
the savage opposition, mendacity, corruption and
misrepresentation of all manner of vested interests.
It had to face M'Millan, the Calico Jimmy, who
must needs oppose the establishment of Australian
industries that will do damage to M ‘Arthur’s
imported softgoods—though when he was a Free-
trade Treasurer in N.S.W. he showed his practical
faith in Protection by protecting almost every
industry which didn’t clash with the interests of
M ‘Arthur’s imported sof tgoods. It was bitterly
opposed by Bruce Smith, likewise, at one time,
an ardent Freetrade Treasurer in N.S.W., who
protected many things, and denied that he
did it; also by the other Smith, who
was in the same Ministry and was B. Smith’s
accomplice and fellow-renegade. It was
denounced by that ardent Freetrader Reid, who
protected sugar, beer, newspapers, tobacco, cigars,
biscuits, confectionery, and other things, and
who assures the public that he never did anything
of the kind; likewise, by his accomplice and
assistant backslider Joe Cook ; and Gould, the
Freetrader who aided and abetted the large Pro-
tective tariff of M'Millan and Bruce Smith, and
the smaller one of Reid and Joe Cook, and denied
both of them. It was anathematised for eight
solid hours of ineffable dreariness by the unflinch-
ing and uncompromising Freetrader PuLSFORD,
who helped Reid and Joe Cook to protect all the
industries above mentioned, and whose objurga-
tions have in them the drouth of a crushed
mummy blowing through the land of Egypt. It
fell under the strong disapproval of old Freetrader
Braddon, who ran a 25 per cent. Protectionist
tariff, in Tasmania, and would probably be doing so
yet, if his friend Miles hadn’t been found out —
Braddon, who apparently sold his alleged
fiscal principles for office, and got very little
office in exchange for not much principle. The
S.M. Herald and Sydney Telegraph and Laun-
ceston Examiner and Perth Morning Herald all
loathed it as a slab of iniquity and a truckling to
the demon of Protection, and demanded at the
same time that they should receive 50 per cent.
Protection through the postage rates as before.
It was fought, inch by inch, by a strong Freetrade
conting-ent from Westralia, the State which would
only join the Commonwealth on condition that it
was allowed, forfive years, to levy Protective duties
against the other States as well as against the out-
side world. It collided with a very massive Cob-
denite phalanx which said it was Freetrade to the
backbone, and which was unanimously in favor of
heavy Protection for Beer. It was declared accursed
by nigger-labor parties in Queensland because it
imposed duties on fodder—duties which Queens-
land always imposed itself in its capacity as a
separate State. It ran the gauntlet of frauds,
backsliders, stonewallers, slanderers, people with
axes to grind, people with parochial animosities
to gratify, people who were enraged because they
were found out passing fraudulent Customs entries
or importing tea adulterated with mud, people
who were hired to oppose Australian industries
in the interests of foreign importing firms and
wealthy absentees, and other people. And having
got through in spite of all, Australia has a restful
feeling as if something had been done at last.
4 .
The Parson and the Sweater.
It is a curious circumstance that the parson is
practically always on the side of cheap labor,
sweating, and the importation of servile races
whom he can patronise as “my pore, deluded
brown brother.” Witness this latest outbreak :
At St. Matthew’s Church of England, Prahran, the
Key. Mr. Storrs delivered a sermon on the drought,
which he attributed to the wickedness of the people.
The slighting of the Bible and the selfishness of the
legislation that barred people out of the Commonwealth
were alone sufficient to bring down the hand of the
Creator on the land.
A question that is worth putting to Parson Storrs,
and many other parsons, is this: If he, as a person
who knows all about the Creator, is positive that
the selfishness of keeping, say, the Hindu out of
this country is so dire a sin, how about the much
greater iniquity of Britain in taking the Hindu’s
own country, and there depriving him of all
rights of self-government, maintaining a despotism
over him, and refusing him the alleged privileges
of a British citizen ? If the Hindu has such a
darned lot of rights here, why hasn’t he some
rights in his own country ? Also, why doesn’t some
solid body of parsons denounce the iniquity which
deprives him of them. Presumably because the
stealing of India pans out mainly for the benefit
of a small section of aristocratic officials, and
was mainly the work of aristocrats, and the
average parson s abasement before the aristocracy
exceeds that of Jeames Yellowplush, the other
flunkey. On the other hand, the keeping of the
Hindu out of this country is largely the work
of the Labor-party, and the alleged follower of
th© Carpenter’s Son (who loves to follow in a
carriage) as a rule goes on the short and
simple principle that everything that party
does wrong. The Labor element certainly
doesn’t go to church much, which helps to account
for the parsonical animosity. Why should it ?
It meets the sweater and the usurer and
the Pharisee in the way of business, proba-
bly six days a week. It doesn’t want to meet
their apologist - and hired whitewasher on the
seventh. The mass of the people “slight the
Bible ’ because it is associated in their minds with
the average parson—the representative of a class
which hangs round rich men’s doors, and has such
a weird knack of finding out that the Deity dis-
approves of whatever interferes with the rich
man’s unjust profits, and which howls for blood-
shed when the wealthy classes are for the shedding
of blood. If the average parson would only keep
away from the Bible that splendidly democratic
Book might have a chance.

More About Preferential Trade.
Premier Barton in Canada :
It was only possible to come to a general understanding
at the Colonial-Imperial Conference which had re ently
met in London, each Premier to p opose certain measures
dealing with preferential treatment, either lowering the
duties against British goods or leaving them untouched
but raising the duties against foreign goods.
Premier Barton may be quite sure that any
proposals for preferential treatment of British
goods submitted to the Australian Parliament will
be rejected, unless Britain gives us a preference
m return. The situation is something like this :
. Britain buys nearly £170,000,000 worth yearly more
than it sells, and it buys all that immense surplus from
foreign countries.
When the British colonies in general buy £1 worth of
goods from Britain, it buys £1 worth in return. When
its foreign enemies buy £] worth, it buys £1 12s. worth
in return. There are some foreign countries like Ger-
manv, which buy more from Britain than they sell to it,
but the above is the result.
The Commonwealth, during the last year covered by
Coghlan’s statistics, sold about £lOO,OOO worth less
to Britain than it bought from Britain.
The Commonwealth, on the other hand, sold £5,000,000
worth more to foreign countries than it bought from
foreign countries, and the £5,000,000 it got in coin to
make up the difference was the only thing that enabled it
to face its British creditors.
And when Britain asks us to declare war against our
profitable customers for the special benefit of our unprofit-
able customer, and holds that it is our duty to buy
everything from Britain while Britain buys almost
everything from foreigners, and professes that we owe a
special duty to the Empire, while it owes no duty
to the Empire, its conduct comes under the head-
ing of Unspeakable Cheek. The Commonwealth already
buys 66 per cent, of its imports inside the Empire ;
Britain only buys 21 per cent. The people of the Com-
monwealth spend about £6 10s. per head annually in
buying goods from other parts of the British Empire
(mostly from England), while Britain only spends £2 10s.
per head in the same way. It is time Britain did
something in the way of giving us a preference to level up
these irregularities.

Victorian Prisoners and Lunatics.
Consider the state of Victorian legislation at
present. A permanent Factories Act is an urgent
necessity; an Arbitration Act is loudly de-
manded ; a Usury Act, a Miners’ Relief Fund,
a State Bank, an all-round land-tax to square
the finances, are all wanted. Tons of other
legislation have been demanded till the people
are weary of asking, and there is in view
no hope of its appearance. Recent happenings
in Melbourne show the urgent necessity of laying
down anew the principles—on scientific lines —of
the Penal and Lunacy systems. To consider and
thoroughly thrash out these two questions alone
would takeat least a session. Yet there islittlehope
of this being done for years yet. And, meanwhile,
callous, shiftless neglect, and niggardly, procrasti-
nating ‘‘thrift” are making the State a by-word
among civilised peoples for its treatment of crim-
inals and lunatics. The Chief Secretary’s depart-
ment has been engaged in a sort of polyangular
duel with certain doctors and the Secretary, and
in discussing the question whether the Acting-
Inspector-General shall resign, or whether his
subordinate (an alleged habitual drug-user) shall
be dismissed, while all thought of the unfortunate
patients under their care seems to have vanished.
Yet the figures given in the statistical returns are
startling enough to have shaken up a Chief-
Secretary with the welfare of the insane at heart.
Here are a few items : —Of women sent to Vic.
asylums in 1882, 61 per cent, recovered their
reason ;in 1901 but 35 per cent. Victoria has 37
lunatics in each 10,000 inhabitants, Queensland
30, N.S.W. 29, S. Australia 26, England 29. And
the Victorian ratio is increasing ! Taking the
figures for the last 10 years, the proportion dis-
charged cured is :
South Australia .. .. 43-09 per cent
West Australia .. .. 42-02
N.S.W 3399 M
Queensland 32-88 „
Victoria 29-80 ~
The Cabbage-Garden saves barely 30 per cent.,
while South Australia, with certainly no greater
resources, manages to send back half as many
again to life and hope. S.A. profits by doing so,
as lunacy is not, as yet, a self-supporting industry,
and the marvel is that the neighboring Victorians
haven’t perceived this long ago. Doubtless the
Victorian figures are swelled by the inclusion
as lunatics of many unfortunates who ought
properly to be in receiving-houses for obser-
vation and temporary treatment, but this
increase is _ balanced by the omission of
many now in the gaols who ought properly
to be in lunatic asylums. As for these
gaols, the whole penal, even the judicial, system is
utterly rotten. The anomalies in sentences are so
marked that even the stolid dailies begin to
reprobate them. The amount of punishment
a culprit may receive seems to be mostly
a matter of luck—and it isn’t healthy for
a country to gamble with justice. And
when the criminal is gaoled the obsolete
Victorian system makes no attempt at mental
treatment, no endeavor at reform, no effort to
make him maintain himself and his keepers; in
short, no anything. He is just shoved out of
sight, and the thrifty Victorians haven’t sense
enough to see that this folly costs them more than
the two Houses of Parliament put together, and
the Governor thrown in. These are some of
the things that need comprehensive treat-
ment by the Vic. Parliament, but there is little
hope of that while the present cumbersome,
costly, bicameral system obtains. The House
that has at times shown some small capacity for
work grafts under the soul-depressing knowledge
that most of its results will be condemned as “not
wanted ” or “ premature ” by the privileged nomi-
nees of some 1900 large landowners.

Australian Unity.
There are not wanting signs, that the State
Parliaments are to atone for errors and negligence,
past and present, by being crushed at an earlier
date than was inpre-Federal days thought possible,
between the millstones of the Commonwealth and
the municipalities. On .the one hand the splendid
franchise of the two Federal Houses, and the
spaciousness of the vista which the absence of
mere localism promises in Commonwealth affairs
are winning all Australians to a faith in the
competence of the Parliament to enact equitable
and uniform laws on matters not yet included in
the 39 articles of the Federal Constitution. On the
other hand, the civic enthusiasm of municipal
England and Scotland, which has taught Britons
how to glean all the harvest of Socialism with-
out waving a red flag, reading Karl Marx,
or calling oneself a Socialist, is awakening
Australia to the desire of imitation. The
revolt against the chronic chaos in municipal
matteis is forcing the question of democratising
the municipalities and enlarging their powers and
extending their areas, into the field of practical
politics. Ihe attitude of State Parliaments
towards those who urge the reference of suitable
matters, such as factory problems, to the Com-
monwealth for uniform legislation, is so chilling
tha-t advocates of progress begin to see that the
ciause winch prevents the Federal Parliament
moving in such matters, except by permission of.
the State Legislatures, will be the first
obsolete one in our brand-new Constitution. Such
an attitude will only, of course, hasten the time
when all subjects of common iuterest will be found
embedded, without waiting for the formality of a
reference, in the legislative jurisdiction of the
Commonwealth. A new organisation, called the
Young Australian Society, aiming at the
further unity of Australia, held a meeting
during August, in Melbourne, for the purpose
of insisting on the matter of factory legis-
lation being referred to the Federal Parliament.
■ he meeting, from which State politicians were
conspicuously absent, indignantly pretested against
the cavalier treatment accorded by the State
Premiers to Barton’s letter inviting the States to
hand over labor questions to the Federal authority.
Moreover, this society revealed the existence of a
very strong current of feeling in favor of uniform
Australian laws as regards strikes, railways, land
tenure, wills, old _ age pensions, adulteration,
criminal law, municipal government, and other
subjects not included in, or only imperfectly dealt
with under, the 39 articles. Such a society is only
one outward sign of growing inward dissatisfac-
tion with State Parliaments, handicapped to the
point of impotency by anomalous, reactionary,
and unreformable “Upper” Houses, and also of
the flaming into a fire of the embers of unity
hidden in the Federal Constitution. The debt-
burden of the States is helping the cause of further
unity by forcing to the front the question of loan-
consolidation and what seems its necessary con-
sequence, the Australianising of the railways and
public works constructed out of loan money.
Again, the municipal reform agitation, pointing
as it does to the creation of large county councils
on a democratic franchise, will, when it has borne
fruit, leave so little of local moment to the State
Parliaments that even the satisfaction of the am-
bitions of local hustlers will hardly seem a sufficient
reason for their continuance.
The Vindication of Fropputty.
The sacred rights of Propputty were lately vin-
dicated at White Cliffs opal-fields. There are
2000 or more people there, and lately it
became evident that any day it might become
impossible for teams to get through with
stores for them; already the rate from Broken
Hill, 160 miles away, had gone up to £lB a ton.
The diggers held a meeting to consider the matter,
and Frank Hartley, a member of the Progress
Committee, and one of the best-known of Western
N.S.W. Labor men, was urged to speak. “If,”
he said, “ the worst comes to the worst, rather
than see our wives and children starve, we must
communise all the stores in the place.” It would
really be the sensible, civilised thing to do—it
simply meant that, if things should get desperate,
all should share and share alike: just the thing that
men in a boat cast away from a wreck would
do quite as a matter of course ; and being cast
away on a sea of sand is quite as bad as being cast
away on a sea of salt water. But Hartley, most
peaceful of men, reckoned without the. Law. He
didn’t reflect that it is the inalienable right of
Propputy to charge you Is. for a threepenny loaf if
you are hungry, 2s. 6d. if you are very hungry,
and £1 if you must get the loaf or starve. He
didn’t remember that, if there is but one loaf
between Solomon Jerusalem, the opal-buyer, and
Bill Brown, the opal-gouger, Solomon J. has the
sacred right to “ scoff ” the lot if he can outbid
Bill, and even though thereby William dies.
Hartley was, therefore, charged on a formidable
indictment with “inciting to crime,” for which the
punishment runs to 10 years on top of a £5OO fine ;
but. in view of his remarkably good record, the
P.M. reduced the charge so that he might deal
with it there and then, which he did by binding
Hartley over to “keep the peace.” Prop-
putty is happy again—but not nearly so happy
as if the accused had got ten years. And, as’
before, it proceeded to cnarge the digger up to
Is. Od. a lb. for cartage, although even £2O a ton
really means only about 2d. a lb.

Second-Class Legislation.
Not only have we in Australia a legal system
which provides, according to one’s purse, first-
class justice in the Supreme Courts, second-class
justice in the County, District and local courts,
and third-class justice in the J.P.’s court, but in
Parliament, also, legislation tends latterly to
divide itself into permanent legislation for some
classes and temporary, second-class legislation for
others. In Victoria, for instance, the reactionary
Legislative Council, when, almost at the point of
riot’s pikes, it yields to persistent demands for
remedial legislation for the poorer classes, has got
quite into a habit of making all such Acts of
Parliament temporary ones thus exposing
them to all the dangers incidental to changes
of Government and official forgetfulness,
and making it necessary to fight the battle all
over again every few years. The Employers’
Liability Act, relieving some workmen from the
cruel effects of a piece of Judge-made law, was
originally passed as a temporary Act in 1886, and
was only made permanent, after proving itself to
be a conspicuously harmless measure, last year.
The Factories Act (Wages Board clauses) was
built on the temporary plan, and it has disap-
peared. A third Act, framed to relieve the very
poor from the undue pressure of grasping credi-
tors, especially of the money-lender variety—
namely, the Wages Attachment Act—was also
temporary, and is also to expire. Yet this Act is
a permanent one in England, and if the Victorian
Legislative Council’s reverence for that country
was a little more real and a little less gassy, the
same Act might be a permanent institution in
Victoria also.
♦
The knottiest question of the year came before
the newest of N.S.W. Supreme Court Judges last
week. Lawyers revel in the difficulties raised ;
laymen, as is their stolid wont, pass the arguments
and go to the pub. opposite for a drink. The case
was Hannah v. Drake —a cabman versus the
Commonwealth Postmaster-General, for damages
sustained by the falling of an electric wire.
Against whom was cabby to go? Drake said
“me,” as Postmaster-General of the Common-
wealth, but not “me” as Drake personally.
Attorney-General Wise proved conclusively
that his Honor had jurisdiction. The way
he labored from the left to the right,
constructing and demolishing, edified old
lawyers. _ Justice Pring said it was the most
extraordinary state of affairs he ever knew.
But it ended up in a non-suit. Drake, as Post-
master-General, is amenable only to the High
Court, and as the High Court doesn’t exist yet,
Drake, as far as State jurisdiction goes, doesn’t
legally exist, and he can knock out a cabman, or
a cabman’s horse, or any other man or horse, with
impunity. That was the upshot of Hannah v.
Drake ; but now that the fun of the lawyers is
over, the fair-minded members of the Common-
wealth Parliament should take a turn at the sub-
ject. Political exigencies may delay the forma-
tion of a High Court, but no exigencies condone a
defeat of justice. If the cabman has an equitable
claim the Federal Government should promptly
honor it —law or no law.

The shearing strike in N.S.W. has ended with
the capitulation of the A.W.U., which was ob-
viously fighting a hopeless battle from the outset.
There still remain as legacies of the trouble
various law actions in the Arbitration and Equity
Courts, and the A.W.U. is still under the neces-
sity of acting on the defensive. It must also, in
the interests of the bush-workers, prosecute with
vigor its legal campaign against the bogus
Machine Shearers’ Union, and so bring the con-
ditions in the pastoral industry to a stage at which
a fair and free decision as to wage-rates may be
made by the Arbitration Court. Officials of the
A.W.U. are rather inclined to blame the Indus-
trial Arbitration Act for some of their troubles ;
they would with more justice blame themselves.
The plain fact of the matter was that they were
out-manoeuvred. Superior tactics secured for the
Pastoralists’ Union what it desired—viz., the
blocking of any decision on the wages question.
- •*
The Westralian Arbitration Court has decided
that it cannot award “preference to unionists,” as
the Maoriland and N.S.W. courts can and do.
Provision for it was in the Bill, but the Council
knocked it out. It was put to the court that that
was not to be taken as proving that Parliament
wouldn’t have it—-it might simply mean that Par-
liament thought it was surplusage, wherefore the
court was not barred from making the award.
But the court knew the Leg. Council better than
that and decided that it couldn’t. On the other
hand a local court has given a verdict with heavy
costs against a union member who had let his con-
tributions get into arrears and wouldn’t pay up.

The most grievous plaint at the Fruit-growers’
Conference, held in Melbourne during show-week,
was against the iniquitous Factories Act, which,
the country person said, had increased the price of
his fruit-cases by Id.—a whole penny—apiece. Of
course, the said Act isn’t entirely responsible for
the increase—even if there is one, which is by no
means certain—but even supposing it was, what
then ? A fruit-grower has to be in a pretty large
way before he needs 200 or 300 cases, and these
last for years; so the fruit grower objects to a
Minimum Wage law because, he says, it
costs him about half-a-crown a year. He
hasn’t the sense to see that higher wages
for his principal customers (the Melbourne
workers) mean better prices for his own produce,
and that a larger industrial community means still
better prices and a larger demand. If the time he
wastes declaiming against anti-sweating legis-
lation were devoted to organising co-operative
distributing-agencies and jam-factories, he would
save a lot more than 2s. 6d. Low wages in the
Melbourne factories simply mean starvation prices
for the countryman’s fruit—and the sooner he
learns that truth the better for himself,
4 .
N.S.W. Premier See has written “ a strong
letter” objecting to Victoria using any of the
Murray waters for irrigation purposes. N.S.W.
isn’t using the waters itself, and as far down the
river as the site of the proposed Victorian irriga-
tion works the Murray is almost wholly supplied
by Victorian streams ; but though the Victorian
mallee is perishing from drought and the unused
Murray is running to waste past its front door,
See says it must just go to waste. For 50 years
N.S.W. has claimed practically the sole control of
Australia’s one big river, and has let its waters be
lost while the land perished from thirst, and John
See holds that the dignity of his State requires
that this policy shall be continued till further
notice.

Our Prophets.
That wise and pious legislator, Mr. Affleck, has
introduced a Bill into the N.S.W. Legislative
Assembly to amend the present laws in reference
to the observance of Sunday. The Bill provides
for the _ imposition of fines on anyone found en-
gaging in any of the following games on the Sab-
bath :—Baseball, bowls, coursing, cricket, cycle-
racing, football, foot-racing, golf, horse-racing,
hurling, lacrosse, or tennis. Pitch-and-toss and
ping-pong seem to have been overlooked :
When Israel fell to wickedness,
A prophet always rose
To promise drayloads of distress,
And divers kinds of woes.
Alas, that in our sunny land
This thing should also be,
In spite of all the beauty bland
Of earth, and sky, and sea.
We lived right pleasantly and well,
And did not Faith deny,
Yet gave but little thought to Hell,
And Worms that Never Die.
We thought the life God gave us here
Might be a thing of joy—
That thought was a delusion drear,
Intended to destroy.
The world might still as pleasant seem
But that, with tongue of flame,
To rouse us from our foolish dream
The prophet Affleck came.
He showed us, in the nick of time,
What made us wicked be
Was not our surplusage of crime,
But lack of Piety.
Not swindling, in a business way,
Will bring us future woe,
But breaking of the Sabbath Day
Will turn our cake to dough.
The Sabbath is a Day of Gloom
(So Affleck seems to mean)
For meditations on the Tomb,
And searchings of the spleen.
You cannot laugh in Affleck’s teeth,
Throw dust in Nobbs’s eyes—
They know Apollyon, underneath
The cunningest disguise.
You dance with some young damsel sweet,
Bright-eyed, with honeyed breath—
They see her feet are cloven feet,
That galop down to Death.
They know that when the dance is o’er
Your silly waltzing souls
Will hop and skip for evermore
On gridirons o’er the coals.
Foot-racing shall be turned to pain
When sounds the last dread Drum ;
And you shall sprint—and sprint in vain—
To flee the wrath to come.
The sons of Belial may sneer
To show their worldly wit
With prophets Nobbs and Affleck here
We yet shall clear the Pit.
