♦
“The Belle of New York ” is so essen-
tially an American effort that it would be difficult
to imagine a wholly satisfactory representation
by any but an American co. The humor is
American, the curious orchestration is fitted to
American styles of comic singing, the whole spirit
is American, and it needs to be played through
the company’s joint and several nose or its flavor
is apt to be lost. When it is said of Williamson’s
Comedy Co., which is not American, that it gave,
on the whole, a very good and amusing represen-
tation of the extravaganza at Sydney Palace last
Saturday, that is very high praise. There is no need
to institute comparisons with the previous stage-
ingin Sydney when “The Belle” was played by
people of her own tribe and tongue. Nevertheless,
there are many points which suggest criticism.
Mr. Graham, who, as lehabod Bronson, bears
the chief burden and heat of the night, is not old
enough in his make-up, is not dry enough in his
speech, is not clear enough in his sing. Thereby
some very good points are lost. The present
lehabod, for example, didn’t make it at all clear
to the audience that “if he fell out of a fourth-
storey window, he was fast enough to reach the
ground as soon as anyone else ” ; and that’s an
essential point of this curiously attractive absur-
dity—this jumble of Wagnerian music, Gregorian
chant, Chinese opera, Bowery uproar and
American mental cocktail. The song, “Of course
you can never be like us, but be as like us as you
are able to be ” (which has almost a diplomatic im-
portance since it was inflicted by a cheeky Aus-
tralian contingent band on a visiting English
general or something in China) was rather spoiled
on Saturday by “gagging.” “Perhaps,” how-
ever, was well sung, and, in the second act gener-
ally, the new lehabod Bronson was better than in
the first.
• • • •
Miss Rose Musgrove was unexpectedly
successful as the Belle. She has not before been
suspected of so much voice or of so much cuteness
in using it to the best advantage. Miss Digges was
fairly good as Ei-Fi; her pretty, pert ways atone
for some vocal weakness. Cora, the Queen of
Comic Opera (“kammic appera ” the last co.
called it), found a sympathetic interpreter in Miss
Blanche Wallace, whose robust acting was, per-
haps, the best performance of the evening. Arthur
Crane lacked the bravura air necessary for Harry
Bronson ; he was altogether too meek and curate-
like. In an unwisely-interpolated song, his voice
absolutely failed on Saturday to meet the demands
made upon it. Fred Leslie was very good as the
strangely unaccountable Blinky Bill, and other
notably effective performers in the long cast were
Dudley, as the Polite Lunatic ; F. Morton, as a
Chinaman ; and Miss Madge Bennett, as a Pell-
street girl. R. Needham sang the patriotic song,
which seems to be dragged in by its broken feet,
with some brightness and vigor ; and the chorus
and orchestra deserve congratulation.
** * *
The fat emperor still runs his empire with
a certain measure of dignity and a good deal of
pomp at Sydney Royal, and his large round face
beams on 300,000,000 subjects, all liable to be
boiled alive at the shortest notice. Once or twice
lately The Bulletin has imagined that it detected
the flavor of a new joke in the “Chinese Honey-
moon,” but the tail of the fleeting impression dis-
appeared round the corner of this paper’s mind
before it could be properly identified. Herr
Slapoffski, whirling his mane and his stick of
office in the seat of the scornful—the seat where
the conductor sits and tries to conceal his scorn of
the company and the play—continues to drive the
melody of the alleged comedy for all it is
worth, but the lean and sombre musical
animal gets up no special pace for all his
efforts. The “ Honeymoon *’ hasn’t been on for
many weeks, but the last nights are already
announced, and the rather dejected joke is already
packing up its trunk. Like the hairs of our head,
as mentioned in Scripture, its days are numbered.
How few they are the advertisement doesn’t say
vet, but they are probably not numerous. If the
play could be taken away and the scenery left
there might be a show for a longer season, for the
scenery deserves to be found in better company.
When the “ Honeymoon ” is gone, a dramatic
censor should be appointed with power to block
all future productions with the mother-in-law joke
in them. It never was much of a joke, and now,
in its decayed old age, it suggests some disorderly
female of long standing making her 200th appear-
ance before the magistrate.
*# * *
At Sydney criterion “The Lady Slavey ”
has entered on her seventh and last week. On
Saturday the co., reinforced by Charles Kenning-
bam, will appear in a new production, called
“ Mrs. Goldstein.” The programme sets forth
that Rachael Mendoza pretends to be Mrs. Gold-
stein, and, as Johnny Sheridan will pretend to be
both of them and a woman at the same time, the
case looks complicated as well as Hebrew.
** * *
“Riding to Win,” or the Drama of the
Dog, is now in its second week at Sydney Lyceum,
and the sagacious animal who only rescues moral
characters from drowning, and who chases the
well-dressed villain off a Melbourne park seat,
holds the play up as much as formerly. In these
days when a dress-circle lady whispers, “Isn’t he
lovely ? ” she doesn’t mean Baker, as she used to
do. She means the dog. The canine’s animosity
towards sinful but well-dressed persons is more
untrue to nature than anything else in the drama.
The real dog is generally a snobbish animal, who
nips the ragged but honorable dustman, and
judges the Base Betrayer mostly by the kind of
belltopper he wears.
** * *
Cinquevalli closes his Sydney season at
the Tivoli to-morrow (Friday), and as The
Bulletin went to press the appearance of
Wallace Brownlow at this variety theatre was
announced. The Sidney Drews have replaced
“ When Two Hearts are Won ” with “Diana on
the Chase,” a rather poor copy of “Niobe,” which
does not give such hilarious opportunities as the
first piece. It is chiefly remarkable for the fine
make-up and posing of Mrs. Drew as the Statue
of Diana. The two principles are well assisted in
the production by Miss Noni Rickards. The
Harvey Boys have changed their act for one with
more fight and less talk; and the change is much
favored by the gallery.
** # #
“Sherlock Holmes,’’from Adelaide and the
West, arrived at Her Majesty’s (Melb.) on Satur-
day, with Cuyler Hastings in the name-part and a
strong company at the back of the new man.
The drama, by Conan Doyle and Gillette, is
unique in its realistic impossibilities. An exciting
nightmare without a moral, a panorama of crime
and counter-plot, very absurd, very theatrical,
decidedly clever. The story is a string of inci-
dents pertaining to certain mysterious papers and
a wildly unaccountable girl—the daughter of a
crazy mother who pops in casually, takes one brief
stare at the drama, and pops out again. Seems
that the bundle of papers came into the possession
of a sister, now deceased, through a love affair
with some exalted personage unknown, and the
unaccountable girl means to spring them upon
the personage in a revengeful spirit. Tracey*
Sherlock Hawkshaw Holmes, the detective,
is commissioned to recover those papers, but the
vengeful damsel has allowed herself (and her mad
mother) to become captives in the hands of two
gilt-edged criminals namsd Larrabee —Jim and
Mrs. The Larrabees also want the hidden docu-
ments for blackmail purposes, and can’t get them.
They burgle the young woman’s safe in their own
drawing-room, and find it empty. They are
starting to torture the sweetly ridiculous spinster
when Sherlock arrives. Sherlock has the eye of
a hypnotist and the temperament of a butter*
freezer. He knows nearly everything that was,
is, or ever will be, as regards men, women and
Eapers. His cold cheek appals the Larrabees. He
olds all the clues in the hollow of his cocained
brain. Consequently the baffled burglars appeal
to one Professor Moriarty in their dilemma, and
that grand old gentleman decides that Sherlock
must either drop the clues or perish. Sherlock, it
appears, has lately been getting on the Professor’s
nerves.
• * * *
Moriarty is a greater man than Sherlock,
for he has built up an enormous criminal business
without the assistance of drugs. His private office
is a stone vault in the bowels of the earth, where
he sits at a desk and talks through a tube to his
myrmidons. It is terrible to hear the Prof,
breathing instructions for murder down the tube,
like a waiter ordering chops and spinach for three.
The imperial ancient arranges for the “ removal ”
of an ex-forger who acts as spy to Sherlock. Also
Sherlock’s boy-in-buttons is marked for burial if
necessary. Somehow the Professor’s faithful
assassins contrive to bungle the job. The spy
escapes with a pink, puffy protuberance on the
head, and the rorty boy-in-buttons flees from
his livery whilst the human bloodhounds are
hanging on to it. After this the Prof, perceives
that death is the only remedy for Sherlock, so he
signs the warrant for the detective’s execution,
and leaves word that he will meet him in H—l
later. But Sherlock, otherwise Tracey the Hawk-
shaw, walks into the Professor’s trap and breaks
up the scheme. Subsequently he puts all the
criminals under arrest, and secures the papers, as
the result of capturing the affections of the extra-
ordinary girl. The last seen of Sherlock is his
limelit head associated with the head of his
beloved. The rest of them is utter darkness.
They look like the illuminated remains of a
ghost-story, and their impending marriage pro-
mises to be a brilliant failure. Sherlock is a
drug-maniac, and his future mother-in-law is
madder than the bride-elect.
* * * *
The drama, as produced at Her Majesty’s,
deserves to get on. Its sudden plunges into dark-
ness at the end of every act, and the generally
slick management of its horrors and alarms dis-
tinguish it from ordinary spectacular melodrama.
Mr. Cuyler Hastings, the new arrival, scores a
full success as Sherlock. He plays the part with
power, or he gets inside the part and lets it play
itself, which is the same _ thing. Miss May
Chevalier, as Sherlock’s girl, has little to do
except talk as though she meant it, and she does
that little quite thrillingly. Miss Mabel Lane is
a strong picturesque adventuress. Hamilton
Stewart supplies an imposing Jim; the wickedness
of Athohvood, as Prof. Moriarty, has much to
recommend it; the boy in buttons is good; and
Edmund Gwenn, as safe _ burglar, shines
noticeably among a number of minor lights.
** * #
At the Princess’s, the pretty, sentimental
story of the philosopher who trained up a ward in
the way he didn’t want her to grow, is a story that
appeals to all women, other than the richly-
upholstered female from Toorak. It is rather too
mild and gentle for the d.c. If Mrs. Goodlake s
designs on the captain were not doomed to
failure, Toorak would probably take more interest
in “ Mice and Men.” From the standpoint of
advanced society, Mrs. Goodlake looks rather a per-
son to be pitied. Her husband is,a doting idiot of
uncertain age, whose bucolic chuckle and full-moon
countenance would drive any smart young Joanna
to indiscretion, and it’s hard lines for his lawful
wife to be robbed of her indiscretion by a damsel
who borrowed her second best yellow satin to
wear at a masquerade ball. Miss Nellie Stewart
displays the yellow satin to great advantage, but
looks even nicer in her work-a-day dress, the one
in which she takes her dancing lesson from
Kit. The dancing lesson is a sweet morsel
of comedy, and another item that only a
Nellie Stewart can make the most of is
the scene where she turns her back to /the
philosopher (and the audience) and puts her
stockings on, singing the while. The Thornton
Co. returns to the Princess’s next week, and Little
Britain—that’s Nellie—will complete lower
portion of her toilet for the last time on Friday,
the 26th. By the way, the management might as
well explain the meaning of “Little Britain ” in a
footnote to the programme. A mostly Australian
audience shouldn’t be expected to understand the
wherefore of the odd London names given to those
foundling girls. For that matter there are a good
many people from Great Britain, and a lot more
from Ireland, who never heard of Little Britain.
** * *
“ A Woman of Pleasure ” supplies the
weekly want at Melb. Royal, and the balloon that
bears her from Pretoria will descend upon the
West Coast of Africa till further notice. Also,
the tramp who parts Bert Bailey from his trousers
will repeat the same insulting behaviour with
enormous success. This is understood to be the
first production in Australia of the Anderson
drama aforesaid, and inasmuch as it permits
Inman to kill Willard like a dawg, and results in
the triumph of moderate immorality, it deserves
its luck. On the many occasions when Inman
was a truly noble character, he never got the
satisfaction of wiping his enemy out with his own
revolver, neither has Miss Duggan been previously
allowed to strike the happy medium between
distressful virtue and crimson vice. “ A Woman
of Pleasure ” doesn’t run to extremes. The narra-
tive proceeds on eccentric lines of reasonable sin,
under the patronage of a rector named Luke.
** * *
At the Bijou, the Hawtrey Co. is pulling
“ The Lady of Ostend ” through her third week.
Burnand’s farcical comedy would bear being pulled
by a stronger co. The weakness of the three
young ladies whose voices need oiling, and whose
art is primitive, makes it a very lop-sided pull,
but the result of everybody’s combined effort is
satisfactory all the same. Moreover the little
curtain-raiser, “ Counsel’s Opinion,” is applauded
for the sake of the very small boy in the fore-
ground. An occasional touch of nature in the
performance of a well-trained infant seems to pro-
duce an easy tear in the public eye, and the public
delights to be impressed that way. It makes it
feel good.
« * ifc *
At Melbourne Opera House the cry is
Sandow. Melbourne talked of Sandow before he
came to hand, and is now taking him very seri-
ously, both as a man able to pick up another man
with his left hand and drop him down on a horse’s
back like a bag of lollies, and as a professor who
urges his fellow-creatures to lay up such muscle
on earth as is healthful and necessary as well for
the body as the soul. Professor Miller, the very
large and worthy citizen who used to come down
whop upon Mons. Victor in former days, was
desirous of luring Sandow into a series of athletic
exercise competitions, but the Physical Culturist
was not taking any fencing, boxing, etc. All
things considered, a wrestling match between
Sandow and Miller would probably be a deplorable
thing, on account of one of them getting broken.
** * *
Madame Melba, a vocalist whose name
carries a larger Australian advt. than any other
name The Bulletin can think of, will make her
re-appearance at Melb. Town Hall next Wednes-
day evening for the first time in many years.
During her period of absence the market price of
Melba’s famous voice has risen to a high pitch,
and all the seats for Madame’s forthcoming five
concerts are practically disposed of already at not
less than 10s. 6d. for the extreme back-row. Per-
sons who are “dying to hear Melba” had better
save their lives immediately by sending a guinea
to the box-office.
** * *
At Adelaide Royal the fine co. that played
Gillette’s “Sherlock Holmes” (in which*every-
body was just going to get shot, and didn’t) and
his “ Secret Service ” (in which people got shot a
great deal) has faded away and made room for
“Robin Hood,” who has to rely on a property
bow and arrow when he wants to do any shooting.
The hosts of up-country people in town for Show
Week have been much pleased with the mediaeval
revels of Lauri and Hugh Ward, and Florence
Young and the early English outlaw with the
American accent. At the Tivoli, Charles Hollo-
way has been doing good business with a very
large guillotine that could hardly be got into the
house, but which worked with great deadliuess
when it once arrived.

State revenue returns, so far as the same are
available (S.A. publishes once a quarter, and Maoriland
seems to issue its figures at no definite date) :

These figures are, especially as regards Queensland, baiter
than was expected. In Queensland there is an increase
in land revenue, railways, taxation, and miscellaneous
receipts—in other words it is an all-round increase. In
N.S.W., on the other hand, there is a drop in the revenue
from stamps, income-tax, licenses, land, railways and
tramways, &c., —in fact, if it wasn’t for the increased
revenue received under the Federal tariff there would be
a slump of nearly £50,000, and N.S.W. would be going to
the bad at the rate of nearly £600,000 a year. Appa-
rently Federation is going to be the financial salvation of
N.S.W. in a very bad time. The increase of £102,512 in
customs revenue last month by reason of the Kingston
tariff would have been badly missed.

Queensland and Victoria both had a heavy decline for the
first month of the new financial year (July), and they
have both more than made it good. For the moment, at
least, Tasmania is the only State which keeps persistently
on the down grade.
Only three States issue monthly expenditure
accounts. Their figures are as follows :
Revenue Expenditure
July-August, July-August,
1902. 1902. Surplus.
Queensland £546,133 £347,428 £198,705
Westralia 623,810 632,433 91,377
Deficit.
N.S. Wales 1,642,258 1,661,774 19,516
Queensland pays its interest bill in two half-yearly in-
stalments (June and December), whereas N.S.W. pays at
various dates, and Westralia charges each month with its
share of the year’s interest. Therefore Queensland’s
surplus doesn’t mean so much as another State’s might.
Queensland requires to accumulate about £750,000 by the
end of June, and a corresponding amount by the end of
December, to meet its liability. If it can keep up the
progress made during August it will just about do this,
and there need be no visible deficit —that is to say, no
deficit that can’t be disguised and faked out of sight in
the usual way by charging unproductive public works to
loans.
The surpluses and deficits compare with those
of the corresponding date last year as follows :
August, 1901. August, 1902. Improve-

Surplus. Surplus.
Queensland .. 110,317 198,705 88,388

It is generally suspected, however, that the improvement
in N.S.W. was largely owing to the carrying forward of
many liabilities this August. The dr.on of nearly £200,000
in the expenditure that month looks too sudden. As
regards Westralia, one-fifth of that State’s border Customs
duties will be taken off in October, which will probably
begin to put a new complexion on its affairs.
* * * »
N.S.W. has spent £714,717 of loan money in the
first two months of the financial year, as compared with
£508,776 during July and August, 1901, an increase of
£205,941. This is at the rate of over £4,200,000 a year.
The increase is more than accounted for by payments on
account of the Sydney land resumptions.
• • * •
Queensland spent £139,467 of loan money during
July-August, 1902, being £37,521 less than during July-
August, 1901. Queensland is spending at the rate of
£836,000 a year—roughly, £1 13s. per inhabitant per
annum, as against N.S.W.’s £3 per inhabitant per annum.

Tasmania’s solid old Commercial Bank is doing well again,
especially since Federation began to give business a sub-
stantial lift on the Island. The reserves are once more
growing rapidly, and the earnings represent the very
substantial figure of £7 2s. lid. per cent, per annum on
the shareholders’ funds as at the commencement of the
half-year (capital, £141,493, and reserves, £107,815). The
business has remained about the same during the half-
year. The most noticeable feature is the reserve of cash
and liquid securities which stands at £665,343, represent-
ing over 42 percent, on all liabilities to the public—which
is conspicuously above the average. The Commercial
Bank of Tasmania holds £180,922 (considerably more than
its entire,capital) in State securities. If other institutions
did their duty equally well in the way of propping up the
State credit, this country wouldn’t owe nearly so much to
the foreign Cohen as it does. If we must have a Cohen,
let us be our own Cohen 1
» * * *
Melbourne City Council discovered recently
that it had paid £7281 as fire insurance premiums during
the last 14 years, and received back £lO5B as fire losses.
This means that the Council is £6223 to the bad, together
with the interest, which, at 3£ per cent., would make the
loss about £BOOO. That is to say, if the Council had
established its own insurance fund 14 years ago, that fund
might now be £BOOO in credit, and constantly increasing.
A proposal to transfer the business to a municipal fund
was regarded coldly by a council which, possibly, holds
shares in a number of fire offices, and gets part of its divi-
dends out of the above loss.
* * * *
Queensland’s railways for the year just ended
yielded a net revenue of £389,428 —an increase of £130,473
on last year. The gross revenue (£1,382,179) was the
largest, save that of 1900, which Queensland ever collected.
As the public income is looking up all round, Plunket,
M.L.A., who proposes that Queensland should secede
from the Commonwealth to avert “ national insolvency,”
is rather mistimed. The £520,000 deficit with which
Queensland closed its career as a separate State rather
suggests that it only just federated in time to avert
national insolvency.

lany doubtful claims have been advanced on
behalf of duplex pumps. Some makers state that their
duplex pumps are “ double the capacity of single pumps
of same diameter and stroke/’ An A. S. Cameron single-
acting pump can be run safely and comfortably at a speed
that would knock a duplex pump to pieces. But even at
a low speed, duplex pumps seldom attain even their
theoretical capacities, and the reason for this can be
readily explained. Apply to the Agents, Messrs. W. and
J. Lempriere, 506 Little Collins Street, Melbourne, for
further particulars. The A. S. Cameron pumps are noted
for their simplicity, durability, and absence of outside
valve gear.*

* * * *
The Government of New South; Wales have
entered into a contract for the manufacture, supply, and
delivery of 13 miles of Mephan Ferguson’s patent Loek-
ingbar or Rivetless steel pipes. These are to be 32 inches
diameter, in 25 feet lengths, and will be used for convey-
ing water from Nepean River to Prospect Reservoir. This
type of pipe has been used by the Government of South
Australia for water-supply purposes for some time past,
and has given great satisfaction ; also by the Govern-
ment of Western Australia for the Coolgardie water
scheme, for which about 320 miles of Lockingbar pipes
have been delivered.*

A Sure Fortune.—The best investment in
Australia is buying land at Esperance, W.A., the natural
port of the Kalgoorlie goldfields. With a railway,
Esperance will become one of our busiest ports, as the
trade of the goldfields will pass through it. A permanent
survey is now being made ; the railway is a certainty.
Get in early and make a fortune by purchasing a few
blocks before the great rush sets in. Price, £2O a block.
Terms, £1 a month. Write for plans and particulars to
The Esperance Land Company, Nicholson Chambers,
Swanston-st., Melbourne.*

* •*., * * *
The great success of the Wilfley patent Con-
centrator since its merits became known to the mining
community of Australia, has led to imitations and
piracies under various names being placed upon the
market. The Wilfley Ore Concentrator Syndicate of
London, which .owns the Australian patents has decided to
prosecute all users and vendors of concentrating machines
infringing its patents, and action has been commenced
in TiCtoria. Similar action to protect its rights had to
be taken in America, where the Supreme Court has upheld
Wilfley patents, granting an injunction and damages
against the users and vendors of the infringing machine.
The sole manufacturers in Australia of Wilfley patent
concentrator are the Austral Otis Engineering Conlpany
of South Melbourne, which supplies customers direct
or. through any of its accredited agents, in the various
mining-centres.*

Ovens Valley (Vic.) Dredging Co.’s returns for
half-year show some interesting results. 109,512 cubic
yards of wash treated at a total cost of £1205 (including
over £2OO for repairs, and other items), or 2.82 d. per yard ;
gold won, 5090 z., or 2.23 grains per yard ; profit for the
term, £728. This is the nearest approach to prospectus
calculation yet recorded. Manager reports 15 years’ work
in sight.

The Maoriland Government Accident Insur-
ance Co. (Wellington, M.L.) issues its first balance-sheet.
It is nominally an annual balance-sheet, but it really
covers a period of only about seven months, from the in-
ception of the office in June, 1901, to December, 1901.
Results are as follows
Income. Claims ’ Ex P ense9 ’ oSSo. ReserveB ’
1901.. £11,597 £2428 £5373 £7BOl £4090
The expenses are always heavy in the first year of a new
office, but in this case all the preliminary charges have
been paid without the exes, running to quite 45J per
cent. The reserves consist of reserve for unexpired risks
£3780, and surplus profit £316, and the fact that,
after making full allowance for unexpired risks,
and paying all preliminary charges out of only
seven months’ income, it is possible to show a
profit of £316 speaks well for the prospects. A loan of
£lOOO from the Government is the only thing in the way
of capital, so the profits represent over 31 per cent. If
this success is maintained, it, together with the remark-
able prosperity of the Maoriland Government’s ordinary
life department, should encourage Maoriland to make a
renewed attempt at the long-postponed State fire insur-
ance idea.
Maoriland has now two accident insurance
offices. Australia hasn’t one office solely devoted to this
business, though a certain amount of accident business
is done by one or two fire offices. The bulk of the Aus-
tralian business, however, goes to one Maoriland office
and an English one.

approximation—the exact figure is never known till next
year’s balance-sheet is issued. The discrepancy, at the
most, won’t exceed £5O. The Farmers’ Co-operative is
certainly a prosperous institution, and though it does
business in a very limited circle, it is accumulating
reserves at a respectable rate. The paid-up capital is
£49,568—an enormous amount considering that the gross
premium income is only £11,548. It is very rarely that a
fire and marine office starts on such a solid basis.

The Theatre Royal Proprietary Co. Limited
(Melbourne) made 11210 profit for the half-year ended
30th June, 1902, which is at the rate of only £5 os. lOd.
per cent, per annum on the paid-up capital of £48,000.
And even this was only done by charging 1372 spent on
decorations (which are necessary repairs) to capital, and
carrying it forward as an asset, instead of paying it out
of earnings, as should have been done. The shareholders
get a dividend at the rate of 21 per cent, per annum,
which is a good return on the present market-value of
the shares (6s. or 6s. 6d.), but mighty little on the £1
paid up. The £6OO would have been better devoted
towards abolishing the earthy, musty smell noticeable in
the reserved stalls section of the ground floor, but George
Coppin and family, who represent the bulk of the pro-
prietary, prefer to take the threepenny div. regularly.

The Provident and Industrial Insurance Co., however,
professes to have an assurance fund of £6262 made up as
follows :
. Assurance Fund as above £723
Added to paper value of property 3 240
fExpenses not charged but carried forward as an
Asset.. .. .. .. .. .. .. 2,299
Total (alleged Assurance Fund) .. .. £6,262
’There is room for a good many remarks about the
concern which makes up a (largely imaginary) assur-
ance fund in this fashion. It is common enough for a
new office in the lamentable industrial business to carry
forward part of the expenses of the first few years as an
asset, but in a 13 years old institution the proceeding is
disgraceful. As to the device of putting up the value of
the co.’s office property, on paper, from £1268 to £4508,
when the co. was in a tight place, any co. can do that
sort of thing if it feels so inclined, and if the law allows
it. But when a large proportion of the alleged funds,
Which serve as a guarantee that the policy-holders’ claims
Will be paid, consists of preliminary expenses and alleged
sudden increase in the value of the co.’s office, it is a very
foolish kind of policy-holder who is satisfied with the
security.
The expense rate of the Provident and Indus-
trial Insurance Co. has lately been as follows :
Expenses in Proportion to Total Income.
1898.. £9O 7 3 per cent. 1901.. £57 15 8 per cent
1899.. 70 8 3 „ 1902.. 59 110
1900.. 63 4 8 ~
The enormously heavy expense rate of 1898 and 1899
came down a little, hut is now going-up again, and though
the income is £1499 more than it was last year, the
amount saved and added to the funds is a good deal’less
What the present value of the liabilities (i.e., the amount
that the co. should have in genuine assets, to be solvent
in an actuarial sense) may be, this writer has no idea hut
for a co. which is pledged by its policies to pay somethin"-
like £140,000, an accumulation of £723 in 13 years seems
a Very miserable and desolate outlook. “ Office furniture
and stationery, £592,” is an asset which nearly accounts
for the whole fund. It is high time Maoriland' had a law
providing for a State audit and valuation of assets and a
legal actuarial standard of solvency, with, of course a
compulsory shutting up of institutions which don’t keep
up to the standard. The Provident and Industrial is an
institution which calls aloud for some such statute.
The Victorian Railway Dept., after a few
months trial, has decided to discontinue the concession of
one week’s grace for return tickets on Melbourne subur-
ban lines. The old system of issuing tickets for one day
only seems eminently calculated to suit the rival Tram-
way Co. The first class fare from several suburbs where
the trams compete against the railway is less than 6d. —
the price of two tram journeys. Therefore, the new
rule which made the ‘return’ half of a railway ticket
available for a week from date of issue tempted many
people to travel by train who would otherwise have taken
the tram. Even if they were not likely to want to use
the return ticket on the same day they would probably
be able to turn it to good account within a week, and get
j °" rne J * or EA or which would have cost them 3d.
or 2jd. by the other route. The only concession that the
tramway Co. makes is a parcel of 12 tickets for 2s. 9d.,
available for any time—a very small concession as com-
pared with, say, two 3d. railway journeys for 4 id. when
the return ticket was good for seven days, but a quite
considerable inducement as compared with the ticket
that expires in orie day, and becomes a dead loss if not
used before midnight. The Tramway Co., at all events,
is pleased to hear that the railways are going back to the
old groove. A general desire to please any and every
private monopoly characterises the present Government.

This may at least be described as reasonable prosperity.
“ M‘Gr.”: Smith’s Creek promises to turn out
even .more sound than most of its original barrackers
conscientiously, or otherwise, anticipated, • and, for the
sake of a district half-throttled, by some ugly proceed-
ings, this is good news. Writer anticipates when sluicing
operations begin that several chutes, such as the one
manager Potter lately reported finding off main body,
will be discovered adjacent to lode. But, speakinw as
one who has prospected the vicinity, the great balance of
shows shot on to the gullible on the strength of an
honest parent, are a disgrace to any mining district
Newspaper talk of lode being discovered in other leases
is often brought about by jovial, winey treatment of in-
terested visitors. There are some smart mining
men on Garnet. If Surprise Gully even possessed
sufficient tin to reward a small and economically
run co., it has not the necessary fall for
sluicing operations; its composition is mostly of a
cement-like nature, so much so as to debar even puddlino-
—and yet some talk about dredging. (Given birth a few
years earner, before bottom of market fell in, and it
would have been dredged—on paper, anyhow.) Drift
formation in old creek bed—there is little possessing pay-
able dirt—has long since been washed up by original tin-
getters. Best of this would not go what boomsters’
journals now bible-oath to the public the gully—mostly a
blind gully—will average. Great Mt. Garnet—catchy,
high-sounding title, only, as I said before, they have some
good “mining ” men on Garnet—is going strong on paper.
One of prospectors and self were talked into having a go
at this promising monster on strength of “ rich speci-
mens ” and a 15ft. tin lode running parallel. After a look
at old workings we threw our hand in. At that time (last
Feb.) this 15ft. of good tin was porphyry formation, with
odd—awfully odd—little patches of tin, and 10 or 12 inches
of copper which was dog poor.

■* * * 9
A fearful and wonderful Bill is that which
Premier Philp has introduced in Queensland Parliament
to amend the mining companies law. It provides, among
other things, for the registration of stock exchanges and
the licensing of brokers and agents who deal in mining
shares. The object is said to be to promote brotherly
love and affection in the mining zoo and aquarium ; the
bear is to play in the same yard as the bull without con-
templating a skin-full of blood and beef ; the dingo is to
occupy the same fold as the lamb, and is not to have
visions of succulent savor augmented by mint sauce ;
the wild oat is to fondle brer rabbit; all is to be animal
amity. As possible would it be to so transform animal
nature as it would be to put common-sense into the
crania of some alleged politicians. In what way is the
registration of exchanges and the licensing of brokers to
promote honesty in share dealing or to instil confidence
in the minds of speculators? Will th’6 fact -that
Fitzsmith holds a broker’s license in which the
Warden certifies that he is a “ man of good fame
and character” make him seem more honest than he is,
or induce his average client to forego the common idea
that he buys in the highest market and sells in the
lowest ? The Bill also provides—or rather assumes to do
—for the limitation of liability on mining shares. It is a
Yes-No liability proposal. The limited liability principle
(which cannot be said to have been a failure in Queens-
land as far as legitimate mining is concerned) is to be
retained in a form which will lead to rampant dumniyism
and other rascally devices to shirk responsibility while
keeping the advantage within grasp. Dodger Smith,
well-known as a man of substance, will transfer his shares
to Dummy Brown, and the liability will be nominally
Brown’s while there is nothing to gain, but actually
Smith’s when any profit is likely to be made. Many
attempts have been made in Australian and American
legislatures to get over this difficulty, and all have failed,
but Philp’s attempt'is the most feeble ever suggested.
Further, a man may’ surrender his shares to the co., and
for three months afterwards he will be liable for calls
without having the right to re-take his shares. Then the
no-liability principle (which has been a positive curse in
Victoria) is to be allowed, but the author of the Bill is so
ashamed of this proposal that it is introduced only as a
side-wind—a No-Liability Co. cannot be formed without
having been first registered as a Special Limited Co. It
is said that the provisions of the Bill as to liability have
been devised with a view to induce Southern speculators
to dabble more freely in Queensland stocks. Then, why
not have, gone bald-headed for the no-liability system
with all its viciousness, with its violation of every com-
mercial idea and of every element of honor '?' Queensland
mining has not fared so badly that it wants the stimulus
of a system which enables the wary to grow fat and jolly
at the expense of the unwary. Taken altogether, the
Bill is a shocking sample of impracticability—it has not
even the saving grace of being well drafted. It would
require no lawyer to drive a coach-and-four through it—
any ordinary fool can see loop-holes for escape in every
line.

North Queensland anthracite coa, prospecting

A N.Q. paper credits the manager of Mt.
Garnet with the statement that he is now making a profit
of £4OOO per month, but, it is added, “ all construction
works are stopped, and reproductive work only is being
carried on, and there is much grumbling on all sides at

as to profit be true, why is it that the half-yearly meeting
has been deferred indefinitely? Are the directors waiting
for three or four months’ returns from “ reproductive
work only,” so as to have some semblance of show for
the repetition of the promise of divs. 11 at an early date? ’
• » * ►
“Galvo” gives an opinion: A par. in 8.,
16,6/02, says, re the Mount Kembla disaster, that
a fortune awaits the man who invents an electric
ramp that can be carried in a miner’s hat to re-
place the dim and unholy safety. But why wait
for such an expensive and unsatisfactory contri-
vance when dynamos, cables, and incandescent lamps
are to be had, ana when nothing but the criminal parsi-
mony of the companies which control the mines prevents
their being properly lighted ? Present writer has had
experience of electrical work in mines, and can see no
difficulty whatever in the matter, and yet very few mines
are equipped with electric light. The dynamo could be
fixed in the engine-house, and cables (which need not be
expensively insulated except in wet places) run into the
mine on insulators fixed at intervals to the timbering,
distributing lights in the various drives and tunnels as
they go In mines such as are most of the Southern
collieries, very few lights would be required, as the drives
go straight into the sides of the hills, and a lamp every
100 yards or so, as a beacon, would be enough. Where
the miners are actually at work on the faces, portable
clusters of lights, fed with current by flexible cables,
could be used, and removed when shots were to be fired.
The main cables could easily be extended as the drive
went forward by a mechanic with even a slight electrical
knowledge. Such an electrical plant would not, as a
rule, run into more than hundreds in cost, and the money
saved in lamp-oil would pretty well pay interest on the
outlay. Electric lighting of coal mines should, in any
case, be made compulsory. It is a monstrous thing
that hundreds of lives should be sacrificed to the
greed of shareholders who grudge every penny that
is spent towards anything but the actual “ getting” of
the coal.
• * * *
W. J. Barnett, the dapper Cornish engineer,
who has been commissioned to examine the Great
Cobar mine on behalf of the proposed purchasers,
has arrived in Sydney. “Little Billy,” as he is
known to his familiars, has been in N.S.W. before,
representing the Consolidated Mines Selection Co.,
which has been dubbed the Rejection Co., by reason of its
consistent policy of never exercising an option, but he is
no longer associated with it. During a former visit, Mr.
Barnett made a thorough examination of the Cobar field.
His appearance, combined with the intimation that other
mining and financial experts will soon be here, ought to
silence the know-alls, who assume that the purchase-pro-
posal is a fake. It is rumored that the big mine never
looked better than it does at present, more particularly
in the bottom levels, where high-grade sulphide is said
to be showing for a width of nearly 100 ft. Also, lately,
crosscuts have been put. out at different levels to inter-
sect the eastern lode, which was never properly prospected
by the original owners, and in every instance a large body
of good smelting ore of fair quality has been found. This
means practically a new mine. If the sale be effected,
doubtless it will be regretted by the syndicate—lucky
dogs to have occasion to growl at such a transaction.

• * »
The prophets have been all astray as to im-
provement in the value of copper. Correspondents in
England and America (men who are assumed to be good
authorities on the subject) proclaimed that long ere this
the £6O mark would be reached. There has been a dis-
tinctly upward tendency within the past few days, but
the movement is erratic, a 10s. rise one day being
followed by a ss. fall the next day. Perhaps it is better
that £6O should be reached by easy progression rather
than by a sudden jump—the former might suggest the
probability of steadiness when the pay-limit is touched.

The latest thing in gold recovery methods is a
vaporising amalgamation process which, on inventor’s
showing, gives better returns than fire assays in treating
sulphide ore. The transmutation of silver to gold is not
in the same category as this process by which 10s. woi th
of gold, in the various forms in which it is found in
nature, when put into the machine, will be turned out
14s. worth. It is an American idea— verb. sap. !
* * • *
All that’s black is not tin ! Greenbushes (W. A.),
tin ore is very variable in quality—from 40 to 73 per cent.
“ metallic.” In external characteristics the samples look
clean. The explanation lies in the fact that there are
several at present valueless minerals of the same specific
gravity as tinstone, such as tantalite, stibio-tantalite,
ferro-ilmenite, rutile, zircon. Tantalite and stibio-tanta-
lite are the worst impurities, as they introduce a con-
siderable amount of iron and antimony into the metallic
tin and cause loss of that metal in the refining process.
What nice cuss-words these will be for disappointed ore-
sellers 1
* * * *
The development of the Westralian coal-beds at
Collie proceeds steadily. Under Zeb Lane’s control the
Collie Prop, and the Wallsend workings are now con-
nected, and the doubt whether the two mines had the
same seam has been settled. Electric pumps and other
modern plant are being put in, and the briquetting works
at Bunbury are nearly ready. Elsewhere on the field the
Mechanical Coal-cutting Co. has sub-leased a number of
claims, and is working under the advice of ex-Govt.
Geologist Woodward.
• * * •
Associated (W.A.) : The £500,000 plant is a
failure in proportion to its cost. Directors state that, in
spite of milling, roasting, grinding, agitating, cyaniding,
and filter-pressing, at least Sdwts. remain in the residues
out of the 26dwt. in ore treated ; saving, only 70 per cent.
Lowering crowns of furnaces has reduced fuel consump-
tion by 25., but increased gold loss by 4s. per ton.
Timbering in 4th and sth levels was so defective that it
had to be replaced at considerable cost. Directors also
express surprise that a firewood tramway was not long
ago installed, as “ carting with horses uphill is a very ex-
pensive operation in W.A.” Adelaide expenses last year
were cut down from £1735 to £675, but an endeavor to
curtail London expenses by sub-letting present £7OO-a-
-year offices was frustrated, owing to there being no
demand for "such palatial premises.” Only redeeming
features in report are that co.’s ore reserves are estimated
at 100,000 tons, worth 26dwt., which is satisfactory, if
true, and that co. holds 10,000 Northern Blocks and
£150,000 in British Consols. Directors complain that
secrets leak out, and that recent 10s. jump in shares,
caused by alleged discoveries, took place before news
reached them. Discoveries at 800 ft. and 1050 ft. referred to
are of very little account so far.

Princess ivoyal, Norseman (W.A.), is opening
up better than ever at 300 ft., the reef being 14ft. to 15ft.
wideband worth 17dwts. Filter presses have been ordered.
Plant should easily maintain an output of 2000 oz.
monthly. Co. declared 16th div. of Is. Princess Royal
North and South are both suffering from “ that tired
feeling” experienced by most Adelaide-bossed mines.
• * * *
Lake View Consols (W.A.) seems to be in a very
bad way. Ore reserves will be exhausted in less than
three months unless new discoveries of pay-ore be made.
According to Hartman, the bore located payable ore to
the north at 1000 ft., and strenuous efforts are being made
to reach it. Chairman Govett is determined to exploit to
2500 ft. before throwing up the sponge, hence the recent
issue of 100,000 shares. An ore body, 3Jft. wide, worth
30dwts., has been located at the 700 ft. level north, and is
supposed to be the continuation of the Perseverance lo<te.
The main shaft is down 1225 ft., and is to be sunk to
1400 ft.
, * * •
Capt. Parkes retired from management of
Hannan’s Oroya (W.A.) on August 31, after being in
charge for nearly six years. His ever cheery face and
hearty welcome will be greatly missed by visitors to the
mine. Unfortunately, he did'not have the satisfaction of
locating the rich chute at No. 8 level. The mine is now
controlled by Bewick, Moreing and co., who strongly
object to visitors. The Brown Hill Oroya Co. should
soon be in a position to pay regular divs. Rumored that
Brown Hill Extended will join the combine.
* • * m
Bayley’s (Coolgardie, W.A.) has struck good
ore in driving north at 1000 ft. The reef is Bft. wide, of
which 3ft. is worth 36dwt., the balance being low grade ;
370 ft. of driving on the course of the reef were done*
before payable ore was reached, a great part of the dis-
tance carrying no quartz at all. Of course, Coolgardie is
elated at, and making the most of, the find, but a lode
formation in which the ore occurs in patches, and in
most of these patches is unpayable, is hardly worth
getting excited about.

OLASCOM.
Mrs. de la Juveney, the Melbourne lady
who is prosecuting Parson Worthington, alleges
that he deceived her by making her a bogus Rosi-
prucian, and giving her a metal pyramid, or some
such marine stores. Also, she alleges that the
following letter was received from him, and that
it confirmed her wavering faith when she was dis-
inclined to lend any more money to the unprofit-
able prophet:—
Isis, mother, Empress. The master waits, the Nile
rises, go at once. Fly swifter than the light, be still as
night, do not excite him. Be calm. The building waits,
the mosque waits, the lodge waits. Before the moon
comes to the disc, now, to-day, offer at once, compel
Osiris to accept. Take to him, or the Nile swallows for
another 1500 years. Alpha, Mooc, The Two Halls, Omega,
Delta.
The good old useful Nile, what a lot of money has
been borrowed on the strength of it ! In such a
case distance lends mystery to the document.
Even the most unaccountable prophet couldn’t
rouse any mystic excitement or abracadabra by
stating that the Yarra was a good deal deeper
than usual, but a statement that the Nile, which
looks much the same as the Yarra, only there is
more of it, isn’t so dry as it was yesterday, has an
element of awe about it, and seems to have a cash
value at times. And Isis, mainly because the
letter “ s ” appears in it twice and gives it a curious
sibillation, has an effect which something equally
ancient, but named Job, could never possess.
If Mrs. de la Juveney was made, as she alleges,
abogusßosicrucian by the Pastor, itshould interest
her to know that she isn’t alone in that misfortune.
A gloomy fact, which has troubled the world for
ages, is that nobody is really quite sure that there
A GAMBLE.
SHE: “ A girl takes great chances on her first
proposal of marriage."
HER: “ Ho you mean if she accepts?
SHE: “ Oh, either way.”
were ever any Rosicrucians. There were certainly
plenty of persons at different times who thought
they were Rosicrucians, being created members
of the order by bogus parties who persuaded
them in return to part with money or beer.
Lunatics, poets, astrologers, long-haired people,
frauds, old women, dreamers, cranks, thieves and
opium-eaters have professed to be, and in many
cases have believed themselves to be, genuine and
original members of the order. But its centre,
business address and duties were never known.
Its office hours seem to have been quite unfixed. The
most probable theory is that it was a tremendous
joke or hoax, like the line of ancient Scotch kings,
and the Tichborne Claimant, and the mahatmas of
Thibet, and Prester John, and El Dorado, and the
hand of the Devil which used to appear above the
Atlantic and haul down ships, and the bogus
Shakespearian plays of Samuel William Henry
Ireland. Another theory is that it was a simple
society of old doctors, astrologers and wizards —a
lot of ancient dreamers who formed themselves into
a sort of Ancient Disorder of Buffaloes to swap
dreams, elixirs of life, philosopher’s stones, corn
cures, spells, magic, empty imaginings, hair
restorers, false teeth, and such rudimentary science
as there was in those days. The Brotherhood is
said to have been founded, if it was ever founded
at all, by a German about 400 years ago, but as
it wasn’t heard of till a century or more after its
alleged formation, and then couldn’t be found and
has never been found since, it is about the most
elusive joke in history. The Germans of olden
times joked laboriously and at great length, and
the Rosicrucians appear to have been one of their
lighter efforts at humor.
Tasmania has its Margaret Catchpole. A
girl of attractive appearance and previous good re-
pute, and who had been masquerading in male
attire, was arrested in Launceston on sth inst., on
a charge of horse-stealing. She had previously
been apprehended at Chu Heigh, but bolted with
the first horse she could lay her hands on, rode
into the city and put up at a big hotel, where,
apparently, she called for her refreshment in a
way that disarmed suspicion.
♦ * * •
The war having ended, the occupation of
many of the Jingo parsons has, to a considerable
extent, departed. Some of the parsons have de-
parted, also, to a considerable extent. But the
Watchman remains.
* * * *
The most Jingo city in all Jingoistic Maori-
land is Auckland, which is always crying out
about making itself an impregnable
naval base—if the Imperial "Govt,
will put up the money. So, when
it was rumored that some of the
Auckland employers had stopped the
wages of their employees for Corona-
tion Holiday, “Paterfamilias,” also
“ Head of a Family ” and “ A Loyal
British Subject,” and other strange
creatures broke out in the public
press, and demanded that these pro-
Boers and public enemies be boy-
cotted out of the place. One of
them retorted that he only followed
the example of the local Harbor
Board, which had stopped the wages
of its men for that very occasion.
The Harbor Board explained that, if
it fell into the practice of paying for
holidays, the extra outlay might lead
to an increase in the rates. And
then the question droppedsosuddenly
and completely that one might have
imagined someone had drawn the
bolt, and it had fallen through the
trap and been hanged. There was
such a great peace that the noise of
a fly’s footfall was distinctly audible.
There are many silences in this world
—a memorable one is said to have
occurred at an Indian railway station,
when a tiger put his head in at the
pigeon-hole and breathed hard on the
tickets, and the Hindu in charge got
under the table andsaid nothing.
But for a real dead silence there is
nothing to equal the Jingo when he
hears that the course he is pursuing
may lead to an increase of the
rates.
The most crawlsome function held in
Brisbane for many a long day was the recent
annual banquet of railway employees. Groveller
after groveller (from a service honeycombed with
justifiable discontent) kissed the boots of the
Minister for Railways and the Philp Cabinet
generally. There was, however, one sturdy fellow
present, an engine-driver, who told the Railway
Boss it wasn’t a square thing to make engine-
drivers work 18 hours at a stretch; that men
sometimes fell asleep at their engines owing to
long hours ; that he himself had fallen asleep on
duty; and that some day a terrible accident would
occur. There was only one skull at this Egyptian
banquet—but it was a big one, and it smelt
horribly.

Before Judge Moles worth, recently, at
Melbourne, an old offender with a list of 21 con-
victions (six being for assault) was sentenced to
one month's imprisonment for violently assaulting a
boy, whose leg he broke. One month ! And he
might easily have got three times that for stealing
2d.
A. Theos: Be the
par in Bulletin (6/9/’O2),
advocating the rating of
churches as well as pres-
byteries. It is only neces-
sary to look at the two
cathedrals in Sydney to
see the justice of the claim
that religious buildings
should not go scot free for
the glory of God. St.
Andrew’s, dumped in the
centre of the busiest street
of the city, lessens the
value of all the adjacent
properties for business
purposes, and thus causes
quite sufficient loss of
rates to the City Council
without being allowed to
enjoy all the privileges of
city improvement for no-
thing. Then St. Mary’s
with its school buildings,
which are also free from
rates, occupies the whole
of a triangular block round
which the Council main-
tain three streets and
footpaths, with street
lamps, and the addi-
tional luxury of water-
carts in dusty weather
for the benefit of its
worshippers, school
children, and clergy. The majority of the people
do not go to church, so it seems unfair that those
who don’t use the buildings should have to pay
more rates to allow those who are church-goers to
escape their fair share of taxation.
m * * m
“ Buzz ” writes on a delicate subject:—
Apropos of “ Rude Man’s ” query as to what lovely
woman now does with her surplus abdominal adiposity.
A certain London “figure specialist” has invented a
somewhat terrifying armor-plated contrivance, to be worn
by the over-adiposed female, with a view to dispersing
the objectionable flesh (he calls it “ dispersing,” just as
if the corporation was a North Queensland aboriginal)
“ and distributing it more evenly over the form.”
Another professional figure-maker has patented a corrup-
tion of the Sandow pulley arrangement, consisting of
strong elastic ropes attached to the bedroom walls, at
which the patient (encased in a specially-constructed
metal belt) is supposed to pull 200 times morn-
ing and evening, till the surplus flesh has dis-
appeared, and, to quote the directions sold with
the invention, “ the female form has returned to its
divine grace and beauty.” However, it was reserved
for a Sydney matron to solve the difficulty and discover
the most novel and probably the most certain cure of all.
She is the wife of a hard-headed, middle-aged man of
business, and her surplus adiposity never troubled him in
the least. He received the scare of his life one night,
when waking suddenly he found the partner of his bed
absent and mysterious sounds coming from the hall.
Rising quickly, with visions of robbers clouding his
brain, he struck a light, and then very cautiously glanced
over the bannisters on to a scene he will never forget if
he lives to be a hundred. There was his respectable,
highly virtuous, middle-aged wife, in her night-dress,
deliberately rolling up and down the hall balanced on a
barrel, just as he remembered doing when a youngster.
His first idea was that she was rolling in her sleep ; his
second that she was out of her mind. “My God!
Emily,” he said, “ have you gonemad? My poor old girl,
come up to bed, and we’ll send for Dr. Jones in the
morning.” But the lady only laughed and rolled
furiously; “ Dr. Jones, indeed, much good he is. I
found this out for myself. I’m not mad, Henry, I’m only
trying to reduce my great tendency to heaviness below
the waist-line.” Then she rolled harder than ever.
• * • •
An ungracious Government ! Premier
See, announcing a grant of £25 to the mother of
Heaps, slain by a constable at Ginninderra,
sulkily added: “A sum greater, probably, than
she would have received had Heaps lived.” How
does See know what the poor old woman would
have received if her son had lived ? On this same
basis, the State would pay no compensation at all
for the killing of the son of a millionaire, because
a millionaire’s son, as a rule, doesn’t give the old
man even £25.
Brisbane hasn’t found its highwayman yet.
This was the man who, mounted on a gray horse,
intercepted the pay-clerk of the Gasworks on his
way to pay the men their weekly dole, and
after holding four men magnetised by the
end of his revolver, rode off with a bag contain-
ing £22s—this within the populous area in broad
daylight. Every rider of a gray horse is now
looked on with considerable suspicion by the
pessing citizen, and horses of that tint are going
cheap. It is understood that the police will
leave no stone unturned in the hopes of finding
the robber underneath, and if he is underneath
they will drop the stone down on him hard.
Apropos of highwaymen and such—wasn’t a police-
man named Guilfoyle murdered in Sydney some
time or other, and didn’t something happen to a
gentleman named Tart ?

“ What’s your experience with men?”
“Oh, I find that they often want to he sat upon"
According to a decision of three Malvern
(Vic.) justices, devilish cruelty to dumb animals
may be condoned if the perpetrator is a person
who has been at the Boer war. On a recent
Monday a returned brave, attired in kliarki and
cock’s feathers, appeared in front of said Bench,
charged with cruelly using a horse on 4th March,
1901. The prosecuting constable, who had “saved
it up ” for offender, could not catch him till he
returned from the war, hence the delay. He
described the animal’s pitiable plight after accused
had finished operating on it. It was raked along
both sides by continued use of a pair of flash
“hooks,” had a large bleeding wound on the
hook, withers and back raw and bleeding, mouth
chafed, bruised, and full of clotted blood, and
saddle-cloth soaked with blood. One of the
justices, who saw the occurrence, said “ the horse
had been treated in a terrible manner, and no
man in his sober senses could have knocked a
poor dumb animal about in such a way. But,
under the circumstances, as accused had been
serving his country, and had put in testimonials
of good character, he would be fined 205.” If the
benefit of the doubt was to come in anywhere it
would have been more logical to make the fine £5
extra, on the off-chance that defendant might have
been equally brutal to some other horse in S’Africa
while he was away.
• * * *
The Anglican flock in Tasmania is very
much disturbed and can’t sleep of nights, partly
because of the excitement consequent upon the
arrival of the new bishop from ’Ome, but mostly
because of some nasty remarks made in England
on the bishop’s embarkation. The words com-
plained of were used by Prebendary Bevan in a
farewell sermon to a batch of departing shepherds.
Referring to the fact that Tasmania was not an
easy field to labor in, he said :
Sixty years ago, when Dr. Nixon went out as first
Bishop of Tasmania, and the whole island was cursed by
the consequences of a convict penal system then main-
tained by our English Government, it was a common
saying that there was no God in Van Diemen’s Land, nor
ever would be.
This is indignantly impeached as “extremely in-
sulting,” and Tasmania’s “first families” are
shaking a threatening fist at the British Pre-
bendary, and there is a considerable rattling of
tea-cups and things.
• * * •
Decided by the Supreme Court of S.A.
(following an English precedent) that a man who
wrote across his will a revocation of it, properly
signed and attested, did not revoke it at all.
Because he remarked at the time “ That makes
my old will good,” and the old one had been
revoked by the execution of the new one, and
could not be thus revived again. So he appeared
to have died intestate ; but the Court was quite
sure that that was not his intention, and as it
would not hear of admitting the old will (which
clearly was his intention) it astonished all parties
by pronouncing for the new one.
* * * »
The old members of the committee of
Melbourne Women’s Hospital, thrown out at the
recent election, ascribe their defeat to the rival
faction’s action in making votes by stuffing the
subscribers’ roll with dummy names. If so,
this is a just retribution on those old members
who were so thrown out. The practice of
“Bought Votes” is much resorted to at elec-
tions of honorary doctors. The doctors have
always deprecated this custom —that is to say,
many doctors got in by means of dummy votes,
and then were deeply shocked when other doc-
tors tried to do likewise, and wanted the com-
mittee to alter the mode of election. But the
old committee found the practice an easy way
of raising the wind, and, besides, it was easier
to let things slide. Now it has slid itself.
SPRING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A whiff of rose ; of gold, a gleam,
A bunch of violets and a dream
Of silver through some green—a stream.
A glint of sunlight, scrap of blue,
A song-bird’s throat, a thrill or two—
A burst of song the bright world through.
Some spreading wings thro’ blue skies clear,
A joyous echo far and near
Which says : The Spring, the Spring is here !
HELEN JEROME.


“Travels in West Australia,” by May Vivienne
(from George Robertson and Co., Sydney), is the fruit of
an evidently most industrious and a distinctly intelligent
observation of life in the great Western colony. The lady
has collected her material with great diligence, has
enriched it with some very interesting photographs, and
the publishers have done their part well, with clear print
good paper, and admirable etching. The net result is a
“ chatty ” impression of the goldfields and coastal settle-
ments of W. A. There is now and again a useful hint —
for instance, that the boronia flower should be utilised
for perfume-making. What is said of that might also be
said of the pittosporum of the East Coast and many of
the wattles. But Australia learns very slowly how to
utilise her resources.*

anP Troubles.
A Roman father of the most cast-iron type
has transpired at the coaly town of Newcastle
(N.S.YV.). There were bailiffs in the father’s
house, and the 21 year-old daughter borrowed £2
to pay them out. In negotiating the loan she
stated that her parent wanted the money, which,
considering the bailiffs, seems strictly true. All
the same, when the Roman father heard of the
unauthorised use of his name, he was so shocked
that he offered, even out of his poverty, to pay
half the cost of prosecuting his daughter and
getting her put in gaol. Trie court looked un-
kindly on the stern old man, but it convicted the
girl, in a technical sort of way, of getting money
by false pretences, and imprisoned her till the
rising of the court. As the court rose immediately,
she ceased to languish in her dungeon after a con-
finement of ten seconds.
* * * *
By way of throwing a side-light on the
value of some identifications—especially some
women’s identifications—a divorce case, heard last
week in Sydney, is worth noting. The story was,
btiefly, that Ann Green had lost the run of her
husband, Bill, for 14 years. At the end of that
Eeriod she seems to have come across a well-known
ydney butcher, also bearing the plain but useful
name of Bill Green, and concluded that he was
her husband. She went further and positively
identified him as such, and started divorce pro-
ceedings against him. Butcher Green had to get
into the witness-box and tell about his whole
career, and his father, and his three sisters,
Du’cibella, Caroline and Augusta Amelia, and
call witnesses to testify to his long and unblemished
career among the chops and sausages, before the
lady decided that she was harassing the wrong
Green. In his case the name helped to bring him
into trouble, but even if a man is named Demitrius
Poliorcetes he isn’t really safe from identification.
* * * *
Jacob Garrard, boss of Sydney Water and
Sewerage Board, reported himself as being too
indignant for words over some recent statements
about the scarcity of water. But Jacob’s indigna-
tion makes no difference in the fact that the
cutting off of Sydney's morning plunge bath is
nowin sight, and if Jacob habitually plunges (this
paper doesn’t profess to know whether Jacob
plunges or not) he ought to realise that the case
has now got so bad that the public is past caring
even if Jacob bursts with indignation. There
comes a time in the affairs of man, especially
when man has nothing on and is standing with a
towel in his hand looking at a dry bath, when he
doesn’t care if he puts both feet on Jacob’s
feelings.

Ihe Sydney Town Hall committee of the
Mount Kembla Fund threw a little cold water on
a proposal to levy on the children in the public
schools, with the result that Education Minister
Perry rushes out with a statement of the £3OOO
odd the children subscribed for the Patriotic
Fund. Instead of allowing such subscriptions to
be a precedent, Parliament should, by a definite
expression of opinion, warn other Ministers from
“getting at” what are really wards of the State
while they are on the school premises. No re-
putable head of a drapery firm or factory would
circularise his staff for subscriptions. He would,
if he felt strongly, show a fine example himself,
and leave his hands to follow if the example in-
flamed them. A Minister for Education might
show such an example to his teachers, but to
employ “suasion ” of any kind upon the children
(who are not his children, and who may be chil-
dren of parents who condemn his opinions root
and branch, or who may be poor children) is an
offence against the spirit and letter of the
Public Schools Act.

As a record of doing things in a hurry
the case in Sydney Divorce Court t’other week,
wherein one Mary Johnson asked for the un-
tying of the knot with Thomas Johnson, on the
ground of desertion, brought out something that
would be hard to beat. The wedding took place
in Sydney in 1898, and from the evidence of the
petitioner, the whole business of courtship,
marriage and desertion seems to have been put
through in half-a-day. A little bit from the
published reports :
The proposal came to her (petitioner) between 1 and
2 o’clock in the afternoon, arrangements were made at
3.30, and at 7.30 she was his wife.
His Honor: No engagement before?
Petitioner : No, your Honor.
His Honor : Well, that’s about the quickest I’ve heard
of yet. How old were you at the time ?
Petitioner: Twent.y-four.
His Honor : Old enough to know better. People marry
in this sort of way, and I have to unmarry them.
Petitioner then said that the day after the marriage
her husband told her that he was going to Goodooga,
there to prepare a home for her, but she never lived with
him as his wife. They separated at the church-gates after
the ceremony.
* » * , •
Melb. University Council kicks at con-
ducting the proposed exams, for commissions in
the “Royal Australian Artillery,” remarking
“that the proposed curriculum is of a much
lower standard than its own matriculation exam.”
This means, in effect, that the proposed test is of
so elementary a character that the University
does not want to be mixed up with it. There is,
by the way, a fine financial opening for an Aus-
tralian lad in professional soldiering. With the
idea of making it a close corporation, those already
in it have been, for years, circulating the yarn
that nobody could attempt it without private
means. But, as a matter of fact, a boy of 18 or
19 is asked to pass a competitive exam, the
standard of which is lower than the matric.
There are seldom more than one or two candi-
dates, and the successful one is straightway
appointed (on certain easily carried out condi-
tions) to a billet worth about £3 10s. a week. His
parents have certainly to provide him with an
outfit at a cost of about .£100; but there the
expense ends, and the young man gets yearly in-
crements until, at 25 or 26, he receives about £250
to £3OO. It looks like a fairly easy career for
young, men with not.much brains.
* * • *
There is a muddle over health matters in
Brisbane. The Govt, recently took over plague
matters without abolishing the Epidemic Board—
so the Board lives to levy on local government
authorities, take care of “other” infectious
diseases, and hamper the Health Commissioner
generally. Plague re-appearing found everybody
unprepared. Three fatal cases this season out of
three ! (Last season’s fatal cases were 13 per
cent.) The Home Secretary, Foxton, pulls one
way; the Health Commissioner pulls another
way, and the Epidemic Board pulls any way that
will make them both wild.
* m * *
A close shave. William Henry Chard
married Annie Henderson a quarter of a century
ago, knowing at the time that Annie had been
divorced. But at the close of the quarter of a
century he faced N.S.W. Divorce Court, and
begged Judge Simpson to annul the contract.
The marriage took place August 28, 1876. Annie
was divorced August 25, 1876. That, though a
close shave, looks right enough. But at the time
of his marriage a law called 36 Victoria No. 9 was
in force, and under that law an appeal against the
decision of a Judge sitting in divorce is valid
within 14 days of the pronouncement of the
divorce. Mr. Chard put Aug. 25 under Aug. 28,
placed 14 opposite, and there was his case. His
union with Annie was, he contended, not legal.
The Judge agreed with him and annulled the
marriage.

S. Australian up-country police picked a
man up at the foot of the railway steps, and smelt
his breath, and asked him to say “metamorphosis,”
and, when he couldn’t, they locked him up for
drunkenness, and had him “ dealt with.” After-
wards, as his voice was still thick and he couldn’t
walk straight, they shut him up again ; and this
is what the doctor said at the inquest:
He had both eyes blackened, an abrasion on the left
side of the chest in front, a bruise on the left hip, and an
abrasion on the lower jaw, the vessels and membranes of
the brain much engorged and at one point bruised ; in
front of the brain, clots of blood ; the brain on right side
lacerated ; the skull on either side fractured ; at the base
of the skull, a fracture 4in. in length,
and much more.
That frail Reid, the Vic. Minister of
Education, with a view to making savings in his
department, purposes calling upon the inhabitants
of sparsely populated districts to provide the
board and lodging of an underpaid teacher among
themselves. The teacher will receive from £4O to
£5O per annum from the Government, and bed
and pumpkin from the parents of his pupils. He
will be allowed to be a sort of intruder, or un-
bidden guest, or spectre at the feast, wandering
from one house to another till lie works round the
circle of his pupils’ homes and gets back to the
first one again. Ho won’t be able to quit the
unendurable establishment till his time is up, for
he will be a fixture there, eating out an agreement,
and doomed for a certain space to consume
such hash, as comes his way. He will take his
turn in good houses, bad houses, leaky houses,
fowl-houses (or places only fit to be such), and
dirty houses. He will sit successively at table
with all the kids he has justly whacked. Who
would be a teacher ?
The Lost Book, alleged to contain par-
ticulars of some £17,000 worth of Jookoyork cele-
bration expenditure, was referred to in Tas.
Assembly recently. The book, explained Minister
Mulcahy, was last seen alive just before the Duke
left the State. “Put not your trust in Princes,”
casually interjected Urquhart, M.H.A. The
interjection passed, evidently unnoticed, for nary
member smiled, but when next day it appeared in
the papers, M.’sH.A. looked inexpressibly shocked
at their fellow-member’s irreverence, and more
than one in the lobbies regretfully referred to
Urquhart’s “ atheistic tendencies.”

“Cash”: Happened into the Supreme
Court at Adelaide one day during the late divorce-
case. Downer was holding forth about some other
case in which a man had kissed a married woman.
“It was in the presence of others. The lady
was in great grief. He merely offered the con-
solation which any man might offer to a woman.”
There was a general laugh. Boucaut sat, chin
on hand and the broadest possible grin on a
mouth that was tight shut, while he looked clean
through Downer. But his expression might have
meant anything, from “What an innocent you
are!” right round to “Well, you ought to
know.” It was inscrutable.

The death-list of the Drayton Grange was
eclipsed by that of the Britannic, which recently
arrived from South Africa witli Maoriland troops
on board. Up to date the packing on the Britannic
has been accountable for the loss of 23 lives. A
Royal Commission is now sitting to inquire into
this horror, but the press is not allowed to publish
evidence.

The Argus has been industriously collect-
ing evidence to prove there is nothing in the
alleged Newcastle (S’Africa) fracas. Captain
Coutts, “ a recipient of the Queen’s scarf ” (this is
corroborative detail), assured the paper that the
affair was a disturbance between a few Munster
Fusileers and three “totally unarmed” Maori-
landers, who were peacefully buying furs when
attacked. Then Captain Dalrymple supplied
further proof positive: “No lives were lost, and
the Maorilanders were armed with nothing more
deadly than mallets for driving tent-pegs.’ Three
totally unarmed men peacefully shopping with
large mauls!
“Melb.” : In a pawnbroker’s
window to day I saw eleven sets of
second-hand false teeth in a saucer,
Wow-ow ! These must be out of
dead mouths. Nobody grows sets
of loose teeth. Are tombs prised
open to get these teeth or are they
pawned by the broken-hearted rela-
tives of the departed? _ Consider
that as much as eleven guineas—and
how much more ? —may be paid for
a set of false teeth. They are
more valuable than all the rest of
the clay tenement. What’s a poor
woman to do who wants teeth and
sees eleven second-hand sets in the
pawnbrokery ? Prejudice may suc-
cumb. Why should the teeth be
thrown away ? They came out of
some mouth before they went into
the other. They chewed strange
food, and smiled with a pallid
gleam on people that you might
not like your own teeth to smile
on. and they gnashed at strange
creditors. It isn t nice to think of.
Yet everything is secondhand—even
your best girl. Someone else’s best
girl died, and became dust, then
grass, then cow, or wheat, and your
best girl was sustained by that cow
or wheat, and she is really the
previous girl dished up again.

“ Gumieaf ” writes about certain obnoxious
and over-rated animals :
To what extent are house cats, dogs, and other pets,
responsible for the spread of filth diseases ? It is admitted
by medical men that, many of these animals are subject
to the same diseases as mankind, but that phase of the
question is of comparatively small importance. No one
denies that they are capable of conveying diseases of all
kinds from one place to another and from one person to
another. That is the serious feature of the subject.
The germs of tuberculosis, fever, plague, and a -hun-
dred other diseases, may be carried about in the hair of
cats or dogs. It is utterly impossible to limit the wander-
ings of these dissipated hangers-on. The cat that this
morning lies purring on her ladyship’s lap, and the dog
around whose neck baby has thrown his arms, may have
been flirting in the dirtiest corners of the city during the
night.
Dogs, and even cats, have been useful to man. In a
barbarous age they took the place of the municipal rub-
bish-man and desiccator. Dogs and cats are still useful
to man in the country. Especially is this the case with
the dog. Ho helps man to manage his sheep and cattle.
In big cities, however, cats and dogs are not only
useless, but they are a positive danger. Unfortunately,
they are not only a danger to the dirty people who keep
them, but to their cleaner and more civilised, neighbors,
who do not. Modern science has shown the importance
of cleanliness ; particularly where human beings live in
great masses. Modern health authorities, therefore
insist upon people keeping their drains and back-yards
clean and wholesome —and it ought to insist on their cat
and dog being clean also. It is apparent from the num-
ber of fiith diseases still recorded in the big cities each
year that the clean-living portion of the community
must, in the interests of public health, take far more
drastic measures with their dirty fellow citizens than
hitherto.
Medical men declare that the fleas harbored by cats and
dogs also transmit diseases from one human being to
another. The feasibility of this view is plain. It cannot
be healthy for one to have his cat or dog bring along to
the house the fleas that have been on a plague-stricken
person or a leper. The beeping of cats and dogs is noth-
ing more than a custom. If it were only a silly one like
the church custom or the belltopper custom it would not
matter.
