A WOMANS LETTER.
Sydney, September 16, 1902.
My Dear Arinx—
A wet garden-party at Govt. House in a bril-
liant raoe-week is reminiscent of a bird of paradise
after a hose has been turned upon it. The rain
that spoilt the Rawsons’ party last week started
to fall at the time —ten minutes to three—when
the majority of the invitees, dressed all for the
blue unclouded weather and dry lawns, were on
their way to Cranbrook. The harbor steamers
were full of pretty, perishable gowns and dis-
appointed faces afraid to leave the shelter of the
cabins. For between the steamers’gangways and
the cabs out on the road there is time for rain to
ruin a frock, a hat and a sunshade. As for those
persons who were in trams when the rain started
—well, between the tram terminus and the shelter
of Govt. House many expensive dresses and sun-
shades were ruined, and became draggled shocks
and wrecks. At four o’clock the sky was again
but not the faces of the persons principally
concerned.
Not a patent-leather pressed the lawn, but
drawing-room, billiard-room and hall were packed,
for about 700 of the 1500 or so invitees turned up.
No peacocking of your beautiful new train was pos-
sible ; you just folded it round your feet and pon-
dered on the awful mismanagement of Persons
Unknown in sanctioning rain on a Govt. House
garden-party day. Most of the 700 got to the
refreshment tables and did a vigorous cake walk.
Those who got cards for the G.P., yet whose
nanies did not appear in the newspaper lists, are
paid out this time for their discourtesy in not
replying in proper time to the vice-regal invita-
tions. Some persons didn’t reply till 48 hours
before the G.P. occurred, so their acceptances
were not handed to the “ gentlemen of the press.”
I visited a somewhat literary household the day
before the wet party, and found the daughter of
the house trying to fish up a pen out of a full
quart bottle of ink, so that she could write an
acceptance of the vice-regal card. “ I hadn’t any
decent paper to write on before now,” she said, as
she fished with a slip-knot of string and finally
‘ got a bite ” with the glove-stretcher. There
were five other pens down in the bottle. When
she had dried her recovered handle and inserted
the one good nib she possessed, and taken a dip
into the quarter of a yard of ink, the nib broke
against one of the remaining snags. By the time
her note reached Govt. House, the party that
didn’t garden was one with the Transvaal war, and
Xerxes, and other back numbers.
The Tennysons occurred at the party, and Sir
Harry presented Her Ex. with a basket of prim-
roses. In any little attention like this the crowd
always looks for the illustrious father sig-
nificance, and some were asking “ was it His
favorite flower?” One woman, who had rushed
through (the index of) her Tennyson before
starting, asked the son of his father : “Isn’t this
gathering a Dream of Fair Women?” Pity he
didn’t reply with the name of another of his
father s poems, “ My life is full of weary days.”
Probably the rain of Garden Party Day was
precipitated by cannonading in celebration of
Admiral Beaumont’s rise in the Viking peerage.
His Ex. is now a Vice-Admiral. He and his
calm-faced American wife (who, the members of
the Sydney Women’s Club say, talks like a bar-
rister) will go hence, probably in January, and will
be replaced, also probably, by Rear-Admiral
Atkinson-Wdies, whose ships disturbed our fish
and our maidens some years ago. He married
Miss Alice Mort. Her late pa, T. S. Mort, stands
in stony effigy in Macquarie-place, Sydney, round
where the cheap ’bus drives it® three skeletons to
Circular Quay.
Farewell of the A.J.C. Spring Meeting last
Saturday. The Governors’ families and suites left
very early. A race crowd soon spots and nick-
names anything remarkable in the way of dressing
on the lawn. “Rhoda under the Pagoda” was a
lady with a tucked chiffon sunshade, perfectpagoda
shaped. Another woman soon beoame known
variously as Lady Gold (from Aubrey Beardsley’s
picture), “The Yellow Gohanner” and “The
Golden Butterfly.” The dress hailed from New-
castle, and it did Newcastle proud, for its wearer
has a very fine figure, and her frock—long, trailing,
golden—was beautifully made, trimmed length-
wise with black lace; and the train stood out
with a proper air of hauteur. That golden climax
simply dominated Randwick. One pretty, unob-
trusive dress was trimmed cape-fashion round the
shoulders with acorns of pink silk in gold cups and
oak leaves of gold thread.
Outside theatres and racing it has been a
phenomenally dull Spring meeting. A few
society hostesses entertained “a few friends,” so
the society columns say, at a dance, or a launch
party, or a picnic to National Park. Otherwise,
things were flat and sepia-toned. JVIr. Hennessey
(wish Mr. Dooley and his Mr. Hennessey would
come along), of Three Star Brandy fame, who
arrived by the China on his first visit to Australia,
has been watching our racing, and has expressed
himself as surprised and delighted. He had an
idea that we raced on bush tracks, with shearers for
jockeys, and horses bridled with strips of stringy
bark. Mr. Hennessey is a young man, ratlier
foreign-looking and very well-dressed, and he is
more at home talking horse than phylloxera.
A London paper of standing It is evi-
dent that art and literature will be honored in
the reign of Edward VII. Miss Marie Corelli
will be present at the Coronation as the King’s
guest, and so will Madame Bernhardt.” Marie
then, represents English literature; what an
injustice to Guy Boothby! Talking of Miss
Corelli, a literary Australian woman, now struggl-
ing in London, got a letter from her and sent it to
Australia to be read and admired by her dearest
woman friend, adjuring her to return it by next
post as she valued it enormously. The friend duly
read, admired, and returned it —after she had cut
off the signature for her autog? aph book.
Will Sydney women please follow Mrs. Presi-
dent Roosevelt’s lead and adopt the sunbonnet for
out-of-door morning wear. They couldn’t copy a
more becoming fashion. Made of fine muslin
and trimmed with lace, the usual objection of
weight cannot be urged against it. By the way, I
see that the cable joker’s statement that the
.rresidentess had left her home rather than meet
and entertain a frisky Grand-Duke has been taken
quite seriously—in Sydney, at least. Already a
tew editorial comments have been made, extolling
the ladys courage; and in private there is a
universal uplifting of the voice of praise. “If
only such a stand were taken here,” say the com-
mentators, “we wouldn’t see men of social
standing—married men, some of them—parading
the lawns at Randwick with bold, beautifully-
dressed ballet-girls.” Personally, I don’t for a
moment believe that Mrs. Roosevelt left her
h °Tkr j tJie reason misstated. If she did, and
PP ,: x , H le fact, there might be an interruption
or diplomatic relations. Besides, suppose our own
visited New York, would she let the dead
past bury its dead, or remind the world of his
frisks of younger days ? This story is on a par
with the £6O per year dress-money fiction.
Almost daily I used to see A. B. Worthington
crossing m the N.S. ferry steamers. A tall,
erect, gracefully-built man, no superfluous flesh ;
lean, clear-cut, clean-shaven face, quite unlined,
and free of any suggestion of grossness; and
square, prominent chin; the mouth with close-
shut bps. Face and figure might be those of a man
ot 00. His hair was quite white, and of the usual
manly cut._ There was nothing of the church or
stage in nis dress or (steamboat) demeanor, but
he always attracted attention, and the under-
toned guesses at his identity were interesting.
1 m sure he’s something at a university,” was
one, and the general opinion vaguely left him
at a scholar.” Pie had a man-of-the-study air
until one met his eyes—grey or blue, I think.
• °i-u an f lc l ul fy behind them, no steadfastness
m pee gaze, they were bright without being clear,
quick-moving without suggesting strenuous
thought, bn allow, unmeditative eyes, and want-
ing m sincerity; very alert as to glance. Still,
there was no mark of the Beast on Worthington.
He was often accompanied by a damsel of about
, °. r always the same damsel, a simple-
looking simply-dressed one, who used to gaze
adoringly at “the Master.” He must have had
what, for want of a better name, we call “mes-
meric power ” over his followers.
One woman I know, a woman with a strong
mind and an educated brain, telegraphed to me to
come to afternoon tea, that she might tell me of a
beautiful phrase the Yankee Chrysostom had
used m a sermon : “There was the Door to which
J- round no Key” (with the remainder of the
verse). ‘That’s a crib from Omar,” said some-
one, and got a book to prove it. The Master’s
new disciple was so infatuated that she said we
were jealous, and that Omar had stolen it from
A.PS. Vv. and she left me to pay for the tea.
Mr. J. 0. Williamson has bought the Holds-
woi th property at the top of Ithaca-road, over-
looking Lhzabeth Bay, and will move in there
next month. It is a big red mansion, with good
conservatories and grounds. “Ithaca,” his pre-
sent bydney abiding-place, which he bought some
years ago from Mrs. MacQuade, will be auctioned
to-day (Thursday). Architect Pitt, who designed
the Princess Theatre in Melbourne, has been in
bydney planning the re-building of Her Majesty’s
for Mr. Williamson.
Those who were at the Sydney University
commemoration last year, when degrees were
conferred by the Duke of York, may remember
Mr. J. J. Walsh, an elderlv man amongst all
those young ones, who received his degree of B.A.
His courage was admired, and great applause fol-
lowed him. He did not live long to enjoy his late-
acquired Arts honor, for his death occurred last
week at the age of 64. Mr. Walsh had been
teaching since 1862, and for 10 years, up to ’99
was headmaster of the Redfern Public School. It
was when he retired four years ago, at the age of
bO, that he took up Art studies.
Whom the King delights to honor! Melba,
who arrives to-day, has the vice-regal cars at her
disposal all over the Australian railways. A
London correspondent of a Melbourne paper says
that among the presents handed to Melba at the
moment of leaving London were eleven gold
pencil-cases set with jewels, several rings, and a
diamond and turquoise necklet, besides fruit,
flowers, and wine. There is a happy land every-
where for the great singer. Melba was born in
1865, it seems, so she is coming to us in the prime
of her voice. Well, I have no gold horseshoe
handy, but I’ll contrive one of golden daffodils to
the repatriated Australienne. Welcome, Melba !
Miss Vida Goldstein, who passed through
Sydney the other day on her way back from
Washington, gave an interviewer some interesting
information about the happy lot of the American
woman. As thus : “It is an absolute fact that
the American men treat the women splendidly.
Ihe home life is most beautiful. The women are
comrades in every sense to their husbands.” Then
further along : “It is extraordinary to see the
way the American men work. They are off to
their offices by eight o’clock in the morning at the
latest. Again: “They (the men) devote their
lives to making money. . . . Having made their
money they are more ready to turn their attention
to j üblic affairs. I hut accounts for the freedom
of women. The men make the money and the
women spend it.” Three queries arise out of
this yarn : How does the American man accumu-
late money if his wife spends it ? What kind of
beautiful home is it where the man is at his daily
grind by 8 a.m. and hardly sees his home in his
haste to get dollars ? And what kind of woman is
it who finds such happiness in being married to a
mere dollar-shark ?
. The Orphan’s Fair, which holds the Town Hall,
is a huge bazaar, and a Tivoli Theatre, and a
concert, and a race-course, all under one roof. Go
and see the show, for it is a worthy charity, and
unsectarian. The loveliest lace, fine as a mist,
may be seen at Mrs. Phillipson’s stall. It is
made at St. Martha’s Home at Leichhardt, where
are fashioned nearly all the swell trousseauxordered
by Sydney brides. St. Martha’s Home is, as well,
a servants’ training school.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The baritone of the Melba co. lias arrived.
He has a presence—is swagger, with fiercely
brushed-up moustaches, dark-hued and rather
handsome. His tongue lisps no English, and the
night he arrived, messengers were despatched for
Ernest Collins —Musgrove’s right-hand man—to
say a ‘How d’ do?’ and play the polite in the
gentleman’s vernacular.
“ Pa Mitchell (Melba’s dad) grins hugely at the
moths that swarm about the family-welcome
candles. ‘ None of these people wanted to know
me, or bother about me before, and I’m dashed if
I want them now.’ And so the swarm of syco-
phants are scorned by the grim old gentleman.
Mrs. lorn Patterson the best-known of the
Melba relatives —went to Sydney on Saturday to
meet the star, and was seen off by a host of gush-
ing friends. The Pattersons, like the Mitchells,
and all the rest of the clan, are simply overwhelmed
by the claims of friendship put forward since the
first Gabriel trumpet-tootle of Melba’s coming.
“Miss Secretary Fraser, of the Presbyterian
Ladies’ College, is another one fretting at the
claims made upon her by mythical school-chums
of ‘ dear old Nellie Melba.’ The lists of subscribers
to that superfine clasp souvenir, and the list of the
old she-collegians to ‘receive’ the diva, have
closed down on many bogus claimants.
“ Hibernian faces in all parts of the Town Hall
at that Hospital concert given by Miss Castles
and co. last Wednesday. The faithful clan turned
out in force to support the waning Amy, and the
Melb. Hospital fraternity helped to pack the
place. Nurses, in clean-looking prints and with
white satin bows astride their nobs, dis-
pensed the greenest of Erin-go-bragh programmes,
at sixpence apiece, and raked in the shekels.
The audience was not smart, save in patches, and
mostly used its brolly and its welted sole to
emphasise approval. Judge Williams’ family
provided one of the smart patches. The bride-
elect of the family was as gorgeous as dove-grey
silk and chiffon and a towering Sarah Marlborough
head-dress could make her. Clerics loomed up
like cypress trees in a balcony-scene of floral
mountings.
‘ The pilgrimage of Castles and co. through the
backblocks wrought certain changes. Miss Amy
herself substitutes a quick, lively tread on the
platform for her former graceful if apathetic
saunter. She takes her vegetable trophies, too,
more readily—perhaps because they are harder to
get than of yore. The clever and pretty Maud
Dairymple grows smarter and sweeter vocally.
Una Bourne vanishes to a shadow.
“Woolly Scherek’s little girl—Paula—proved a
rather clever and very confident debuting fiddler
at the Castles’ sing-song, though she was a poor
substitute for Nora M'Kay. Paula is in her
teens, and just passed into the long-frock stage.
Pa Scherek’s excitement this night showed in the
extra flushed cheeks, the spasmodic brow-
wrinklings, and the extra dressiness of the white
mane.
“ There was much neglect about the V.R.
arrangements that Castles night. In the first
place, the visitors from Guv’ment House walked
in without any National Anthem. No Governor
should have to walk in without that music, or how
are people to know that he is a great man ? Then,
as the musical Clarkes courteously and patiently
waited till the concert’s very last tootle, they had
to defend themselves from a pushing, boisterous, ,
rough-handed throng. Their exit in this way was
undignified, and all the lean Capt. Vallange’s
scornful looks fell on the scrambling crowd heed-
lessly. His long hands swam out from time to
time to break the tide that foamed around his
V.R. charges, but the side strokes were futile, and
the Clarkes were hustled.
“Part of the affectation of the tea-room business
is that the ‘ attendant ’ doesn’t choose to be
regarded as a waitress, but as a professional lady
of the same grade as an actress and liable with her
to ‘stage-fright.’ One relates how, at her first
‘ appearance, ’ a panic seized her, and fear rendered
memory a blank, and limbs inert. When she
managed to control her perambulating parts, and
walk to the table where some people had just
taken their seats, she found her tongue glued to
the roof of her mouth. It must be a very nervous
young person indeed who is thus stricken dumb at
the dreadful responsibility of carrying around tea
and cake.
“ Mr. and Mrs. Sandow, despite their six years
of wedded life, are loving as a honeymoon couple.
Each possesses, deservedly, the undisguised ad-
miration of the other, and they are ‘ dear ’ and
‘ darling ’ to one another till one thinks of the
other strong men of history and remembers how
Samson, Hercules, Theseus and the rest were all
slaves, drawn by the single hair of beauty. Mrs.
S. watches Eugene leave Menzies’ with the scrupu-
lousness of a valet, and fixes his tie straight, and
removes the last speck of dust from his beautiful
clothes before she permits him to appear before
the world. And Eugene himself is a man who
knows the value of appearances. ‘ What do you
smoke?’ asked an admirer at his private display.
‘I prefer a pipe,’ replied Sandow ; ‘ but you can-
not smoke a pipe in the street, so I generally use
cigars. ’
“ The Oaklands Hunt Club had a big crowd at
its Moonee Valley races on Saturday, but smart-
ness took no part in the dress show. Even the
contingent of hunting women couldn’t boast a
first-rate frocker amongst them. A theatrical
charmer took the cake with a clerical grey hairy
garb, cut according to the broad Yank fashion,
and having a Vandyck tracery in black on the
skirt, and some pink velvet pipings in the vicinity
of the neck and shoulders. With it went a big
French hat of coral felt, banked up with clumps
of coral chiffon.
“The first Melb. audience to see ‘Sherlock
Holmes ’ was the least fashionable that Her
Majesty’s has seated for many an opening night.
The dry, detective name did not appeal to the
chiffon duchesses. Only one girl-matron, gorgeous
as a bit of Japanese tapestry, shone with any
lustre. Her golden thatch showed up in the in-
terval illumination like jocund day on a misty
mountain-top. Miss Janet Waldorf sat in the
circle, her hat on her knees, and a woebegone look
m her intelligent eyes.
The imported Yank., Cuyler Hastings, is
something new in stage heroes. He has strange
ways, and a habit of avoiding direct talk or pose
to the audience. He says his best things with
closed eyes and a masked face. He is dark,
melancholy, pallid, and not so good-looking as
the camera makes him. His ease is marvellous.
So are the bass and baritone tones of his voice.
He fits the part so well that the wonderful science
of discoveries, with eyelash quiverings and nail-
specks for ground-work, seems in no way absurd.
“.Loose garb is to reign in the coming hot
season. The er—er —stout part of the human
frame disappears, and a waist belt is drawn down
over the obliterated region. Sac coats in grass-
lawn and holland are to the fore, and big bell
sleeves, Russian blouses, and transparent yokes,
collars and neck bands, all combine to give com-
fort. The most approved head gear inclines to
bread-platter shapes, the sauces and soup straws
having declined in favor. Trimmings are flat;
the towering mast sails are off.
“Harcourt Beatty, whose reserved, rather
frigid role as the philosopher of the ‘Men and
Mice’ bill, fails to get home with any but the
studious and the dignified, reminds one all through
the performance of Joseph Carne, one of the finest
actors ever associated with the Broughs. In
manner, in gait, in gesture very oft, Carne appears
in Beatty ; but the latter lacks the other’s charm
of voice and the sweetness that his face ever
caught in love-passages. But Harcourt is con-
vincing in intense passages, and at his best in
moments of dramatic force.
“ The sentimental sigh for more tender nestling
in ‘ Men and Mice.’ It provoked the multitude
to see the giddy Nell and the dissolute Charles
part (in the last play) after winning their hard-
earned solitude, without any hugging. That sort
of thing annoys one. It isn’t a likely thing, and
it isn]t fair. In ‘ Men and Mice ’ there are only
two instances of what golfers would term ap-
proach. In one case, the Professor adjusts a cloak
on the sobbing Peg’s pretty shoulders, and he
distantly, reverently, fearfully twines his arms
about them, looking over her head the while, as
he stands at her back and leans timidly towards
her. But Peg gets alarmed at this touching atti-
tude, and the hugging is switched-off just as it
promised thrillfulness. The second occasion is a
real hug and one brief kiss which Peg gives her
young lover. There is one faint attempt at a
nestle on the Professor’s part in the last act, but
as it takes a purely platonic hue the sentimental
Miss is not interested.
“The Rupertswood Bracelet stakes for the
Findon Harriers’ swagger races are on show in
Collins-street, and are most glittersome gew-gaws.
Madame Rupert remains longer abroad, having
booked herself and young sister for the Delhi
Coronation spree, for which all fashionable India
will put on a clean pinny of cloth of gold and
rubies. The rest of India will tighten the string
of its pinny, as usual.
“Two of the most chic frockers and formerly
fashionable girls in this Parliament city, have
started a petticoat and blouse business, which
Sassiety promises to patronise. A petticoat
shop ! It was destined to come. Never in the
variable dress chronicles of past centuries has such
great prominence been given to the petticoat. Nor
does this scribe remember seeing in any old world
(that is, the skirt-world) picture the slightest
inkling of under-frills, let alone a bare-faced strip
of petticoat. No matter how the beauties bared
their shoulders, they observed a certain propriety
(in pictures) over their petticoats. The next step
after a bare neck was absolute nudity. Now,
dozens of photos, to-day favor the dress-sacrifice
and the under skirt display. The train is gathered
up and the petticoat exhibited.
“This week saw the end of the Geelong golf
contest, and Mrs. Harry De Littlb (the smartest
squatteress in the district), who outpaced all the
local and visiting players in the handicap struggle,
capped her contest with the championship in the
special crack match. Mrs. De Little’s play broke
the record, and all suburban club glories fade
beside her Geelong achievement.

Visitors find new cleverness in it, since the

“ THE AUSTRALIA ”is loftily situated; from
its dome, the Pacific Ocean can be seen, and the
whole of Sydney.
To its favorable and airy position, “THE
AUSTRALIA ” adds every comfort and appoint-
ment. It is fireproof from tower to basement;
and an efficient staff patrols the building day and
night. It possesses the most elegant of drawing-
rooms, handsomely appointed dining-rooms, cosy
bar lounges and winter gardens. The system
and attendance of the hotel are unsurpassable.
J.. URE SMITH (Late of Menzies’), Manager,

care of the great Scotch surgeon. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are tne favorite prescription
of the famous Edinburgh University doctor.
They actually make new blood—and good blood is the only cure for skin com-
plaints, indigestion, palpitation, nervousness, headaches, and backaches.
These are all diseases of the blood —and they have all been cured by Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills.
When you take Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills it is just the same as if the doctor visited
you and prescribed —only it’s not so expensive.
Mr. G. Griffith, March, near Orange, says: “Liver troubles affected me seriously for five years. I grew weak
and nervous. Sometimes I couldn’t stand through strange attacks of h 'art palpitation and dizziness. I suffered so

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Obit.: Mrs. Mary Macdonald, alleged to
have been the first female white child born in
Maoriland (in 1830, at Hokianga).
A Melbourne politician declares in writing
that it is “an outrage to pay a mere clerk in the
Civil Service £4OO per annum.” And how do you
think he spells “ annum ? ”
“ Coghlan, the Quetelet of Australia ” ;
“Parkes, the Gladstone of Australia”; “Gordon,
the Swinburne of Australia”; “Lawson, the
Kipling of Australia”; and so on. Even Lombroso
falls into the banal habit.
Mercer, Tasmania’s new Anglican bishop,
is a brief round person, with a self-satisfied expres-
sion and the air of having just finished a good
dinner. He presents a fleeting resemblance to a
low comedian of the George Lauri type.
Not often in Australia or anywhere else
does a boss get such a certificate as was awarde 1
this month to the late William Rome, mine
manager of the Broken Hill Central, when a fine
monument was bought by nearly 400 former
employees and built over his grave.
There was very great hesitation and some
delayonthe part of the S.M. Herald in chronicling
the bankruptcy of the man whose downfall was
due to lending money to Congregational Pastor
Tremayne Dunstan. The S.M.H. proprietors
“ sit under ” a Congregational pulpit.
At Sunbury (Vic.) cemetery, the other
Sunday, death came to a local identity named
Parkin just as he had finished shovelling earth
into the grave of an old friend. Stepping away
from the grave-side he fell down dead, the result,
seemingly, of his getting mentally and bodily
distressed before he had digested a “hearty”
Sunday dinner. How many people have shortened
their lives by the hearty dinner habit. And the
hearty supper !
Worthingtoh, the apostle of “Trewth,”
now up to his neck in worry, bears a striking
resemblance to Joseph Carne, the grave, grey pro.
whom Brough brought to Australia. Carne once
complained wofully that he had been forced to
keep under cover in Maoriland, especially in
Christchurch, because every time he ventured on
the street he was rushed and forcibly embraced by
some plump, middle-aged dame or another who
insisted on calling him “the Master.”

Over in Arkansas passed out lately Oapt.
John Hallahan, who had roamed a good many
Australian mining fields, and in the end got to the
White Feather (Westralia) with the first of them.
He could not settle long at anything, and at last a
spell of soldiering in Cuba sent him home an in-
valid to crawl out the rest of his days at health
resorts.
The N.S.W. “naval” forces take them-
selves very seriously. A Gazette just published
sets forth that two Sydney doctors retire on the
rank of “fleet surgeons,” another person on the
rank of “ fleet engineer,” and a fourth individual
gets back into humble civilian life as “chief
gunner.” Problem: Find the guns and the
engines and the fleet.
A story of hard luck. An olden-time pros-
Eector in Stawell (Vic.) district got it into his
ead that a group of little hills, regarded locally
as barren and hopeless, contained good gold He
fossicked round for two years, during which time
his wife and three youngsters just about starved.
At last, he came home with specimens worth, at
least, 20oz. to the ton, but would not divulge the
locality of the find until he had put his pegs in.
His wife, on waking next morning, found him
dead. He had taken his secret with him, and his
lode, if it was one, is still missing. This sort of
thing is Nature’s idea of humor.
Not often a layman gets thanks from a
judge for advocate’s work, but 16 years ago some
came the way of A. E. Sawtell, a S. A. chronometer
maker. With remarkable pertinacity and some
skill Sawtell championed the side of the “con-
tribs.” in the Commercial Bank of S. A. wind-up ;
and Judge Hay not only gave him a little out of
his vast store of grease but ordered the estate to
bear the layman’s expenses. The incident is
revived by Sawtell’s death the other day in Ade-
laide. The principal in said bank-smash, by
the way,'isn’t nearly so far outside Australia as
most people have supposed, and his position is
very modest indeed.
Judge Boucaut, who tried the interesting
Adelaide divorce case, of which all Australia has
been talking, on the verge of 72 remains virile,
alert, and incisive. Many think him a hard man ;
but be really isn’t,
though he is absolutely
unemotional, matter-of-
fact, and a detester of
shams. Though fairly
well-to-do (he breeds
high-class horses as a
paying recreation), he
has always kept right
out of “society.” Of
all things he hates
“ side ” most. When
not at work he has been
happiest on his yacht;
and his bronzed sons
follow him in devotion
to the sea and aquatics.
Once he was suspected
of inclining to a repub-
lican Commonwealth of
Australia; and his re-
ligion was set down as
“reverent agnosticism.”
He became a lawyer 48
years ago, and has been
a Judge for 34 years,
which latter beats the
record of Yic. Judge
Williams by three years,
and N.S. W. Acting-
C. J. Stephen’s by nine.
Ho w jokes travel
and change. Late Judge
Cope, of Victoria, told
present writer about 20
years ago a story of his
experience as a barrister.
He had helped a man—
a butcher by trade—out
of some trouble at a
county court, and was
being driven by him
back to hotel. Client
was grateful, and, as
Cope declined a gift-offering, said: “Well, then,
take this tip —never eat sausages in summer.”
This joke, varied but not improved, appeared
recently in an English snip paper as having come
off “at Tynemouth the other day,” and was
transferred to the Saturday comic column of the
Sydney D.T. Where did Judge Cope get it?
Probably from the Talmud !
Dr. Backhaus, a R.C. priest of Bendigo
(Vie.), who died in 1882, left large properties in
Bendigo to accumulate for 20 years. The time
has now expired, and £BO,OOO becomes available.
The money doesn’t go for roads, irrigation, or
suchlike plain, useful purposes; the Church
swallows it.
N.S.W. ex-Judge Wilkinson, whose de-
cisions when on the bench were pretty often dis-
puted, once professed to have doubts about the
existence of a material hell, and on a trip to Eng-
land consulted the late Cardinal Newman on the
point, but got no answer except “to pray for
guidance.” Returning to Sydney, he had a
talk with the late W. B. Dailey, hoping to
raise a theological disputation with him.
But Dailey refused to be “drawn.” “You
may not believe in a material hell now,”
said the monkish-looking little man with a
twinkle in his eye, “but, believe me, in
the next world you will, when you are con-
fronted with the shades of the many liti-
gnats who were the victims of your extra-
ordinary decisions.”
“ Hermes ” has a reflection :
One of the best assets of a dynasty is a long list
of dead princes, slain in battle at the head of
their country’s troops. Imagine the moral effect
had that deadly search-light that suddenly
flashed over Magersfontein glared on the figure
of a British prince shouting to his men not to
run, but to lie down and shoot, or charge the
rifle-pits; and later on that prince had been
found dead in those same rifle-pits I It might have pro-
longed the life of English monarchy another century. But
no British princes do these things. They figure as Field-
Marshals, Generals, Colonels, Admirals and Commanders ;
they wear bushels of medals and orders galore, but they
do not wear the V.C. nor do they carry scars. Truly
one distant, poor relation did die of fever on the West
Coast of Africa, but he was so far removed as not to
count. The Princes are found everywhere but at the
Mauser point.
The founder of Margarey’s mills,
whose flour did much to make the reputation
of South Australian wheat, died in Adelaide
at the end of August, after many years of
retirement. For a time Mr. Margarey was
associated in the milling business with John
Ridley, the man who claimed to have made
the first reaping or stripping machine.
Like most of them, he found the handling of
wheat a very risky business.
Tasmania’s new Bishop, Right Rev. Dr.
Mercer, who was enthroned at Hobart, the other
day, with considerable pomp and ceremony, hails
from Gorton (England). He is accompanied by
his wife and three adopted daughters. Tas-
maniennes were getting ready to give the dear
bishop a hearty welcome till the announcement of
the “three daughters” item modified the enthu-
siasm considerably. Bringing three more un-
married girls to Hobart is a superfluous proceed-
ing.
The deceased David Cohen comes under
stamp-duty in N.S.W. to the extent of £52,840.
Altogether he left behind nearly a quarter-of-a-
million. All the N.S.W. Cohens did well at
storekeeping—their Maitland, Tamworth, New-
castle, and Morpeth businesses served as a solid
stepping-stone to' larger ventures in England.
David, Abraham, Louis, Samuel and the rest
counted money in five figures. Even Mr. Justice
Cohen did a share of lucrative work behind the
country-store counter.
“ Zasa ” : Re Ernest Weaver, mentioned
in B. (19/7/’O2) as the murderer of the captain of
the schooner Colonist:
He is still in gaol, at Suva. About a year after his
incarceration, a small one-roomed house was built in the
gaol-yard, and then a high wall was erected around it.
In this prison within a prison Weaver was immured—in
solitary confinement. The official reason for this has
never ieaked out. Things don’t leak out much under
Crown colony government. The leading Suva newspaper,
at Coronation time, published a letter suggesting that
Weaver be set at liberty, but nothing has so far been
done in the matter.
A Hobart policeman, on wharfside duty,
the other night, stumbled against something in the
darkness. Suspecting a drunk, and observing
that the prostrate one was dangerously close to
the water’s edge, the bobby laid hold with the
object of dragging the weary snoozer out of danger.
But it felt wet, globular and shiny; moreover, it
barked at him ; so he let go and ran for reinforce-
ments. A lantern revealed a monster seal, which
was cold-bloodedly slain. It is supposed that the
damp one was on his way to Parliament House,
looking for Fysh.
The Melbourne widow who parted with
£1096 to Prophet Worthington states that his first
appeal for temporary assistance was quite modest.
He wanted only £2B, because he was “behind
with the rent ” of the rooms where he babbled his
Mahatmatio religion. One would have thought
that even a widow would find it hard to develop
a mystic faith in a man who borrowed money to
pay the rent and offered her a mundane bill of sale
over the furniture, but the lady went on supply-
ing coin until she believed herself to be‘ a high-
priestess of Isis or something.
“ Wreford of Moorara,” who died on the
track going home from the abortive Broken Hill
conference over the shearing row, was one of
the half-dozen best-known men west of the Dar-
ling. A fine judge of horses, he imported a few
good ones, and bred more. Though he had few
rows with his workmen (one of the oldest of
them dropped dead on hearing of his old boss’s
decease), it was at Moorara that about the most
daring and sensational stroke in an Australian
strike was aimed—the burning of the river-steamer
Rodney, laden with non-union wool.
A FAMILY AFFAIR.

IMPEOGNE : “ Why not? It was your daughter
who put me on my uppers."
A hard old pioneer passed out, the other
day, at Omeo (Vic.). “Yankee Bob” Fordyce
was on all the rushes of the early days, spent
money as fast as he made it, was a hard drinker,
and the best poker-player in the district. Lat-
terly he had been out of luck, and when he broke
his right arm t’other day he refused to see a
doctor, because he was unable to pay for his
services and would not accept charity. After a
fortnight of suffering the tough old man (he was
69) tried to blow himself up with dynamite, but
only suoceeded in burning himself badly. Taken
to the hospital, “Yankee Bob” seized the first
opportunity to get out of bed, and sprang head
first through the window. The broken glass cut
his throat, and the valiant old poker-player died
in 20 minutes.
•Frank Moore, who was sentenced to five
years’ imprisonment for threatening to murder
M.L. actmg-Premier Ward, but who subsequently
was pardoned on condition that he forthwith left
the State, has come to Australia. When the M.L.
Judge, after a jury had brought in a verdict of
guilty, gave accused five years, he doubtless
believed that it was merited. When the M.L.
Cabinet afterwards pardoned Moore, it apparently
did so on the ground that he wasn’t fully respon-
sible for hisactions. But after coming to the conclu-
sion that the prisoner was too irresponsible to be
held accountable for his doings, Maoriland made
his release conditional on his clearing out to some-
body else’s premises, to be dangerously irrespon •
sible there, and so threw the burden of its
generosity on a foreign State which hadn’t the
slightest say in the matter. And it didn’t even
give Australia warning by putting him on the
ship’s manifest as “ one irresponsible character,”
along with the potatoes and things.
John M‘llwraith (brother of Andrew and
the late Thomas), who dropped dead at Ayr
(Scotland) railway station last week, was one of
the first Australian manufacturers to discover how
far the Yanks had improved on the old-fashioned
British machinery. The machinery for lead-
rolling, etc., which he introduced into Melbourne
in ’76 was mainly copied from ’Murkan models,
though, like a loyal Scotsman, he had it made for
him at his native town of Ayr. M‘llwraith
served the land of his adoption by getting his
raw material from the lead mines of Westralia,
and running his local manufacture successfully
against the imported article. The Melbourne
Lead Works of to-day (established in 1855) are a
respectable monument to his memory. In his
middle-age, Mac. took a hand in Melbourne
municipal affairs—mayor for 1873-4 —but from the
time when he became partner with his brothers
and Malcolm M‘Eachern in the MTlwraith-
M'Eachern firm, he didn’t figure much outside
the commercial circle. In every circle that he
entered he was the same old, hard-headed Tory.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— ln the case of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
“Oktis”: The phenomenon of springs
re-filling in drought-stricken districts has repeated
itself in the Young (N.S.W.) district, where the
waterholes along certain creeks—in granite country
—have filled up without rain. The waterholes,
for months, have contained more water than in
many previous years.

Some reminiscences of the cow and the
wilderness :
Dear Bulletin,—" Quartpot ” (8., 21/6/’O2) is quite right
about D’Arcy Uhr bringing the first mob of cattle through
from Queensland, but he mixes things up. Uhr brought
the cattle through for Dillon Cox, about May, 1872. W’e
bought some cattle from him then on the Roper. His
brother brought another draft through in 1873, when I
was on the Roper re-poling the overland telegraph line.
Phil Saunder and Adam Johns came through by them-
selves —they had no mates.
JLatour, Elvey, Nation and a boy, I forget his name,
left Queensland in the beginning of ’74. Nation, an old
man of about 63, and Elvey, only went to help Latour
with the cattle—Bo head. They had a row at a place
since called Settlement Creek, and from there Nation and
Elvey started to strike my camp on the Roper. They
had two saddle-horses and two pack-horses. They got
bushed in the Coast Ranges, where they had to kill the
two pack-horses and live on their flesh. Then they killed
one of the saddle-horses, and dried his flesh. Elvey left
half with Nation, who could not travel any further, took
the remaining horse, and rode until he struck my line,
and, luckily for him, some bullock teams under the charge
of Mr. James Aldridge.
I went out again and found Nation’s body, and I buried
him in some ranges, which I named the Nation Ranges,
about half-way between the Limen Bight and the Mac-
Arthur River. On that trip I named the Rosy, Aldridge
and D’Auvergne streams—can’t call them rivers.
There was no black killed wantonly, or otherwise, on
the Roper in my time. “ Quartpot ” writes from hearsay.
Bill Batten, not “ Ballon,” was killed on his way back,
but to settle all these confused stories about the N.T.
would take too much space.—R. R. Knuckey, formerly
officer in charge O.L. Telegraph Construction.
“ Oktis ” : lie “T.N.’s” complaint (B.
16/8/’O2), the present drought is not as severe as
many shorter droughts of recent years. There is
enough fodder grown in N.S. \V. in a good season
to feed (if it were saved) all the cattle and sheep
in Australia for years. Australians will never
store up for a bad year. They let their grass dry
up and blow away, or be swept off by bush fire,
when it should be in stacks. They let their straw
rot in the paddocks with the autumn rains. They
sell their wheat and hay for rioxt to not hing when
it is plentiful, and buy it back at treble the price
when the dry time comes. They are constantly
doing it, and all the years of heartbreaking ex-
perience has never taught them the necessity for
saving fodder.

“ 6xB ” : Just now the West is a fine field
for vegetarian apostles. To one western town
frozen mutton is imported from Maoriland. In
Bourke meat is 7d. a lb., and only one butcher
open now ! Tinned dog is too dear to be even
thought of by the workless and penniless popu-
lation, while kangaroos, emus, wallabies, rats
and “gohannas” have long ago given up the ghost
round here. Yet we read in the dailies that
Australian mutton is firm on London markets and
in good demand at 4Jd. a pound !
* * * *
“ E.S.S.” : Be aboriginal twins (B. 6/9/’2).
They occur often enough, but are seldom reared.
I knew a gin at Yalpunga, north-west N.S.W.,
who, some years ago, gave birth to twins, and
killed one of them immediately afterwards. An
inquiry followed. The gin pleaded inability to
rear and transport two at once. This system of
regulating the family according to circumstance is
common all over Australia.

According to the tracker’s account of the
Kenniffs’ capture, the Queensland police fired a
dozen or fifteen shots, and yet the K.’s surren-
dered without a scar on them. Also at Capella
(Q.) poisoning case, the poisoned water caused the
death of dozens of horses, cattle, sheep, and goats,
but did not kill the policemen, although they drank
it. After all, it takes a lot of things to kill a Queens-
land policeman, and a lot of policemen to kill any-
thing.

More matrimony. “ Taiki ” writes from
N. Queensland: —
He had been dying for weeks, and each night she
brought the children to the bedside to say “ good-bye,”
in case he should be dead in the morning. Once she even
sought her neighbours to rejoice with her, for he had
passed away. The joy was short-lived, for on entering
the death chamber the supposed corpse sat up and
yawned. They fled precipitately. One woman had not
joined the happy throng, but, gathering fair white
flowers, had formed a wreath ; armed with this, she also
went to view the corpse. The sorrowing wife took the
wreath, and, in answer to the donor’s flattering words of
sympathy, said : “ He isn’t dead yet, but soon must be.
I’ll put the wreath in the cellar, and water it for a day or
two.” On the seventh day the corpse, refusing to die
any longer, got up and walked. Finally, the woman who
made the wreath died. The wife, being a frugal person,
had kept the foundation of the wreath, so she in her turn
culled fair white flowers, trimmed it afresh, and, casting
a reproachful glance at her husband, placed the symbol
at the feet of the maker. N.B. —This story has haunted
me since I have been here. They showed me the man
who should have died, his wife, also his cellar. Probably
they would have shown me the woman, but she had left
no address behind.

“D.H.R.”: The other day a good old
joke happened again. If the boss had been a
regular reader of The Bulletin lie would have
recognised the joke, but he didn’t. A man,
mounted on a broomstick, and dragging after him
an old box limb, tied to a piece of string, -accosted
a squatter on the station verandah and requested
permission to put his two horses in the squatter’s
paddock for the night. “Poor fellow,’’said the
.storekeeper, who was standing by. “Demented ;
bad case.” “Yes, my man, you may put your
horses in any paddock you like, ” said the squatter.
The crank beamed on them both, then uttered
several short yells and jogged along on his broom-
stick. Next morning an irate boundary-rider
demanded from a traveller, camped in the bend,
who gave him permission to put his horses in the
paddock. “The boss did,” said the man ; “ask
him.” “Yes,” said the squatter, an hour later.
“I did give him permission to put his horses in
the paddock. Poor fellow; he is a sad case.”
And the boundary-rider went away and hit him-
self hard against a tree to find out if he was
awake.
“ Werrimee ” : The “ marvellous
power” which Mr. M'Cooey attributes
to the aborigine (23/8/’O2) may be ac-
quired with little training by the
average white man, and is possessed
by nearly all pugilists. They don’t
watch the movements of the
other’s hands —they watch each
other’s eyes. The eye’s glance
anticipates the hand’s action;
and it is this, not the pencil,
that guides the aborigine.
“Form” in cricket is also de-
pendent on the eyes; one doesn’t
I'org ;t how to hit during a long
spell, but the eye “goes out,”
and practice is necessary to
“get it in” again. I’ve played
cricket, and I’ve fought in a
roped ring; and if M‘Cooey will
meet me in The Bulletin’s back-
yard, I’ll convince him that I can
tell where he’s going to hit every
time. [The Bulletin ain’t got no
backyard.—Ed. B.]
“Gouger” writes from White Cliffs
(N.S.W.): —
Just a fact or two. Our much-talked-of famine was
mostly talk. About the only article we were ever short
of was bread, and this only for a week at most. We have
been too much dependent on stores and bakers for our
supplies, delivered daily at the door, and so were caught
napping, but if we had just kept a week’s supply of flour
the necessity of threatening to eommunise would never
have occurred. An abundance of all other requisites was
always procurable—spuds, onions, and vegetables of all
kinds being plentiful. Chaff was certainly high ; only
one store had it, and it made good use of the opportunity
to pile it on. Saturday (Aug. 30) brought a few showers
and gave us about a month’s or five weeks’ supply of
water, so water-carting from Ford’s Tank, 10 miles dis-
tant, is done away with. The yarn of all our young men
having cleared is all bunkum. Opal is still coming in,
and I do not think the amount unearthed lately is any-
thing behind the usual thing. Of course, a good rain
would largely increase our population, and this, of neces-
sity, would mean that a wider field would be prospected,
no doubt just as good as the few miles now known. In
my opinion, there is a great future before the field.
Dear Bulletin. —A frequent cor-
respondent of yours (“ Yarrowee ”) says,
in a recent issue of a Sydney weekly, that
though thoroughly conversant with
mattei's aboriginal, he never heard a
native use the “coo-e-e” call, nor has he
known of any white man who has so
heard it. Well, his experience doesn’t go
for much, seeing that the call is con-
stantly used by the blacks of the Mus-
grave Ranges (S. A.), and of Petermann
and Rawlinson (W.A.). Have heard it
myself repeatedly used by Rawlinson
blacks who knew no word of English. I
was also four months in the Barron
Ranges in ’97, and the natives who were
friendly invariably “cooe’d” when ap-
proaching the camp. Clean across tne
desert westwards, at all the rock-holes
and “soaks,” we heard this cry—
“ Coo-o,” and “Coo-e-e,” being probably
the same in origin, with the termination
accidentally altered. By the way, re the
bell-bird, which has been unmercifully
treated by aboriginaliars in your columns.
I can’t declare whether the male or
female bird performs the bell-imitation,
but I know I’ve been “ had ” frequently
when hunting for camels. The Westralian
bird may be different from the Eastern
variety, but I feel riled to read the sneers
of one of your contributors at “a duffer
who could be misled by the notes.” At
a distance the “clonk,” “clonk” is
exactly like a bell sound, but when near
the bird seems to say, “ Won, won, bur-
illo,” the “bur-illo ” being rather low and
throaty.— S.
* • * *
“E.S.S.” : Be eel and snake
par. (23/8/’O2). All Australian snakes
are swimmers, and any of the various
species may be seen swimming idly
about in rivers or creeks on a hot day. An eel,
which eats almost anything, could easily capture
one. On the other hand, eels frequently leave the
water, particularly at night, and crawl about in
the soft mud on the bank. They have also been
dug out of mud, alive, long after the water lias
dried up. As they are slippery customers for a
snake to deal with, however, I presume it was a
dead one that “Boko’s” snake was in the act of
swallowing. Eels float when they have been dead
a while —wild cats don’t.

On the tame hawk question two correspon-
dents write :
“ S.F.” : In your issue of 6th, a correspondent asks “ if
any reader can say whether hawks kept in gardens will
keep other birds out.” I know of two cases where these
birds were kept, but as soon as the other birds got used
to the hawks, they came in droves just the same.
“E.M.P.”: Re hawks versus parrots. A neighbor
(Western District, Vic.) kept an eaglehawk chained in his
garden, and its performances, on the whole, appeared to
be fairly satisfactory. I obtained smaller hawk about
size of cockatoo whose performances on the whole were
fairly imsatisfactory. Didn’t seem to frighten the pests
worth a cent. Didn’t chain him. He was tame—too
tame—idiotically tame. He moped round the garden for
about a week, and then becoming despondent at his ill-
success, suicided in a tub half full of water.
WHAT HAPPENED TO THE MAN WHO
TRIED TO COMBINE AGRICULTURE
AND POULTRY-FARMING.
Lake George (N.S.W.) has gone dry for
the first time in 52 years. It was dry from 1845
till 1850. It reached its greatest known extent in
1874, and has been fading steadily away ever since.
Therefore it would seem that the present drought
isn’t really responsible, but that Lake George was
a perishing institution in any case. Possibly the
destruction of timber by settlers had reduced the
rainfall just sufficiently to let evaporation beat it
by a short head. Lake George is one of the pro-
posed sites of the Federal capital, but its name,
technically speaking, is now Mud. The Federal
city wants to be located beside permanent water,
and this year’s experience has shown that the
Snowy is almost the only permanent water in
N.S.W.
“THE COOMA SINK.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a grand
Old smooging hand,
A whaler from a foreign strand ;
Along the Murrumbidgee’s flow
He’d humped his blue an age or so.
The city youths were ’mazed to see his beer’s incessant
come and go.
“ You fellers think
That I can drink—
You should have seen ‘ The Oooma Sink.*
His name was Joe, a whaler gay,
What whaled from Gunderoo to Hay.
That man would drain a brewery dry, an’ think he'd had
a droughty day I
"Up somewheres near
To Yass last year,
A train let slip a cask o’ beer ;
The barrel cantered down a bank,
And never started bung or plank.
’Twas eight-foot round the bust, an’ held just half as much
as Hooley’s tank.
‘ ‘ The devil’s luck
Was Joe’s ; he struck
That keg. They chased it on a truck,
And found him hoisting it to drain,
In answer to their words profane,
He dumped it on the fence and said: ‘Hie! mishus, fill
’em up again I ’ ”
SILAS SNELL.
AN AGRICULTURAL VILLANELLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Stir yourselves about
When the season’s good—
Always comes the drought!
When the heavens spout
Every month a flood,
Stir yourselves about 1
Best of seasons out
Fool you never should—
Always comes the drought 1
Sow, when past all doubt
Nature’s in fit mood—
Stir yourselves about, —
Reap and store, to rout
Times that drain your blood—
Always comes the drought l
Almost could you flout
Dry times, if you would
Stir yourselves about! —
Always comes the drought!

They kicked up a beautiful row at Mil-
parinka (N.S.W.) the other day; but because they
don’t keep any lawyers within 200 miles they
dropped it after the first round. (Milparinka is
alongside Tibooburra, which is not so far from
Wompah, which you go through to get to Boulia ;
and if you don’t know where that is you are Lost.)
This was the point: Who owns a meteor ? Accord-
ing to the account one fell near Milparinka lately,
and, it being the only fresh arrival in two years,
the population took a few days off and rode across.
Then they got down on their heels, and after they
had each drawn a sketch-plan in the dust and put
in the pegs the trouble began. Finally the court
decided that it belonged to Billjohn—or Jimbill;
it really doesn’t matter.

“The Bogan": Re drought-straits. I
recently saw a man in Peak Hill (Q.), bringing in
a load of belar leaves, if you can call them leaves
now, to try and keep his horses alive; they feed
belar out of a nose-bag so as not to waste it. But
even belar is getting scarce in this locality. All
the squatters and cookies hereabouts have started
shearing their paddocks. All they want to take
the wool off is a bag and a shearer who knows the
dead sheep odour at long range. There are no
strikes in this part. The smellers-out of dead
sheep have no union. They are not skilled arti-
sans. Any man who has had experience of
snatching cockroaohes off the butter knows all
about the game.
A PICTURE FROM THE WEST.
Dad fells the last bi' of scrub for the last horse.
“ Werrimee ” : Re shearing tallies (8.,
28/12/’01). At Barenya Station (Q.), on 19th
Sept., ’95, eight men averaged 236 each; on the
20th, 26 men averaged 175 each; and next day
J. Power shore 315 mixed sheep in less than eight
hours. The previous best record was Jaeky
Howe’s 321 in Bhr. 40min. He afterwards sur-
passed this at Alice Downs, on the Barcoo, shear-
ing 327 full-fleeced sheep in 7hr. 20min. At
Relalie, Warrego River, 1884, Sid. Ross shore
nine lambs in 9min.; and at Evesham, in 1886,
Jimmy Fisher shore 50 lambs in one run before
breakfast (lhr. 15min.). At Charlotte Plains,
Warrego, in 1885, Alex. Miller shore 4362 sheep
in three weeks three days—an average of 203 per
day throughout the shed. “Long” Maloney
shore 22,000 in one season in S. A., Victoria, and
N.S.W., making a little cheque of £2OO. In 1876
Angus MTnnes shore against Jack Gunn at Par-
ratoo (S.A.) for £5O, when the former shore 180
sandback wethers in one day, and nearly killed
two boundary-riders because they had no more
sheep in. At Fowler’s Bay, in 1874, “Long”'
Maloney shore 11 big wethers in llmin., using a
pair of Ward and Payne’s 38’s. At a shed on the
Paroo, in 1884, seven—namely, Allan M'Callam,
Bamphil (“ The Warbler ”), jack Lynch, Jimmy
Donaldson, “Warrigal Jack” M‘Donald (the
“Barrier Ringer”), “Long Bob” Hobbs, and
Jack Reid—shore 1540 sheep in one day—an
average of 220 per man. At Parelli, on the Lower
Warrego, in 1885, Jack M‘Donald (the “Splinter”),
weighing 6st. 91b., shore 187 full-fleeced wethers
in 7hr. 30min. The above records were mostly
made in Warrego River sheds. If particulars
were forthcoming, we should find that equally
big tallies have been made in the old sheds of
N.S.W.

“ Mao B," > The Faruborough sugar plan-
tation (Yeppeon, Q.) and the surrounding cane-
growing farmers are making a good thing this
year, They are feeding thousands of sheep from
the drought country, Sugar-eane is worth 10s. a
ton delivered at the mill, whereas, cut up into
chaff for fodder, it is worth 30s. a ton, There is
no waste, as the tops are cut up as well as the
eane. There is to be no crushing this year.

“ Kea ” (M.L,) : The two brothers M‘Par-
ritch, after scraping in the one gully for 32 years,
found they had eaeh hoarded just enough to pre-
vent their receiving the old-age pension, Sandy
get his wits to work and formally handed over his
wealth to Jamie; and three months afterwards
Sandy eelleeted the first Govt, siller and divided
it with his brother. For five months, on the first
of each month, did Alexander MTarriteh regu-
larly present himself at the little local post-office,
hut on the sixth occasion he was missing. Jamie
came instead, and showing his brother’s papers
pleaded the latter’s illness. He got the siller,
pocketed it, and then got the local bobby to go
out to their hut. They found Sandy a corpse ; he
had been dead for at least three days. James
admitted that he had a tough job to keep Sandy
alive till the 28th. “He was aye a selfish man,”
said J amie.
According to a Maori correspondent in a
Maori paper, there is quite a track worn by the
ieet of the spirits of departed natives making for
Te Reinga. The way is marked for them by
knotted flax, which never comes unravelled, and
never decays; and they go down into the tumbling
waves over a precipice of rock, swinging them-
selves by a rata vine that grows in a place the like
of which no other rata ever grew in. The nearest
Maori settlement is 15 miles away, and sometimes,
impelled by an awful curiosity, the bolder spirits
have watched and seen the shades go by.

“D.H.R.”: Has any Bulletin reader
ever found pelicans’ eggs ? I have lived for years
where the birds abounded, and have seen plenty
of young ones, but have never found an egg nor
heard of anyone who had.
* * * »
Of 283,777 miles of country held under
lease in the N. Territory at the beginning of last
year 133,344 miles has since been thrown up.
Most of this was never used ; southern speculators
simply got their claws on it in the
hope of an indefinite something
turning up, and, as usual, nothing
did. Even now, of 168,549 miles
leased, only 28,820 miles (or
l-18th of the Territory’s total)
is stocked. Cattle prospects
up there are a lot better than
they were, though ; and if
the stock could be got, there
would be a good deal of
spreading-out. As it is, the
big prices are drawing all the
surplus southward over
36,000 head passed the border
in the year, and there are
buyers hovering round wher-
ever there are fats awaiting
first news of rain on the stock
routes. There are probably
in the Territory now 185,000
cattle and 7500 horses. The
sheep number only 300; the
goats 8000.
In all 491,376 rabbits were despatched
from five stations on the Western line to Sydney
during the last four months —the figures being:
Perth, 260,876 ; Locksley, 52,300 ; Brewongle,
8000 ; Raglan, 5000 ; Tarana, 165,200. Trapping
and despatching of “bunny” is quite an industry
inN.S.W. now. A future heading, “The Great
Australian Rabbit Industry.”

The N.S.W. Art Society Exhibition.
SECOND NOTICE.
A “corrected impression” of the pictures at the
Sydney Art Society’s 1902 Exhibition does not
call for any particular modification of the first
judgment. Sid. Long’s big canvas appeals more
at a second and third glance; some of Lister’s and
Collingridge’s landscapes show a little better ; and
that is all. There are, however, a few pictures
not noticed before in the oil and water-color sec-
tions worthy of mention. A J. Daplyn has some
very meritorious work, the best of which is
“ Where Bracken Grows,” a canvas that is some-
what badly hung. Thea Proctor’s decorative
work is not, on the whole, strikingly good.
“ The Dance ”is probably the best. This artist,
whilst very successful in some black and white
and book-cover studies, spoils her pictures by
strivingafter Pre-Raphaelitism of which sheattains
the form without the spirit. Some promising
work is to be credited to Gertrude Williams, W.
A. Moir, Albert J. Hanson, Arthur J. W. Bur-
gess, J. H. Young and John W. Tristram. “A
Winter Storm,’’ by R,. Sydney Cocks, is a good
landscape showing a rich color scheme in a gloomv
key. C. H. Hunt’s work is of dainty quality, his
paintings on ivory being particularly notable.
**m * •
Two specimens of sculpture are shown. “The
Ruling Powers,” by Nelson Illingworth, is an
example of careful modelling of a plump,
maternal lady in the nude, but hasn’t a spark of
inspiration. “ Bust of Rose Scott ” is, at any rate,
something like the subject, but gives no particular
reason for existence.
* * * «
The Black and White work is, on the whole,
excellent, but shows to a marked degree a lack of
interest on the part of the exhibitors. Most of
them seem to have taken up the duty of “showing
something ” in a perfunctory manner, sending in
anything they had handy, but not troubling about
special effort. The drawings rather show what
the artists are capable of than the best that they
can do. That is a pity, for, in this department of
art at least, Australians have gone a long way,
and a Commonwealth exhibition of black and
white could with confidence challenge comparison
with work in older art centres. Possibly this
is due to the fact that there is a fairly steady
demand for Australian black-and-white work—
locally and abroad. Money is a great stimulus to
art effort. At present the only artistic occupa-
tions on _ this continent which are steadily
remunerative are those of the scene-painter, the
black and white artist, and the teacher in oil and
water colors, though there is no reason why land-
scape painters, if they devote themselves to dis-
tinctively Australian subjects, should not find a
fairly reliable market for their wares with the pro-
motion of an export trade.
* * • *
Of the black and white work Norman Lindsay’s
Bacchic study is about the best, showing fine
technique, and a Rembrandtesque realism of
coarse fleshiness; it is not “pretty,” but it is
strong. “Tim Pageant of the "Flesh,” by
the same artist, is of very much inferior
quality; the technique is still good, but
the idea is poor. A. Dattillo Rubbo’s work in this
section is good—especially a “Sketch in two
crayons.” H. G. Garlick has some very good
studies in animal life. Thea Proctor’s book-plates
are good, and Ethel Roberts has some commend-
able work in the Same line. Other successful
exhibitors are Fred Leist, Clara Speare, G. \Y. L.
Hurst, (Miss) C. Davis, and Edith M. Loudon.
But, as before remarked, the whole section gives
evidence of a perfunctory spirit, as though the
exhibitors were rather too tired to do much. Yet
it might be easily the strongest and most interest-
ing of all. A'suggestion to the Art Society com-
mittee Why not organise an exhibition of
black-and-white work only?

In view of the depressed state of the
Treasury, if for no other motive, there should be
a prompt veto on the latest Owe’Sullivan sug-
gestion to “reclaim” the beach at the Spit,
Middle Harbor (Sydney), and make a park of it.
At present the beach is a stretch of clean, firm
sand, picturesque and useful. The proposal is to
turn it into a horror of the type of “ Lyne Park,’
Rose Bay, a stone-wall-bounded desert —flat,
angular, mechanical, and accursed.
An aged and weary individual, hung around
with rags, presented himself before the Benevo-
lent Trustees in Dunedin (M.L.) recently, and
told a mournful tale of wifely treachery. Years
ago, he said, he was possessed of much property.
He was also possessed of creditors, and in order
to provide for his old age and establish a reputa-
tion for thrift he carefully made over the whole
of his properties in some occult way to his wife,
and the creditors got nothing. His wife, he said,’
had never been the same since, and he had had a
hard life. Finally, in his old age, she had locked
up all the food in the house, and told him to
hustle for himself as regards victuals, though her
affection would not allow her to turn him out.
He explained to the trustees that if they
would arrange for his tucker, his affluent
wife would still let him sleep in the stable. When
last mail left the trustees were doing a big think.

A Vic. country jeweller has fixed up over a
hundred kharki uniform buttons, large and small,
as souvenir hat pins for lady friends of returned
oontingenters. Many of these buttons came from
the kits of men whose bones lie under the veldt.
Ping-pong is said to be on the wane, just as
hostesses felt they had a settled form of enter-
tainment inane enough for most people. Games
are substituted in many houses ; they have the
doubtful advantage of showing-up the guests’ edu-
cation—or the other thing. A popular thing is
to make words out of one word. 1 ‘ Resolution ”
was lately given. At the end of the allotted time,
a guest, who proudly announced that he had 50
words, was crushed to hear that he could have
totalled 375.
Two American girls in London write to that
refuge of the girl-writer, the Daily Mail, of their
shopping experiences. They had intended to
dress “so English ” in tailor-dresses, which they
had been told were “intensely English”; com-
fortable shoes, with nice broad heels ; shirt waists
and high collars. And they knew that the correct
English girl didn’t wear the audacious transparent
yokes. Well, in a whole week of watching they
didn’t see one “tailor” gown, and every second
woman wore French heels. Tnere were no high
collars, but many yokes, of such a transparently-
transparent type as they had never expected to
see even in Paris. English people at their hotel
were shocked that the two girls dined in town
without a chaperone ; and the girls reciprocated
by being shocked to see English women wearing

decollete dresses in restaurants, and in hansoms,
going out to dinner or to the theatre in broad day-
light. Americans call shop-girls “clerks,” and
these two got frightfully mixed at the difference
in the shop language of London and New York
We spent half a day buying a tape-needle because we
didn’t ask for a bodkin. We asked for shirt-waists, and
the clerk said she was sorry but they didn’t keep them,
and when Catherine saw some she begged her pardon and
said, “You mean blouses, I see ! ” We went to a shoe store
and asked for “ Oxford hes.” They are known the length
and breadth of the American continent as the reigning
English style, but in London they are “ the great Ameri-
can shoe.” Then shoes were not shoes unless they were
slippers ; when they were high they were boots. When
we needed a spool of thread we asked in four different
places and they said, “We don’t keep it, thank you!”
We said, “ Thank you,” and they said, “ Thank yoii,” and
I told Catherine it was evidently the clerk’s right to say
it last, and it was no use trying to dispute the point.
My Melbourne correspondent continues :
“ One of the Catuiittee of the Women’s Hospital
thoroughly enjoys her position and is always
spoiling for a fight. She declares she has never
been happier in her life than during the late pro-
longed row. She has a scrap-book into which she
pastes paragraphs from all papers re the misdeeds
of the Catmittee, and is as fond of it and of the
many sneers and jeers it contains as a prima-donna
of her ‘ favorable notices ’ collection. But wait
until some paragraphist blunders on to her bonnet
and says something unpleasant about that; one
par will then be kept out of the collection.
“News comes from Bananaland that Mrs. Tom
Price is booming in Brisbane. She didn’t boom
in Melbourne, but that’s because her soldier spouse
is the sort that kicks over the footstools of
Sassiety and uses swear words as military full
stops. Evidently Mrs. .Price has got in first with
the Brisbaneites, and made headway in her naice,
bland, pleasant, womanish way before her gruff
old Colonel had time to spoil the good impression,
“The professional women of Melb., whose brass
plates show their polished names in Collins-street,
are not of the type whose ‘peevish proud vir-
ginity’ the Bard of Avon complains of. Two
vigilant teeth-extractors of the petticoat fraternity
are flourishing engagement rings this week.
Strange that the two chums, who degreed and
dented side by side, should be f: >ught in marriage
by two medicoes in a sort of sin: iltaneous mission
act.
“If Melb. beauty aspirants get the craze for
Sandow as the Adelaide girls did, face massage
will be dropped and the muscle fad will start.
Nearly all tne vain flutterers of smart circles are
beholden to oils, steams, vapors, and scents,
and the rubbings of the massage siren for their
nice, smooth, fragrant skins. The cultivated com-
plexion is a thing of beauty, and a joy for many
years if properly preserved. But massage has not
been long enough in use to test its real value.
What effect some years of pottering among
the flesh oils, and much play of swift, soft
hands on the face may have, cannot be said
at this stage. All is serene so far. The
advanced advocates of massage have now the
steam and vapor apparatus fixed up in their
boudoirs and self-treatment is cheap and some-
times effective. Toorak boasts an instance of a,
good-looking and loving couple who treat each
other to regular sittings at the massage machine.
What a bond this common complexion cream
must be ! •
“The latest drawing-room toys are scent-birds,
that hang from the curtains and chandeliers,
filling the room with sweet odours. The rose
sachet and the flower urn are abandoned for these
perfume birds of gorgeous plumage.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.
N.Y. Herald records 11,000 cases of
cholera, and 8000 deaths therefrom, in the
Philippines. The likelihood of the United States
being attacked by cholera as a result of the return
of troops is discounted by the excellent health
regulations in force in U.S. ports. But the East
coast of Australia, which is much nearer than
Europe to the Philippines, is seriously threatened.
Brisbane and Sydney are ideal harbors for cholera.
At Poverty Point.
Knight Aston, onetime baritone of the Firm’s Opera
'Co., and of Bracy’s Opera Co., went back to England
•t’other week. Aston sang tenor, not baritone, when he
first came to Australia, 20 years ago. Two or three years
previously, whilst he was cutting a considerable dash as
Grenicheux in the first London production of “ Cloches
de Corneville,” the tenor caught the fancy of the
notorious Mrs. “Gordon Baillie.” She wrote to him (or
got somebody else to pen the billets-doux for her) and
having* duly impressed her mash with her evidences of
wealth—carriage and pair, sealskin jacket, jewels, &c.,
.none of which had been paid for -led him to the altar.
Possibly she had several previous husbands living at the
time. Anyway Aston got out of the predicament and
■cleared to Australia, and was much perturbed when a
few years later, his fair friend came along with a bogus
mission on behalf of the Skye Crofters. What a deli«ht-
ful fraud was Mrs. Gordon Baillie ! She worked the
“ Crofters ’’ ticket as a card of introduction to Melbourne
sassiety for a time. Gov. Loch took her quite seriously
or pretended to, and on one occasion at Melb. Town Hail,'
he nursed her pretty children on his vice-regal knees.’
The origin of those children is a disputed point seemingly!
A later report—by the police—stated that she had
■procured them from an English workhouse, picking them
for their good looks and adopting them as tokens of
respectability for business purposes.
• ® e »r Bolpehn.—'Went to a magic-lantern lecture given
■in Sydney by a New Guinea missionary, the other ni'dit
He was talking about the good work done by the mission-
aries in New Guinea. After showing pictures of niggers
houses, &c., he threw a withered old woman’s counten-
:ance on the canvas, and pointing to it with his stick said
• This is a woman we call Mother Alligator xjvhy we call
her that I shall tell you. When she was a girl she went
■down to the river one day to have a wash, and while
bending over with her hands in the water a large alli-
gator swam softly up, swallowed one of her arms and
pulled her into the water to devour her. The poor girl
screamed for help, but there was no one near, and it
looked as if her day had come ; but with great presence
of mind she turned her arm in the creature’s mouth and
tickled the back of its throat, causing the alligator to
vomit the arm." He looked injured when people snig-
gered.—W. s
At Thursday Island a merry-go-round man is reported
to have lately taken £l5OO in a short season. There
was never any difficulty about getting colored patrons on
the affair—the trouble was in getting them off • and the
proprietor struck only one deadbeat who didn’t want to
pay. At least, he didn’t strike him, for he was a Malay
gentleman with a long knife. So the Asiatic was allowed
to ride free as a press representative.
The Shah of Persia, who has just concluded a visit to
London, has placed it on record that the Empire Music-
Hall ballet is the finest thing in the British dominions A
previous Shah—Nasr-ed-Din, or words to that effect—
when in the Big Smoke, was mainly impressed with
Madame Tussaud’s wax works. It was Nasr-ed-Din who
told the Duchess of Westminster that the fame of her
beauty had penetrated even to the Persian Empire
“ Good gracious ! ” said she to a friend, “ he takes me for
Westminster Abbey ! ’’
George Rignold tells of a comedian, not unknown in
Australia : “ I loaned him a tenner once and played for
his benefit. Missing him after the performance I dropped
him a line, hinting that as the benefit had been a good
one it would be a fair opportunity to square matters ”
The reply I thought good :
‘ Dear Rignold,—Sorry I missed you, as I wished to
touch you for another tenner.—Yours regretfully,
Jingle.’ ’’
Miss Susie Vaughan is much impressed with the singing
of the Australian girls. “The singing of the chorus
girls,” she says, “is simply beautiful. If I wanted good
singers for a chorus I’d send to Australia for every one of
them.”
Just as bad as stage-fright is a sudden blankness of
memory. Nance O’Neil, when playing Fedora, in Mel-
bourne, had her mind become a blank in the middle of a
speech. O’Neil is a plucky woman. When “Lady
Inger” failed in Melbourne she fought a hostile audience
for two-hours-and-a-half—fought herself to a standstill, so
that when the curtain fell, O’Neil fell, too, and had to be
carried off the stage to her dressing-room. But when her
memory left her in “Fedora,” Nance lost all her faculties
temporarily. She couldn't even hear the prompter but
sat looking before her, with eyes growing - bigger and
bigger, with hysterical terror. Harry Plimmer, who was
on with her, saved the situation by coming quite close to
her, grasping her wrist, and telling her the lines She
repeated them after him, and as she repeated them her
mind returned, and she finished them splendidly' The
moment the curtain was down she seized Plimmer’s’ hands
in hers : “ God bless you, God bless you ! If I live to be a
hundred I shall always be grateful.”
A civil engineer in Melbourne recollects, in a thought-
ful, dispassionate, grandfatherly way, a little incident in
the early career of a popular and handsome actress. The
O.E. was engaged on a Maoriland railway-line, and visited
the nearest township so often that the best bedroom in
the best pub. was practically his own. Arriving at about
seven one evening, and leaving his horse to the stable-
hand he bounded upstairs, plunged into his room, and—
then dived out, and precipitated himself downstairs
again. There the landlord met him and explained how
sorry he was, but the company having arrived he
had been practically compelled to put Miss and the
two Misses into room 5. He hoped nothing alarming
had occurred. The O.E. said it was evidently quite a
work of skill getting a plump pink actress into a pair of
very tight tights, but still nothing alarming had hap-
pened.
The most tireless theatre-goer in one Austral
metropolis is a local artist who sings for his
bare life very occasionally. He has the run of
one theatre, and haunts the place as regu-
larly as the doorkeeper, turning up
night after night, and always occupying
a front-row circle seat as near the stage
as possible. On a recent night much
commotion was occasioned “behind"
when it was found that the old-estab-.
lished deadhead w'as not in the house.
During the first interval several mem-
bers of the co. knocked at the door of
the manager’s office, and, without entering,
made a formal demonstration of sympathy
in a little speech, in which the deputation
hoped that the manager would not allow
Mr. Blank’s absence to depress him or to
lead him to conclude that he was losing his
hold on the theatre-going public. “ There, there ;
that’s all right, boys,” said the manager ; “ Blank’s in
my office here, having a glass of wine ! ” Then the
deputation caught sight of Blank’s blank face and left.

The mechanism is solely take.; from the ’ed of Professor
Edison, who spent about 20 years in perfecting this won-
derful ram avis. Perhaps you would like to learn
whence we got the name ‘ phonygraft’—well I’ll tell you

and in the second ‘ graft,’ and their derivations is as
follows: ‘Phony,” Latin, ‘phono,’ to speak; ‘graft,’

phonygraft means ‘ to speak without working.’”
As showing the difference in national tastes re tremolo

have you a vibrato ?" asked the manager. “ oh, yees ”
said the Frenchman, “ I have made special study of it for
ze last two year.” He thought that the art of “ shaking
on every note” was appreciated everywhere. Many
French teachers insist upon the vibrato; others like
Marchesi, try to check the habit, and certainly don’t
te a chifc. But; it seems to be more or leas characteristic
of all French singers.
