characters 111 ' Part,,e,, «” atld a whole lot of other
Waif ” makes some amazing remarks to The Bulletin
on the dramatist who had the misfortune to be born
Jones “I first met Henry Arthur Jones in 1877. Ha
was a draper's bagman, inoffensive, but that he talked
talked Jones. It is the knell of our generation
that that man has been one of our chief dramatists this
years. I saw his first piece played at the Court
Theatre, London— ‘ Uncle Dick’s Darling - .’ Steady, honest,
roast-beef sort of stuff without a touch to lift it above
the level of third-class fluff. He was the son of a Bucks
farmer, a yokel by nature, a commercial by force of
gravity, and a writer of plays by license of Bedlam. . . .
successful ho was, for I have heard him say he would
never part with a play for less than five figures, and never
W c <4 ni<^ Q than one a year. That was within six years
or The Silver King.’ Henry Herman, the joint author of
that nug-hty melodrama, did the world the wrong of
] l iy i e,1 t I,| g both Jones and Wilson Barrett. But for
The Silver King’ probably neither of them
would have been heard of. Jones tried to repeat
the triumph on his own with ‘A Noble. Vagabond’
but it fell fiat. But he was a true commercial and he hit
on the industrial problem play in ‘The Ironmaster,’ and
from that forward, for fifteen years, he has played on the
second-class problem string in divers tones and with vary-
ing-fortune.” The first objection to “ Waif’s ” scornful
estimate of Jones is that he doesn’t remember him start-
"s hfe as a dramatic author with “ Uncle Dick’s
Darling ”at the Court Theatre. That harmless play—the
pioneer of “dream ” drama in England—was produced at
the Gaiety, and Jones didn’t write it. The late H. J.
Byron wrote it for J. L. Toole. Also Jones didn’t invent
Wilson Barrett, for previous to “ The Silver King ” the
said W.B. had made a fortune out of Sims’ “Lights o’
London.” Moreover Jones didn’t write “The Iron-
master,” which is a translation of a fine French play by
Ohnet. But Jones did hit the industrial problem with
“ The Middleman,” and his story of Cyrus Blenkarn has
probably done something towards causing patient guile-
less inventors to buck up against the capitalist who
fattens on their genius. For the rest, Jones has written
at least one brilliant play in addition to the interesting
“ Bauble Shop," “ Case of Rebellious Susan,” &c. The
author of “The Liars” is a lot in front of the average
little-minded literary person not born a yokel.
\ Paradis ” : Met the ballet-girl of forty to-daj. An
estimable lady, well-known on the stages of Melbourne
and Sydney 20 years ago and less. She says life is not
worth living after 25. Balzac’s supremely fascinating
woman of 30 cannot dodge on the Musgrove or William-
son boards. Managers are not cruel; it’s the public. The
stalls have “ a heye like a heagle.” They have an eye that
divines to bone and marrow, and the back row has to be
turned inside-out every minute-and-a-half. The ballet
must be four-square, like the palace in the “ Book of
Revelation,” and with four fronts. Flesh must be flung
as fresh as strawberries and cream. The girl of forty
would pay a guinea a night to go on, if she were able.

“ Vox ”: A late contributor to a Melb. daily insists that
the higher education and long scientific training are
essential to the vocalist who would be a successful public
performer. Yet one very famous Italian tenor of the
50’s and 60’s, could not read or write. Nicolini, Patti’s
second husband, was of peasant birth, and so ignorant
that when he died he bequeathed the castle of Craig-y-nos
to his relations, thinking that his wife’s property belonged
to him, as it would have done under Italian law.
Catherine Hayes, the sweet warbler who visited Australia
in ’56, was a boat-rowing girl on the lakes of Kiilarney,
picked up by a passing musician attracted by her native
wood-notes wild. And did not Carrie Swain, who, when
wholly ignorant of the science of sounds, charmed by
trilling like a bird on the bough from the fullness of her
heart, disappoint everybody after she had passed through
the teacher’s mill ? The artificially-produced, machine-
made singer has not got all the points. There is over-
training in vocalism as in athletics.
EVIDENTLY—A FACT.
“ Busker”; Any Thespian pro. remember Charley
Miran ? When I first came in touch with the profession
in Australia he was not there, but was referred to on
every point of independence and etiquette. He be-
came a legendary character, like Lambescasse on the
Parisian stage, the ideal of one who was not a manager's
man. If he didn’t like anything, he would walk
straight out of the stage door. He was the cham-
pion swallower of parts. If an actor witli a part of
forty sides got indisposed, Charley would “wing” the
same, even if he had never seen it. I have heard a stiff
Poverty Point argument between a pro. who said Charley
was always generous with the wine, and another who
maintained he was a teetotaller. I think I saw him act
once. Certainly he is on old bills of the Sydney, Melb.
Geelong, and Ballarat playhouses.
Rumored in London that “ Monna Vanna,” Maeter-
linck’s great and essentially pure play, was banned by the
Censor of Plays in response to a hint from the Queen—
the Queen who went to see “ Sapho ” and “ Zaza ” !
“ Haresfoot” : An honest little book, just issued in Mel-
bourne, is on Ventriloquism, by R. Hughes. Cheap and
rough, but with a large amount of matter. The author,
speaking from practical experience, declares that
ventriloquism is a purely artificial product, the tricks
being, more or less, within the reach of any tractable
voice. Many anecdotes, are told. G'anthony was giving
an open-air entertainment at Raglan Castle, and sent up
a query, “What are you doing up there?” To his
amazement, a boy replied, With au apology, and the audi-
ence applauded this vociferously. Another time he got
the credit for a boy who shouted through the window,
“ What a rotten show 1 ” Hughes demonstrates how the
Egyptian, Persian, and Greek priests used to gull the
people with ventriloquism.

SYDNEY.
POLITICAL
POINTS
Inmates of the State Benevolent Asylums
of N.S.W. are now petitioning for the franchise.
Why is it that Sydney D.T. suddenly finds
out now that N.S.W. railways are being badly
mismanaged ?
Labor unions should take a lesson from the
fact that it is the anti-Labor papers which * ‘ rub
it in ” when the workings of the Arbitration Act
seem to be against the interests of Labor, and
strive to promote discontent with that valuable
piece of legislation.
The wisest and shortest speech ever made
by Premier See was when he was asked in N.S.W.
Assembly by Nielsen if he would join in a move-
ment to secede from the Commonwealth, and
replied flatly “No.” That was all he said, but
he looked several things.
A deputation of woman's franchisers inter-
viewed Premier Philp lately, and put their views
very clearly before him. “ Look at the finances of
the country, Mr. Philp,” said one indignant
dame, “and tell us if half-a-dozen old women
couldn't manage things better.”
The House of Reps, has decided, in an
abstract way, that the Commonwealth should take
over the Northern Territory. It is to be hoped
that it will make all possible haste and take it
over—assets, liabilities, and all. Even the estab-
lishment of a Federal capital isn’t half so urgent,
now that Jenkins Iscariot is seeking to job the
Territory away to his friend Cohen.
Victorian Premier Irvine, when this issue
went to press, was still assuring the country that
if he comes back politically alive from the election
his first move will be to re-enact the Factories
Act. Well, the country had Irvine’s word of
honor a very short time ago that he wouldn’t let
the Act lapse, and he let it lapse. What is his
new promise worth —one straw or two ?
The wholly patriotic aims and motives of
the secessionist “ party ” in N.S.W. are well illus-
trated by a D.T. correspondent of Sept. 10th,
who complains that he lost one day’s services of
his carter through a Customs-house block, and signs
himself “a Separationist now.” It would be a
unique revolution that started over ten shillings’
worth of a vanman’s time lost by the leading
patriot.
“ Veteran’s” theory :
That row at Newcastle (Natal), of which so many vary-
ing and hopelessly inconsistent accounts have been given,
perhaps shows how it came about that the authorities
over there got into such a frantic hurry to clear all the
Australian and M.L. contingents out of the country. The
men, in fact, had got quite beyond control, and their
officers, who had never had them well in hand, dropped
all pretence of management, and went to live in hotels
when the fighting ended.
The absentee South Australian Company,
which has large vested interests at Port Adelaide,
including much wharfage property, is doing its
best to block the construction, authorised by Par-
liament, of another harbor for deep sea ships at
the entrance of the Port stream. Opposition
leader Darling, who is more interested in Port
Pirie than in the harbor of Adelaide, is helping
the absentee corporation.
Some Brisbane importers are fearfully
wroth because Kingston puts the law in motion
against Customs dodgers. Hitherto when a
“mistake” came to light there was always back-
stairs influence, and now that the Federal Minister
has locked up the little back-door, and taken away
the steps, and used the key as a tonic, the gentle-
man who undervalued his imports has to come in
with the crowd at the front entrance.
The Philp Govt., in order to collect its
poll tax, will have eventually to appoint an army
of deputy tax-gatherers, to pad the hoof along the
Western tracks from waterhole to waterhole in
order to levy the annual 10s. from every Western
dead-beat. Said tax-gatherers must be empowered
to impound pannikins, billies, swags and similar
sundries in the great hope of thereby extinguish-
ing Bob Philp’s last half-million deficit.
To the crusted Q. Tory it must be gall and
wormwood to realise that his earthly god, the
Boodlewraith, has lately been kept in power by
the despised Labor Party. The two last divisions
(moved by disappointed one-time supporters)
against Philp and Co. were defeated owing to the
Laborites refusing to be the catspaw. Times have
changed since the haughty Q. politician refused
to hang his hat on the same rack with “honest
Tom ” Glassey’s soft felt.
“Veteran” writes :
Must have made Australia’s amateur naval men squirm
when Long Lyne’s “ Board ” condemned, as antiquated
and useless, the rattletrap steam-launches they have been
used to call “ torpedo-boats.” Lyne, however, had not
the courage to go the whole hog and get real experts’
opinions about the value of the entire land and sea-
defence plant. If he had, he would probably have been
mightily astonished to learn that the whole of the big
guns at Port Philip, Port Jackson and elsewhere are quite
obsolete, and only fit for the scrap-heap. In fact, they
were already obsolescent when first mounted, and some
of them —instance the 25-ton guns on St. George’s
Heights, Sydney—are of a 40-year-old pattern ! The time
spent in drilling men at these antiques is simply wasted,
as it is very doubtful if even the biggest of them could
pierce a Krupp armor-plate at 2000yds. Then there are
on hand large numbers of obsolete rifles, which are not
worth storage ; and, in fact, when all the obsolete and
defective plant of the Commonwealth Defence Depart-
ment has been gob rid of, there won’t be much left; but
there will be a capital excuse for getting rid of the equally
useless staff, and, with it, the pernicious Barracks Push.
Reported that Captain Miles, of Strahan
Marine Board fame, is preparing to run for the
Queenstown (Tas.) seat at next general election.
Miles will have the support of most mine-mana-
gers and owners on the West Coast, and even if
opposed by a Labor man, will probably win.
The miners thereabouts are not too solid politi-
cally yet, and, anyhow, Miles can talk Labor
legislation with the best of them. The return of
Miles to Parliament would be a public calamity,
but the general feeling in Tassy is that a calamity
more or less doesn’t matter much.
N.S.W. Works Minister Owe’Sullivan
wandered out to Alexandria, a rather dingy
Sydney suburb, the other day, and was promptly
seized upon by an alderman, who demanded that
the State should attend to the improvement of
O’Riordan-street, it being in a “disgraceful condi-
tion.” And, instead of replying that only a dis-
graceful set of aldermen could possibly leave it in
that condition, and promising to introduce a Bill
under which these aldermen would be fined £lO
if they didn’t wake up, the man with the Owe
promised to seriously consider the matter.
By reading the Freetrade daily papers on
the Federal tariff result one gleans two facts:
(1). That owing to the patriotic efforts of the Free-
traders in the Federal Parliament the tariff emerges from
debate an absolutely new instrument of taxation. Its
originators do not recognise it, and the Protectionists
now regard it as a betrayal of their cause.
_ (2). That the tariff is now a gross instrument of Protec-
tionist tyranny, and will, at the first appeal to the people,
be decisively flouted, and that it is going to lead to the
secession of N.S.W. at an early date.
The Freetrade party has been so well trained in
the school of Yes-No that it finds no difficulty in
believing both these “facts.”
The cry of the Cobdenites, that N.S.W.
is made the victim of the other States through the
neglect or malignance of the Federal Parliament,
gets a humorous touch from the fact that it is the
N.S.W. Cobdenites who are the champion absen-
tees from the House of Representatives. Only
three members of that body have been absent from
more than 100 sittings this session—Bruce Smith
(133 absences), F. E. M ‘Lean (128 absences) and
G. H. Reid (126 absences) —all N.S.W. Free-
traders. The next in order of absenteeism are
Sawers, Ewing, Cruickshank and Spence (all of
N.S.W.), with absences ranging in number from
85 down to 80. The best record for attendance by
a N.S.W. man (excepting Mr. Chanter, who holds
an official post) is that of J. C. Watson, the Labor
Party leader. All these N.S.W. Freetraders seem
quite assured that they can safely leave N.S.W.
interests in the hands of the members from other
States.

Victoria has paid £5,765,063 in pensions
and gratuities since 1851 ; and, though its fatuous
civil service pension law has long since been
repealed, as far as new members of the service are
concerned, the pension bill last year reached
£344,701. No wonder Treasurer Shiels lies awake
at nights and says things. That one piece of mis-
directed foolishness, done by. the old Tory party
many years ago, costs Victoria nearly four times
as much per inhabitant as Germany pays for it 3
navy.
Brisbane Courier item :
In connection with the proposal to construct a tram-
line to Toowong, which has been applied for by the Tram-
ways Company, an engineer of the Railway Department
has made an inspection of the locality to see what
practicable routes exist. The first proposal was a line
parallel with the railway line.
Consider the hidden magnificence of that item.
The State railway runs to Toowong, and the
privately-owned Tramway Co. wants to build a
line there and take the business. So the State
railway department lends an officer to assist its
business rivals the private co., in finding out the
easiest route.
Postmaster-General Drake has been greatly
agitated of late. It seems that the Department s
obligations towards its employees have not ex-
tended to trousers, and strenuous efforts have
been made to induce Government to throw
breeches in. The Age says that the disclosure
of the discrepancy in the postal uniforms
made a deep impression on the House, and the
P.M.G. has as good as promised to see that the
employees do not go without their pants for the
future. Why thus destroy the mirth of nations .
In a current Melbourne melodrama all the laughter
arises out of a man having lost his pants.
An exceedingly foolish human specimen
(Hall, of Waratah)'has proposed, in Tas. Parlia-
ment, that the Flyspeck island should withdraw
from the Commonwealth “to avoid crushing
taxation.” And the Federal Parliament, by
abolishing inter-State duties, and imposing a
more rational tariff, has really _ reduced las-
mania’s taxation. Even before its taxes were
reduced its people paid less than the English
rate of taxation—though they whined about it a
great deal more. Hall, of Waratah, by the way,
wants Tasmania to leave the Commonwealth,
yet wants the Commonwealth to promise that it
won’t tax Tasmanian goods—which is a hopeful
outlook.
Premier Irvine goes to the people of
Victoria as the man who has destroyed the Fac-
tories Act, and who isn’t going to impose a
general Land-Tax. His agricultural and cow-
spanking constituents have always been opposed
to Minimum Wage and Land-Tax, therefore,
his chance looks good in the purely cow-
spanking districts. But unless _ Irvine a.nd
his financial streak-of-misery, Shiels, develop
more brilliant ideas about how to make good
the shortage, the general land-tax will come
by sheer force of circumstances. Irvine has,
doubtless, all the bad intentions that his party is
so glad to hear about, but many a man is full of
bad intentions, and doesn’t know how to carry
them out.
There are some people in Maoriland who
are far from satisfied with the borrowing policy of
the smug and oily Ward, and they are endeavoring
to induce Robert Stout to resign the Chief Justice-
ship and lead a new self-reliant party, that is
determined to make some effort to prevent Maori-
land from mortgaging away the results of 12 years
Socialistic legislation. There is not much chance
of Stout resigning his lucrative billet for the pre-
carious honors and remuneration of a reform
leader, but the need for that leader is very real.
AND IT IS LUCKY THEY ARE.
HE: “ What would your mother say if she saw
you now?”
SHE: “ Oh, she’d say, 'Girls are very different
from what they were in my young days.’”
David Syme’s democratic principles—con-
stitutional reform, factory legislation and such—
don’t amount to much compared with his regard for
his fad of the moment—retrenchment. There is
no hope of any progressive measures from the Tory
crowd, yet already the Age is barracking for
Irvine, Shiels and Bent. Victoria, which is al-
ready a very cheaply-governed country, wants a
general land-tax much more than it wants re-
trenchment, and Syme, as a big landlord of great
tonnage probably knows that, and that is very
likely why his paper is howling in the same riot
along with Kyabram and co.
Maoriland House of Representatives has
again killed the Elective Executive Bill, on the-
ground that it would destroy Parliament’s control
over the actions of the Government, and lead to
the abolition of party government. The latter,
at all events, is a thing that is badly required.
Members are continually forced to choose be-
tween serving the party and serving what they
believe to be the best interests of the country, and,
so far, the country always seems to lose the toss.
The Bulletin has often pictured a candid member
“ skying a copper ” all by himself just before a
division, and explaining, when sternly called to
order by the Speaker, that he was deciding
whether to sell his country or his party, and the
blessed coin had come down tails.
The gilded R.A.A. lias had its pay fixed
at —for 2nd lieutenants and lieutenants, from £2OO
to £3OO a year ; and for captains, £325 to £4OO.
Now, what is the Commonwealth to get for these
salaries? A cadet draftsman in N.S.W. Lands
Department has to pass a severe mathematical
examination, be able to solve any triangle, and
draw both neatly and correctly—and he begins at
£75 a year. An English artillery subaltern has to
pass a still harder examination before he enters as
a cadet at Woolwich, and to stay there cramming
for two years. Then if he passes the final exam,
and is high enough on the list to win a commis-
sion, he gets ss. 7d. a day. Yet the £2OO-a-year
R. A. A. subaltern has only to pass an exam, that
should be easy for a 3rd-elass public-school boy.
“ T.D.” in reply :
“ Demos ” (Bulletin, Aug. 30th) labors under a pretty
general delusion when he says that a return of the sums
of money paid in Melbourne State schools for school
requisites would open the eyes of many who “ cherish de-
lusions about the freeness of the system of free educa-
tion.” It may astonish “ Demos,” but the money paid
for books and stationery to the Education Department
by parents throughout Victoria last year did not average
£5O. Surely Govt, is not expected to supply all State
school children with books and such articles merely
because less than one per cent, of their number can’t
actually buy them. Every teacher has an allowance
made by the Department for the supply of slate pencils,
and similar materials. Free and compulsory education is
a noble institution, but there is too much whining because
the Govt, stops spoon-feeding at a certain point, and too
little readiness to make up for the spoon-feeding by sub-
mitting to legitimate taxation instead of cutting into the
salaries of men earning 50s. a week.
The Irvine crowd, taunted with doing
nothing to save the Factories Act at the critical
juncture, feebly retorts that the chief opponent of
the continuation was Peacock’s whilom colleague,
Agar Wynne. But, after all, Wynne only played
the habitual game of the whole Rich Landlord
crowd, and is no more bitter against the Factories
Act than J. M. Davies, Robert Reid, and Sachse
were up to the very day they became Ministers
along with Irvine, and will be again the very day
they cease to be paid by a suffering community
for temporarily pretending to be Democrats. The
same remarks apply to those sturdy opponents of
all democratic legislation, Premier Irvine and
Lands Minister M'Kenzie, who never lost an
opportunity of denouncing the Factories Act
until the chance of getting good billets as Minis-
ters suddenly converted them into hypocritical
supporters of the measure.
Critchett Walker, at the Sydney Newspaper
Train Enquiry, was like another Witch of Endor,.
and Mr. Sam. Cook jumped worse than Saul.
“ We didn’t expect that ” is the beginning and end 1 ,
of “interested” comment on the matter. For
Critchett Walker evoked from the grave railway-
manager Goodchap and Honest John Sutherland
and Henry Parkes, and, seemingly, the whole
affair stands : Goodchap was willing to carry the
newspapers by special train for a small subsidy
from the post-office; Sutherland, the Minister,
eager; Parkes would let it go to the Cabinet. But
it never did go to the Cabinet. And so the Syd-
ney morning papers obtained, _ in an accidental
way, railway services which their brothers of Mel-
bourne would gladly purchase for much money.
How much money would the Akgus and Age pay
for a service to land their daily issues in Ballarat
and Bendigo at 6 in the morning ?

Read what Mr. Davis writes.
_ Perth, Jane 5, 1900.
Mr. S. A. PALMER.
Dear Sir, —I have been invalided for about 2 years and
3 months. The doctors treated me for Inflammation of the
Lungs, Bronchitis, and Pneumonia, but failed to cure me.
I nsed to suffer with very severe pains through my back
and chest, and had a very nasty cough. I was in the
Perth Hospital three weeks, but the doctor told me he
could do no more for me, and that I was as well as he
could make me, I was laid aside for 12 weeks at Fremantle.
The landlady of the Club Hotel advised me to try
VITADATIO, which I have done, and after taking four
bottles lam now in splendid health. I will be pleased to
answer any inquiries that any sufferer may require in the
hope that my humble testimony may be the means of
leading others to health.
Yours respectfully,
(Signed) FREDERICK DAVIS, Bayswater.
Witness: W. J. Fist, Perth.
For further particulars,
S. A. PALMER,

The Philp Govt. is now known as the Poll-tax
Govt, it has succeeded in passing a measure
almost unparalleled m Australian legislative
records. Every male adult in Queensland (with
under £100) has now to pay an income-tax
or 10s. In the Q. backblocks at present you can
meet gaunt, hungry men in scores who haven’t
earned £2O in the last 12 months—and these un-
fortunate devils are all expected to pay income-
tax. in -N.Q. there are mining townships where
you pay 17s. 6d. for 501 b. of flour; where butter
(when procurable) costs you about 3s. a lb.; where
tresh meat is unobtainable—even in the form of
goat-flesh, and where hardly a man averages £BO
per annum, and the income-tax man will be there
also, if he doesn’t die of thirst getting there. The
of £52 6m re^use< l even to make an exemption
Died at Wangaratta (Vic.), some weeks

Snazzy was having a bad time. He and his
daughter (who accompanied her dad in the songs)
were just recovering from influenza, the limelight
apparatus went wrong and was omitted from the
night s programme, and only that there was a
good house to disappoint, Snazzy would have gone
to bed in disgust. Under these adverse circum-
stances he put his fruity basso into the show like
a man, and told his yarns, and got through the
trouble successfully. Next morning someone
notified Snazzy, as he was doing the local block
m his white canvas shoes, that Snelling, the oracle
was desirous of saying what he thought about the
show. You wanted to see me, sir?” queried
Snazzy, twirling a walking-stick menacingly.

New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency Co.
Ltd., reports for week ended Sept. 13th that the wool
market has been particularly quiet. Local sales will be
resumed on the lBth, but quantity offering is painfully
small, and few parcels of the new clip are expected
London sales open on the 16th with 192,000 bales offering.'
Sheepskin supplies limited and not improved in quality.
Prices ruled firm, with occasional advances for choice
lines. Kangaroo and swamp wallabies sold at last week’s
rates Scrub wallabies not much asked for. Bears and
rabbits very firm. Superior grey and red ’possums
decidedly stronger, and prices kept up to last week’s
level. Hides are still very inferior in quality, although
good butchered ones show an upward tendency. Tallow
maintained last week’s prices with difficulty, although
supplies were more limited. Hide pieces, sinews and
horns in good demand. Cowhair just firm and horsehair
decidedly weaker. The optimistic ideas of last month
with regard to coming crops of breadstuffs have been
somewhat dispelled by continued dry weather and
quotations, which are purely nominal, may be given as
4s. lid. per bushel for milling purposes.*

. -A new find has been made on the Great Over-
,Bulon" (W.A.), and a test parcel of 30 tons has
yielded 210 oz. This brings the total gold won up to
11,7850 z. from 1408 tons. All this has gone to the
working man, as Government refused to grant leases and
cut up the ground into small areas. For a long time this
was looked upon as a lode, but it is now admitted to be a
lead.
* * * *
Ops- working round about Great Fingall Consols
(w.A.) seem to be in a bad way. South Fingall bored to
2000 ft. without encountering anything of value. West
Fingall got three reefs, but, besides being small, the
grade is low, so that outlook is not too rosy. At recent
London meeting the usual gaudy cable was reeled off to
shareholders to tide directors over another year. Since
then co. has gone in for exemption to recover breath for
fresh efforts.
* * * *
The ill luck of Hannan’s Proprietary Develop-
ment Go. (W.A.) is unbounded ! The Golden Pike and
Lake View East have just taken a spell of exemption after
spending thousands on chasing a will-o’-the-wisp lode.
The Queen of the West is in hot pursuit of the Ironsides
North lode at 500 ft., but why, nobody can say, as the lode
has produced no gold so far. Another of its progeny,
the Oroya East, is on its wav down to 700 ft. to try and
find the Brown Hill Extended lode, which seems to an
unprejudiced observer the height of folly as the lode has
been a duffer so far.
• * * »
The Davyhurst district (W.A.) is looking up
again. The Westralia Waihi and the Golden Pole have
installed rock drills and are busy sinking for water. The
latter is held by a Kalgoorlie syndicate, and shares,
which recently changed hands at £3, are now held for £5.
The Eileen, the rejected of Boulder Hamilton, is also
showing well.
* » • *
Associated Northern (W.A.) continues shipping
rich sulpho-telluride ore to Dapto, and it is probable that
when the returns come in a div. will be declared. A
diamond-drill bore from the 550 ft. level has been put
down 650 ft., but results have not been reported. “No
news is good news,” but in this case, probably, the
reverse holds good.
* • • •
Tom Hewitson, the old Bendigo and Clunes
(Vic.) mining manager who had the Ivanhoe and now the
Associated (Kal.), is to be relieved of the duties, at his
own request, in October. He’s quite tired of trying to
please John Bull and his crowd in London. Gleisberg,
the new man, has arrived on the mine ; he is of the high-
collar and patent-leather-boots brigade.
• * • *
Hercules silver-lead-copper-zinc mine (Tas.),
shipped £14,671 worth of ore during past half-year, and
made a profit on working account of £3116. The profit
and loss account to date shows a credit of £13,873, which,
on a called-up capital of £3750, is a good record. The
gossan ore averages about lldwts. of gold, 19oz. of silver,
and from 4 to 13 per cent, of lead, while the sulphide
returns Bdwts. of gold, 20oz, of silver, and 7 per cent, of
lead. The coppery ore is not shipped. Reports show
that there are immense bodies of ore available, and the
question arises—why is the co. content to continue in its
present tinpot way, making larger profits for smelting cos.
than it does for itself? Why not call up some more
capital to make provision for treatment of ore on a large
scale on the mine ?
• * • •
Editor Bulletin.— Re Briseis mine (Tas.): In
your issue 30/8/’O2 it is stated that the Ringarooma race
delivers to Briseis 810,000 gallons water daily, and a big
output shortly is “confidently anticipated.” In reply,
the race is delivering over 900,000 gallons “ hourly ” in-
stead of daily. In your same issue reference is made
to the race estimate. The original estimate was for a
race barely half the dimensions of the constructed one
and exclusive of the large syphons and the 10-mile exten-
sion to Mt. Maurice, hence the difference in estimation.
During the last three years I have championed the cause
of this much-abused mine against many inaccurate and
misleading statements, a few of which are : That the tin
drifts were considerably over-rated by the various
engineer’s reports ; that there was not one-third of the
water available in the Ringarooma as guaranteed the
company by its experts ; that the Ringarooma silting up
would prove to be the fly in the ointment; that no dumping-
ground was available for overburden, etc., etc. Beyond
a shadow of doubt all these asserted difficulties are
skeletons of the past. One small elevator (6in.) working
on intermediate drifts for July raised about 32 tons tin ;
for August, 41j tons ; and estimated output for present
month is from 45 to 50 tons. If one face will yield this
output monthly it is fair to assume that several faces
operating on similar or better drifts will yield a big out-
put, and fully bear out the reports of various engineers.
The Ringarooma water supply is somewhat in excess of
the report of the co.’s experts. There is more dumping-
ground available than will be required. The Ringarooma
River, as the main water channel, is in excellent con-
dition, the Briseis mine not delivering a particle of over-
burden (or, for that matter, underburden either) into it.
The co. is practically independent of the river except as a
drainage race. I, as a practical miner and mine owner,
living over 25 years in its immediate vicinity have had the
best possible opportunity of judging the surroundings and
possibilities of the Briseis mine. The policy of the present
management is to carry the stripping sufficiently forward
to permit tin-producing operations on a large scale to be
carried on without cessation. It is roughly estimated
this will occupy another four to six months. The two
nozzles operating on Briseis Hill overburden are doing
wonderful execution. Derby and Cascade stripping faces
are also being well advanced. In the meantime, the 6in.
elevator will be responsible for the output of tin, which I
estimate will be between 40 and 50 tons monthly.—J H
Bryant.
The Great Australian Central Transvaal Co.
has received advice from its South African agent that
two of the reefs inspected have proved unpayable.”
This is an injustice to local industry. No occasion to
send men to Africa to find unpai'able reefs. Better
squander the money here. Circulation of it locally might
do some good.
* * * »
The condition of things in London is reported
to be deplorable from a mining point of view. The S.
African slump continues, and there has been no mining
promotion during the last six months, although some
hundreds of S’African mining schemes were all in readi-
ness to come out as soon as the war was over. Bitter and
great has been the disappointment. Shares, at latest
advices, were 50 per cent, lower than they were
in the height of the boom, and considerably lower
than they were when the outlook seemed very blue
for the British army. There are no prospects of a
boom now in sight. One of the big managers ex-
plains the real cause of the present state of things.
The mines are not what they have been cracked up
to be. In most cases the market price was more
than the gold in the mine, making a fair allowance
for working expenses. The average is very low grade,
and cheap working by black labor has been‘the secret of
regular and big divs., but the position has changed.
Only about one-tenth of the required number of niggers
have so far expressed their willingness to resume work.
More than that, it is not possible to dump new niggers
into the mines to work as miners; they have to be trained
like everybody else. As a rule, after working several
years as miners, they got enough money to buy wives and
to live by their work, but others were then being trained,
first on the surface, then below carrying tools, timber,
shovelling and trucking, &c., and then in the stopes until
they became first-class miners who worked for 18s. a
week. For the last three years this training has stopped
entirely, and the number of miners wanted are not avail-
able—especially at the 6s. a week which the co’s. are now
trying to fix as the nigger-wage. It is necessary to have
miners to train new niggers. White labor is offered ss. a
day and rations. What sort of white mining labor could
be got on the Rand at ss. a day ? And if they paid good
miners 10s. a day divs. would drop heavily.
(Continued on page 23.)
Recent arrivals from South Africa state
that no Australian ha*> any idea of the terrible
condition of that country at the present time.
The thousands of irregulars employed in the war
have been thrown out of employment, and, as a
large proportion are local men, they are simply
cast on their own resources, without work and—
when they have gambled away their lump of pay
—without cash. The result is that every large
town is a hotbed of crime. Men who, for three
years, have been accustomed to take life and any-
thing else. they, can get, are not likely to
change their habits merely through the signing
of a. declaration of peace and, generally
speaking, nothing is safe in South Africa
that isn’t nailed down. Dozens of serious
robberies occur daily in all the big cities;
mysterious deaths by violence are happening
everywhere, and every main road is patrolled by
footpads. The police, never very effective under
the comparatively easy conditions that prevailed
before the war, are completely paralysed, and the
special police, manufactured out of Imperial
soldiers, spend their time drinking and brawling
with the colonials or partaking in the proceeds of
robberies. The discipline that was enforced dur-
ing the war has gone, and officers are gleefully
maltreated by discharged and unidentifiable
privates. The position of women in South Africa
to-day is best unthought of. The place is a
veritable hell.
Dear Bulletin. —Advice is only useful
when practicable. The friendly critic in your
last issue (13/9/’O2) overlooks two facts : (1) That
no lawyer lives who can tell what the law is in
regard to trades unions in Australia; (2) That
the trades union movement has grown and
advantageously developed in contravention of
law, and, consequently, had they acted on legal
advice, we would have had no progress or forward
movement. The A.W.U. has for years retained a
solicitor, and his .ad vice has, in minor matters,
saved members in many cases from entering
into legal proceedings, but, on more important
issues, no one knows what the law is until judg-
ment is given in higher courts. If such decisions
are ever given and law is against the essential
advancement of the Trades Union movement the
law will have to be altered. A. pushing progres-
sive movement is always ahead of law and cannot
stop because of legal quibbles, even if it suffers
martyrdom, but democratic, self-governing com-
munities can make the cumbrous law-making
machine keep within coo-e-e, although behind all
the time.—W. G. Spence, President A.W.U.
Sydney, 13/9/’O2. [Yes, but just now “ the cum-
brous law-making machine” is ahead of the
A.W.U. in the Arbitration Act, which provides
a much better way than a strike for settling labor
difficulties ; and Union members may fairly expect
that their officials shall study that Act (which is
plain enough and to be easily understood). Any
counsel of standing would have told the A W U
the inevitable result of the first Arbitration
Court proceedings.]

Cough-Curing
‘‘The Cough was eased almost
inOttediately—is now completely
cured ”
Goughs are as common in this sort of
weather as roses in June; all kinds of
coughs from all kinds of reasons, yet all tha
coughs and all the reasons give way to one
thing:
“ 109, Nursery Road, Lozells,
“ Birmingham, England,
“ April 9th, 1901.
“ Dear Sirs, —My child suffered from
measles, which left behind a most distressing
cough. I tried several remedies for him,
including an emulsion made by another firm,
but it had not the least effect and did not
relieve the cough one atom. I was then
recommended to try Scott’s Emulsion and
to this end procured a bottle, and from that
time my child commenced to steadily im-
prove. The cough was eased almost imme-
diately, appetite greatly improved,* and
vitality and strength increased. I beg to
say my child is now completely cured,
and to Scott’s Emulsion only can I in
HARRY SHARRATT.
(From a photograph.)
justice give the credit of this satisfactory
result. I consider Scott’s Emulsion im-
measurably superior to the other Emulsion
I tried, and its pleasant taste and palata-
bility is a great point in its favour, the child
taking it readily from the first, but it was
only with the greatest difficulty he would
take the other preparation. To show you
how noticeable the improvement must be in
my boy’s condition, I may state our friends
frequently remark upon the change in him
and say how healthy he now looks.—Yours
truly, (Signed) E. Shabbatt.”
“To Scott’s Emulsion only can I in
justice give the credit for this satisfactory
result.” The result Mr. Sharratt alludes to
was the cure of “ a most distressing cough.”
For cough-curing is easy if you know just
what to do. All the coughs of these bitter
winter days will yield to Scott’s Emulsion,
and we have no hesitation in promising
you prompt, almost immediate relief,
with ultimate cure. Is it not the part of
wisdom and comfort to cure the cough you
have?
We are bound to point out to you, not
more in our interest than in your own, that
while curing coughs is easy enough if you
take Scott’s Emulsion, it by no means
follows that you will cure your cough if you
accept anything said to be “just as good.”
There never can be*, an imitation just as
good as the original. As for “ substitutes,”
no substitute for the good done by cod-liver
oil has yet been found.
Then, too, after cod-liver oil has refreshed
and reinforced the blood, two other ingre-
dients—blended in Scott’s Emulsion—come
into play, those perfect bone and tissue
builders, hypophosphites of lime and soda.
The three—as combined by our process—
form the perfect treatment for all coughs
from whatever cause arising.
Scott’s Emulsion, too, is pleasant to taste,
easy to digest, and has—according to an
eminent physician—three times the value
the same quantity of common cod-liver oil.
Itemember, therefore, to see that it is Scott’s
Emulsion, on the wrapper of every bottle of
w rich you will find a picture of a man
carrying a big fish.
You can obtain a sample of Scott’s Emulsion
by sending fourpence to Scott & Bowne, Ltd.,
483 Kent stree*, Sydney, and mentioning this
paper. All chemists sell Scott’s Emulsion.

De Babby.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Kissin’, kissin’, kissin’,
Lordy ! ain’t he sweet,
Rosy roun’ de mouf,
Rosy roun’ de feet.
Jes’ tuk out de water:
Fresher dan de rose;
Kiss him little fis’s.
Kiss him little toes.
Ain’t no babby like him,
Couldn’t nebber be,—■
Him dat all is mine,
Him dat’s kissin’ me !
M.J.C.
The Strongest Hope.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Kate Baylis sat on one side of the hearth nursing
her story, and wondering whether she should use
it further. Todd Frost sat on the other side and
came near to wishing that her own story was far
enough advanced to be held thus, in retro-
spection.
The fire arrived at that glowing stage which
gets at the quick of a woman, and, probing there,
draws blood. Kate Baylis made a little, half-
nervous gesture, strange in her, and took measure
of that younger face hard set against the fire-
ight.
“Iknow I’m treading on dangerous ground,”
she began, “and you’re no chicken, but —here
goes. The yarns I’ve heard about you lately have
been more or less lively, and I haven’t gone
through that phase myself for nothing. I know
that rumor sometimes falls short, and instinct tells
me it hasn’t done you justice. You needn’t culti-
vate that look. Keep it till I’m finished. We’ll
suppose you love this man, and he you. I know
only one thing against him, the trifling matter of
his being another woman’s husband.”
“ That makes the thing more worth while, of
course,. but puts it in the list of prohibited goods.
He’s kissed you, I suppose. If he hasn’t yet, he
intends to. Then you’ll do a little bit of plain
talking, and find it rather agreeable. He’ll kiss
you again, and there’ll be further unmasking.
Presently it will occur to you that the growth of
Love is very pleasant, and you’ll think you’ve
more right than even his wife to be the mother of
his children, because you have the laws of affinity
on your side. Quite right, I agree with you. The
only trouble is, the world doesn’t go round to
laws of love and affinity. The world’s laws hap-
pen to be of another kind.
“ Then you’ll think of your mother and your
friends. It’s rather late in the day for that, but
it’s a sort of auxiliary that usually comes in some-
where, and it gives you the idea that you really
are bestowing some careful consideration on the
problem. Well, some evening the man lets go.
You don’t, the first time ; and it pleases you when
he recovers and swears that he is a beast and you
are a wonderful woman. This little experience
lays bare the thoughts that once were veiled. You
call it perfect friendship. It may be ; we’ll let
that pass. Say you are labelled strong. So are
the Forces, and some time they plump on you
without warning preliminaries and down you go.
“Let this be said for the man, he continues to
want you, and you argue that you’ve done the
thing, and yqu’ll live by it. And away you both
go. It’s a bit dramatic at first, and things are
even better than you expected. That is, your
friends stand by you, and the man gets a billet,
and your meals come regularly. You had calcu-
lated on ostracism, physical discomforts, and pro-
bably the transient passion of the man. You
overlooked the most important factor in the whole
show— your own personality. That’s where the
bleaching begins.”
Kate Baylis leaned down and put another log
of wood at the back of those potent coals. At
that moment it struck Todd Frost that her hair
was 20 years older than her face.
“Then,” went on the other, very slowly, “the
man doesn’t always show tip well, and he deserves
preparatory consideration.”
Todd Frost forgot her own probable story for '
the moment. i
“ Did the man leave you ? ” she asked, abruptly.
“ No ; rather worse than that —he stuck to me,
till he died.”
“ And the child ? ”
“ Didn’t happen to be any.”
There was a forcible pause.
“I’ve never heard a whisper of this,” said the
girl, in amazement.
“Probably not. It must be 15 years ago now,
and I have generous friends. That was one of my
hardest knocks.”
Todd Frost made a convulsive motion forward.
“ Thanks for this, ” she said, jerkily. “It may be
too late, but that’s not your fault.”
Kate Baylis drew out her cigarette-case. “Well,
if you do try that gulf,” she said, puffing, “ I’ll
reach you the strongest rope.”
• * • *
A year later, the story worked to its end, and
Kate Baylis cast about for a rope.
“ The child’s dead,” the doctor said to her when
the climax came.
“ So much the better,” said Kate.
The mother tossed, raving, for a week, while
Kate kept watch, and carefully neglected the
doctor’s orders. He couldn’t understand why the
patient didn’t improve ; neither, in conversation,
could Kate. Being an accredited nurse, her little
extras were undreamt of. When the skilfully-
contrived end came, she surveyed her work with
quiet satisfaction. 1
“ Murder is not a musical word,” she said,
thoughtfully, “but I’ve kept my promise.”
M.L.
M.J.M.
Lately, in Maoriland House of Represen-
tatives, John Hutcheson desired to know if the
Government would take steps to protect the chil-
dren of the Taranaki dairying district from the
rapacity and callousness of their parents. Hun-
dreds of children in that wet, bush-clad, mud-
cursed district, who should be fast asleep in their
beds, are roused out at 3 and 4 a.m. to milk cows,
and thereby save their stingy parents the cost of
hired labor. The practice, Hutcheson said, was
resulting in stunted intellects and dwarfed bodies,
and its abolition was necessary for the good of the
community. Then Minister Thomas Duncan
arose in his wrath and cursed, not the callous
parents responsible for the evil, but the man who
had brought it to light. He said that such a
statement was injurious to the fair fame of Maori-
land. Then, as an afterthought, he denied the
existence of any such sweating. Immediately
afterwards he contradicted himself by declaring
that in Hutcheson’s own electorate little children
went round delivering milk at 5 o’clock every
morning. He seemed to imply that in some mys-
terious way John Hutcheson, and not the adminis-
tration, was responsible for all the evil, sweating,
and lawlessness in Hutcheson’s electorate. Any-
how, when Minister Duncan denied that child-
sweating exists in the dairying districts of Maori-
land he and the truth parted company. Almost
every pound of butter from the Taranaki district
is tainted with the crushed soul of some child or
other. But the dairy farmers have votes and the
tiny children who are being sweated into imbe-
cility and premature decay have none, and the
Farmers’ Union is an institution to which few
Maoriland politicians dare to speak crossly nowa-
days.

too, often very much prefer a country ride or a little touring. But, in
choosing a mount for theconntry ride or occasional tour, all the pleasure
is marred unless there is perfect confidence in the wheel.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
A.J.C. Spring meet scraps. Attendances on
each of the four days exceeded all previous records for
the same fixture, and gate-monev was, roughly, £6OO
better than last year’s. R. Wootton topped prize list
with £2865, O. Carlisle (£1616) came next, and Wakeful’s
owner, Macdonald (£1223), third. Altogether £14,539 was
paid away. Most successful jock, turned up in Victorian F,
Uunn with three wins, all Wakeful’s mounts Cross-
country rider, Chevally, and W. H. Smith, C. M. Godby,
N. Godby and A. McCrimmon gathered two races each.
List of winning sires was headed by Trenton through
Wakeful, who appropriated the three w.f.a. races.
Lochiel (Redeemed and Great Scot), and Invader (Lord
Monmouth and Bulli), were each the sires of two winners.
Mares succeeded in seven events, including the two
richest handicaps and three principal w.f.a. races.
Geldings won nine, and entires (Patronage, Abundance,
Homeward Bound, Strata Florida, Ibex, Great Scot, Lord
Monmouth and Postillion) annexed eight.
* * * *
Ease with which Wakeful gathered in all w.f.a.
races at recent A.J.C. meeting has made her a pro-
nounced favorite for Melbourne Cup (still close on two
months off), and the best price available at moment of
writing is B’s, with indications that that will shorten ere
long, as Mo isn’t over-anxious to lay. Everything going
right meanwhile, it’s a safe tip that Wakefui will start as
scorchingly hot as did Revenue from the same stable last
year. Patronage, The Persian and Abundance have been
liberally supported, too, at thousands to 50 and 60. Caul-
field Cup betting was also brisk on settling days last
week. Simile has been backed to win some thousands,
ditto Patronage and The Idler, about whom perhaps the
biggest parcel has been secured. The bejewelled one’s
heart is further gladdened by the amount of “ double ”
wagering indulged in. All manner of fancy combinations
have already been booked.
* * • *
Noticeable that the Melbourne dailies have
started to make encouraging allusions to the work of
Kinglike on the training track. This looks bad for King-
like’s chance of taking one or other of the Cups. Likely
winners in the Macdonald stable don’t get glowing ad-
vance notices from Mac.’s sporting-press courtiers. Not
much. Last year they never said anything calculated to
spoil the stable’s market for Revenue'and Wakeful.
* * * *
Last year Sequence did exactly what Air Motor
did this— viz., won the Epsom and ran third in the
Metrop. It is remembered now that N.S.W. let Air
Motor’s dam, Windmill, in foal to Gossoon (Ibex’s sire), go
to Maoriland for lOOgs. only.
* * » *
Metropolitan winner, Queen of Sheba, succeeded
four times in 21 starts last season, she annexing Sydney
Tattersall’s Mile Welter, April last, humping 7.7 to the
front in 1.40 J-. This feat should have suggested Epsom
possibilities, and probably did, for a lot of money was
taken about her, but, ’tis now told, she never got any-
thing like a clean run in the big mile, and was blocked
twice.
* * • •
Sainora-Midnight nag Railway, from Riverina
district, won second Steeple, last Saturday, of A.J.C.
meeting, in fine style, and looks a more than likely better
future performer. Half a dozen saddled. Tas. sport
“S. M. Wilson” sent both his—Bay Eagle (12.12) and
Rising Fund (10.10)—and on their merits. Chevally, who
had the mount on Bay Eagle in every recent previous
success, rode Rising Fund this time, and that nag started
first choice, his stable-chum being bracketed with Railway
a point longer. Vic. rep., Drummer, shaping a heap
better than in the first ’chase, previous Saturday, made
the pace from the jump, and the initial round of the
course saw him 20 lengths to the good. Rising Fund
came a cropper over second set of the treble, as most
Randwick touts predicted he would, but Chevally was up
again instanter, and followed the others at a great clip.
After being in front till nearing the last lep, Drummer
turned turtle, and, half-mile from home, the race looked
good for Bay Eagle. But Railway, having 3st. the better
of the weights, came with great dash, and, jumping the
logs on terms with Bay Eagle, finished a dozen lengths
best. Railway’s display of foot at the end was eye-open-
ing. P. Regan rode him.
» » * *
Duff Memorial (1 mile) Stakes Wednesday saw
Great Scot (9.1) easily account for the Derby winner
Abundance (9.10) who lined-up odds on. Five to 1 was
available and 10’s and 20’s had been taken about the
winner, who got home eight lengths best in the speedy
time of 1.40. Only Abundance’s failure to begin well
Went against him, and, as this shortcoming is common
property, it seemed surprising that he should have gone
out such a hot pot. Great Scot was bred by his trainer,
iTom Payten, and is by Lochiel from Scotch Mary, by
Clan Stuart from Ave Maria, by the Hermit (imp.), and
has engagements in all the big things ahead.
* * * *
Why Ikey waxes fat and can put heaps of coin
into bricks and mortar : Cyanide, humping 7.13 and
heavily backed, never threatened danger once in the
Metrop. ljm. covered in 2.36, but next racing day, with
9st. up, she won Sydney Handicap over same distance in
2.36! and finished the meeting carrying 9.12 into second
place for Waverley Handicap (lfm.) which winner
Regalia, appropriated in the speedy time of 3.4!.

Transpires that Epsom winner, Air Motor, was
backed, mostly in patches, to land a big sum ; one lucky
supporter secured £5OO, and would have made the scoop
bigger but for cautiousness of the nag’s trainer (Burton)
through fear of bumping and other possibilities, though
he wouldn’t have it that any nag could beat his. Same
man prepared Dare Devil, who won same race 10 years
ago, and took joy and considerable shekels Bathurst way.

Instances of how little clocking can be relied
Upon in connection with horse-racing are usually plenti-
ful at every big meeting. Ibex’s is the latest. With 8.12,
and heavily backed, he couldn't get near a place in
the Epsom Mile, Saturday week, when the winner’s
figures were 1.41, but third day saw him easily close on
Suburban Handicap, over same distance, in exactly same
time as Epsom winner, and carrying 211 b. more, in this
connection, Ibex’s second, by a head, to Q. crack, Lord
Monmouth, in Highweight Handicap (6fur.), Saturday,
Was something degrees above the ordinary ; he humped
12.8, and only went under in the last couple of strides,
after leading till then. Ibex gave Lord Monmouth 421 b.

Wakeful held such a mortgage over Randwick
Plate that the odds at last got to 10 to 1 on. At least
one conscienceless Mo asked 50 to 1 on. There were
three horses in the race, and the best Mo. would lay that
you could not place the three was 6 to 4.
* * * *
Imported nag, Simile, whose Metrop. running
impressed watchers, has since been freely backed for
coming Caulfield Cup, wherein he carries 7.7. Up to now
Simile hasn’t won a race. Prior to leaving England he
competed four times unsuccessfully, and since arriving in
this country his best performance is third in A.J.C.
Spring Maiden Stakes.
* * » *
It is stated on what looks like respectable
authority that, so far from suffering from the postal em-
bargo, “ Tattersall ” is now doing as good a business as
ever. This means that, in its endeavor to pander to
Pecksniff and co., the Commonwealth, instead of wiping
out “ Tattersall,” has only wiped out a lot of its own valu-
able postal revenue. Still the statement looks highly
improbable.
* * • *
The Stud Bill, which provided for taxing
stallions, and the consequent weeding-out : of the useless,
rubbishy entires that at present abound in Maoriland,
has been withdrawn from M.L. Parliament on the recom-
mendation of the Stock Committee. One clause of the
measure was very clumsily worded, prohibiting, as it did,
the use of horses suffering from physical defects. Now,
colonial thoroughbred stock has been built up from
renowned sires suffering from physical defects, caused by
accidents. A committee has been formed to draft a more
presentable measure for next session.
VICTOR TRUMPER, CHAMPION AUSTRALIAN
BATSMAN.
Photo, by Stump & Co., Adelaide.
The pleasant - faced, Sunday - school - looking
young man whose portrait appears in this issue is Victor
Trumper, who has proved himself to be the boss batsman
of these parts and of all the Australians who have ever
visited England. He is a quiet, unassuming individual
and his prosaic manner acts as a foil to his clean, crisp,
graceful cricket. He was always popular, but since he
knocked up 11 centuries the welcome he will receive on
his return home will probably far outshine that accorded
to his Nibs of Cornwall and York. It is an achievement
which totally eclipses that of any other Australian bats-
man, and is only excelled by that of Englishman O. B.
Fry, who made 13 centuries last year in first-class
English cricket. Fry scored his record in a remarkably
dry season, while Trumper piled his up in a season that
was unusually wet, and it is harder to build a tottering
score on wet slippery wickets than on good dry ground.
The internal significance of Trumper’s feat is that he has
scored nearly 1000 runs more than any of the other bats-
men during the present tour. He has never failed to
score, nor has he had a not-out innings out of a total of
52 journeys to the wicket, which is proba! 'another
record. He has played in every match but two, and but
for his timely century on many occasions the vanquished
Kangaroo had been dragged by his tail three times round
the crowded grandstands. His highest score in a single
innings was 128, and in a single match 109 and 119, a total
of 228.
• * • *
Though Mo.’s gains in W.A. and Q. together
are trifling compared with what he scoops in Vic. or
N.S.W., first-named States make infinitely more out of
him, and yet he remains aggressively affluent. He paid
£7O for betting privileges at Kalgoorlie R. O.’s recent four
days’ fixture. Now Q. turf authorities have decided the
diamond-bespattered one shall henceforth disgorge 4gs.
for each day’s betting, and his clerk 1 guinea a day, as
well as a yearly licensing fee of a similar sum. Great is
his lamentation in consequence, but he’d pay thrice
the sum were it wrung from him, and then remain sleek,
large-fronted, and affluent. Australia is a land of milk
and honey for the backer of the “ feeuld.”
» * » •
A petition in favor of the totalisator, lately-
presented to N.S.W. Assembly, starts with this immortal
truth: “ People do and will to the end of time speculate,
either in mining, buildings, stocks or racing.” Also they
gamble in many other things, including wheat and hymn-
books. The sludge of dogma and axiom may be heaped
on top, and all the sects and creeds may stand on the
sludge to stamp it down, but the fire burns underneath
just the same.

“ L.” : Sunbury (Vic.) Race Club’s last balance-
sheet has one or two rather funny items. It shows a
substantial credit, of which £32 Bs. lOd. is described as
“ moneys paid into the bank unaccounted for.” It seems
the finances of the last meeting got so mixed up that the
two secretaries and the committee nearly qualified for the
adjacent asylum trying to find out where all their
“ splosh ” came from. They finally gave up. One of the
secs, naively promised that there would be no such
trouble next time.
* * * *
Match last week-end, Australia v. another
South of England XI. (at Bournemouth this time)
was brief and brisk. Visitors notched 123 and 91, and
English side 87 and 66. Trumble very much out-trundled
himself, completely squelching all his previous remark-
able achievements with 15 for 68. Nine wickets went
down to his credit in the first effort—a performance
which he hadn’t reached before, though on four occasions
his bag for a single innings totalled 8.
* * * •
Match against the Players, being decided as
The Bulletin goes to press, is the last of present Aus-
tralian team’s engagements in England, and is second
only to the tests in importance. Players have mustered
exceptionally strong, so there’ll be a big fight. If Aus-
tralians succeed they’ll finish up with a better record
than any of their predecessors.
• « * ,
South of England’s 403 (innings declared closed)
in its first effort at Hastings last week was the highest
total, and Hayward’s 106 the fifth century', accumulated
against Australia this tour. The Home side’s score in-
cluded 37 sundries, which would make it appear as if the
wicket-keeping had fallen off, for they couldn’t all have
been wides, no-balls and leg-byes. Australians were not
in it from go to stop, and but for Trumper’s eleventh
century (120) wonld have been wiped out in one innings.
With 95 runs to get to v/in the Britishers lost 5 for 87.
Feature of the match was ’Ome bowler Vine’s second
innings effort—7 for 31— on a pitch favoring the bat.
This feat ranges alongside the best of its kind during- the
tour.
* • * *
Apropos drawn (very much in favor of ’Ome
side) match Australia versus England last week. Same
teams have met eight times. First season was ’B4, when
Australia won both matches, each in a single innings ;
’B6 saw the two meetings drawn. In ’BB Australia had 9
wickets to the good ; but two years later t’others were
successful both times—once by 97 runs and next by 10
wickets. Prior match this tour went Australiawards,
visitors hitting up a majority of 131. Veteran W.G.
skippered the Souths six times, and probably' filled that
position on latest occasion. Lord Harris was captain in
’B4, and W. H. Patterson in ’B6. Previous to this time
12 years had elapsed since South of England and Aus-
tralia met, and then the crack bowlers of the day—
Englishman Lohmann and Australian Ferris (both dead
now) figured—the former in each contest and the latter
in one. Team got together last week was made up of
first-class players to a man. Braund, Abel, and Hayward
shaped for England, and others were pretty near these in
quality.
• * * m
Victorian football progresses. The Association
(the junior league) recently disqualified for life a Rich-
mond player, for offering a couple of opponents a small
inducement to play stiff. As the attempt at squaring
took place at half-time on the open field—the squarees
promptly handed over their half-quids to their committee
—the simple gentleman probably deserved all he got.
But how low has football fallen when “the honor of the
game ” in the individual performer is priced at ten bob.
“Marinero”: Re the unsuccessful attempt of
Holbein to emulate the Channel swim of Webb. Holbein
may be a stronger swimmer and better stayer, but he
evidently lacks something possessed by the successful
Webb. Captain Webb sailed for many years in the mer-
cantile marine, and, when third mate of the National
liner Russia, jumped overboard in the middle of the
Atlantic to rescue a seaman who had fallen from the fore
topgallant yard (the Western ocean boats carried square
canvas in those days). Every sailor knows the amount of
wind and sea there is on when a topgallant sail has to
be furled, and as it happened at nine at night, in pitchy
darkness, the daring feat of Webb can he better under-
stood. A boat was lowered, and the third officer picked
up with the sailor’s sou’wester in his grasp, but the man
he had risked his life to save was no more. Is there
another swimmer who would jump, at night, to almost
certain death, in the teeth of a mid-Atlantic gale, from
the deck of a steamer going 14 knots, as Webb did ? The
courage that sustained him as he leaped from the Russia’s
taffrail sustained him in his long battle with the waters
of the English Channel. The pity is that such a man
should perish miserably in undertaking a feat entirely
beyond human power and endurance.
• * * •
In reply to “R.F.8.” (Tas). Cavill pere did not
swim the English Channel, but went very near it. The
world’s record for 100 yards with turns is Fred Lane’s
recently put up (in England) 60sec. Best straightway
100yds. is “Mud” Bishop’s 1.1 3-5 at Fitzroy Dock
(Sydney) 3 years ago.
* * * *
Apropos cabled news that English swimmer
Billington beat Australian Read over a quarter mile in
5.55, and, a day or two later, that Dick Cavill had
accounted for both Read and Billington at a like distance,
but in ssec. slower time. Probably the Billington-Read
race was swum in a smaller bath than the Cavill-Read-
Billington struggle, and the greater number of turns
necessary to complete the journey would help the time,
particularly with such adepts at that business as the three
concerned. Cavill swam the 440 faster (5.37 2-5) at Wan-
ganui (M.L.), last February, and then created a fresh
world’s amateur record. World’s champion pro., “Joey”
Nuttall, has the speediest figures (5.35 1-5) to his credit.
* » » •
The latest sensation at the London Aquarium
is “ looping the loop,” in which a cyclist, by flying down
an incline at an angle of 45 degrees, gets up a speed,of 50
miles an hour (estimated), and so is able to climb up and
round the inside of a big upright loop in the track. He
is, of course, head downward when at the top of the loop,
40ft. from the ground, but at the pace centrifugal force
makes it impossible to fall downwards. The real risk
(and a big one) is that his steering on the narrow track
may go astray, in which case he rides off it sideways into
space. And when a man is riding with his bike above his
head his steering is apt to be erratic.

Prior to his big Goulburn-to-Sydney win Little-
child held the 100 mile road record for Tasmania; but
failed to get full credit for the performance owing to some
unfortunate rumors concerning a steep hill, some en-
thusiastic supporters, and a tow-rope.
* * • *
“Bunkum”: A Melbourne paper remarked:
“ The average man who can expand his chest sin. does very
well, indeed. Sandow, at the Opera House, expanded his
14in.” A well-known Sydney medico told the writer
once that the man who could legitimately expand 4in.
would do something wonderful—3in. was recognised as
exceptional. Reported 7in. expansions are put down by
medical men as absolute rot. Now we hear of Sandow
swelling out 14in. Probably he does the trick (which
it must certainly be) as Lucifer did it in Sydney a few
years ago. (Lucifer told that he was a pupil of Sandow’s
and travelled with him some time in America.) He would
inflate fully 15in. by inhaling successive great gulps of
air, holding one as he brought the other on top of it till
his chest, back, and shoulder muscles were expanded to
distortion almost, while the veins stood out on his face,
threatening to burst. The pain of the effort was plainly
discernible, and Lucifer always wanted a minute or two
to recover himself afterwards. This is probably Sandow’s
way of expanding, but is it the right thing ?
* • * •
The old story of Samson’s strength being in his
hair is discredited by Sandow, whose torso is inno-
cent of hair as that of a newly-born baby. There are very
few men whose chests are not bushy, yet Sandow’s frame
is clean as the palm of his hands. Possibly he follows the
custom of the ladies of ancient Greece and Rome in allow-
ing only the hair of the head to grow, but that doesn t
alter the fact that he is a living contradiction of the Sam-
sonian legend.
• • • •
Veteran Prof. Miller is out with a challenge to
Sandow, daring him to contest “such exercises as the
gladiators of Greece and Rome excelled in,” and mention-
ing dumb-bell lifting, wrestling, boxing, and sword and
foil combats. The Prof, is a trifle weak in his classical
history when he enumerates the foil as an ancient
weapon ; but, as he is himself a crack fencer, and the son
of probably, the best foilsman in Australia, the slip is
perhaps pardonable. If Sandow should yet accept, the con-
test looks a good thing for the Australian, for he would
certainly be all over the strong man in boxing and
fencing, and almost certainly with the broadsword.
The younger man would score with the weight-
lifting, and possibly with the wrestling, though
this is by no means certain. Sandow himself
summed up the position when he said that physical cul-
ture was not athletics, and that development does not
make a man a boxer, an oarsman, or a footballer, but
simply provides a foundation. Strong men generally
imagine themselves to be fighters and frequently discover
their error of judgment in an uncomfortable way.
“ Why,” remarked a brawny Melb. barrister at a recent
light-weight glove-fight, to a sporting pressman, “ why
do you want to set up these mannikins against one
another? Surely you can get some heavy-weights. Ha\e
a look around the navvies’ camps and you’ll find some
fighters among the big fellows.” The pressman smiled.
“ Bring out your biggest navvy, and I’ll bet a fiver that
little Micko Walsh sends him to join the Seven Sleepers
inside five minutes.” The merely big men and their sup-
porters base their opinion on the assumption that the
other man will be hit. The other man, if he is a skilled
boxer, isn’t hit.
* * * •
Sandow’s notions, if genuinely expressed, are
diametrically opposed to generally-accepted ideas, and
henceforth there need be no more self-denial in the
successful athlete’s make-up. “ Sleep as long as you
can,” says the visiting mass of muscle, “ eat and drink
anything fancied. . . . Alcohol does good, not .harm.
Personally I don’t care whether I drink champagne,
whisky and soda, or beer. ... I smoke my pipe at home
and my cigar in the street. Cigarettes are not harmful
if the smoke is not inhaled. . . . Plunge into the coldest
possible water while you are very hot. ... Do not towel
yourself violently, but merely dab the body to take off
some of the water. There is no need to be particular
about being too dry.”
* * m m
Tennis Tussle, Victoria against N.S.W., ceased
in Sydney, Saturday, with former victors —13 rubs, to 8.
Bright particular performer of the whole lot was
Victorian Dr. Eaves, who gathered up every rub. he
figured in and only lost two sets of 12, playing safe
mostly, but his partner, Dunlop, showed brilliant work
and got in some dazzling smashes. Eaves’ screw service
told effectively frequently, and he certainly demonstrated
indisputably that Australian lawn tennis hasn’t known a
better player. Same player easily won N.S.W. premier-
ship 11 years ago ; since then he has played in the best
English company and came-Australiawards again with a
greatly enhanced reputation, which he sustains to the
smallest detail. Friday Dr. Eaves beat Gaden and Rice
(N.S.W.’s two best men) 6—2, 6 —2, and 7—5, 6 —3 respec-
tively. Most successful of N.S.W. players in the singles
was old Sydney Grammar School boy Sharp, and he
proved the only one on the side to win both his matches.

Is Australia developing a new generation of
bird-murderers. Atop of young (“Parvo”) Fraser’s
slaughter comes news of another juvenile deadly shot in
the person of Charlie Petting.il, a young St. Helens
schoolboy, aged 14. His maiden effort at the traps was
at Yambuck Gun Club’s meet, t’other day, and resulted
in a bag of 13 out of 15. This is surely a record under
the circumstances ?
Melbourne “Micko” Walsh and Sydney “Abe
Willis allegedly fought for Australian “ feather ” supre-
macy at Sydney Gaiety A. C. t’other day, but as no re-
sponsible person saw them weighed, ’tis hardly likely the
winner’s claim will be recognised. Besides, Bob Turner,
now in M.L., but shortly to return, has a say in the
matter, as he beat “ Micko ” Walsh, the winner, some few
months ago. Walsh-Willis affair was a proof of the
foolishness of digging out old-timers and expecting them
to shape well. The thing was over inside two rounds,
aud would have ended in the first but for Walsh’s
leniency. Willis, who, 10 years ago, was considered good
enough to match against negro George Dixon for the
world’s top-notchership, showed little of his old brilliancy
or stamina, but still (just verging on 36) he’s not quite
ancient yet. Match already made with Newcastle’s
armour-plated pug, Paddy King, will supply an oppor-
tunity for Walsh to show the best that is in him.
• * • *
N.S.W. amateur boxing supremacies are an-
nounced for decision under N.S.W. Sports Club manage-
ment next month. Usual weeding-out of ineligibles left
ii qualified to shape up—a record number. Last year,
when the Sports Club tackled the trouble, the Cornstalk
State owned only one hall-marked amateur champion,
excepting, perhaps, lightweight Jimmy M'Ginley, heavy
Harry Beckett, and one or two others. Most of the old-
days’ so-called amateurs almost invariably got away with
a whack of the gate, or something considerable in the
shape of coin, after fighting on Saturday nights, against
admitted professionals before huge crowds drawn by
flaming poster advts. and surrounded generally by very
professional atmosphere.
» * • »
Bruisers Jim Lenton and Fred James supplied
a nice warm scrap at Sydney Gaiety A. 0. Monday night.
James, tall, with well-developed shoulders and chest, and
game as a black Spanish rooster, stood inches over
blocky-built Benton, a pug in shape and appearance and
strong as a donkey. Bast time writer saw Benton in the
ring (opposed to Bob Turner) he had been overworked in
training and looked it; previous to then he shaped any
time his friends or the boxing hall people wanted him,
without boots or socks or the semblance of condition and
fought like a bull dog all the time. Bast Monday it was
a different Benton, trained to the moment, and he fought
as never before, and from early in the fourth till late in
the fifth round, when the fight ended, he gave James a
sorry time of it. James took the stoush and dealt out a
heap himself, but Lenton was too rocky. Once James was
rushed past the ropes out on to the floor, 3ft. below the
ring stage, where his head bumped hard, but he clambered
up and through to fight again and lose more gore. Benton
has come on a heap and will give any in his class trouble
every time he shapes equal to Monday night’s form.
* * » »
Double Bay (Sydney) sculler Pearce came home
t’other day after one and a-half years’ sojourn in England.
He tells that world's champion Towns has come on in
speed and stamina since last seen here, and has always
finished with something up his sleeve. Gaudaur is a
“born gentleman.” Maoriland Tom Sullivan has deve-
loped huge proportions, but can row some yet-—he won a
fair sum of money over Pearce’s match with Hackett in
Canada. Australian Kelly, who annexed last Henley
Diamond Sculls, fitted up his own boat and rowed his
own way. With a,bit more knowledge he’ll prove the
best amateur ever seen at Henley, or elsewhere. Aus-
tralia’s little champ., Daley, could have gathered in this
year’s Henley laurels easily. A good English combination
could beat all Australia at oar-rowing. Pearce is already
looking for a match, and wants them all to come as soon
as possible.
Acting-Premier Ward’s latest on the
Maorilancl Arbitration Act : —“The Act has done
more good to the State than any other legislation
that has ever been passed.” N.S.W. should grip
this testimony all the time.
A railway strike seems just possible in
North Queensland, as one of the results of Phi Ip’s
retrenchment schemes. The N.Q. railway worker
is a man who.takes his life in his hand arid drives
a bad engine over a decayed road, stretching
across a blighted land, for very moderate wages,
and that in a country where victuals are dear, and
every kind of victual has its dead and bloated
cockroach in it.
Even the S.M. Herald prints this :
Johannesburg to-day is probably the most unsettled
city in the universe. During the past few weeks some
25,000 irregular troops have been disbanded in the close
neighorhood of the Rand. Even with economy it is hard
to live under 15s. per day. The capitalists, realising that
now is the time to institute new orders, are endeavoring
to reduce the scale of wages, with a fine disregard to the
fact that living expenses are daily becoming higher.
And it was for these— the Lord forgive us ! —we
helped to smash the Boer and snuffled that we did
it in the cause of Liberty !

N.S.W. Fisheries Board, which has for
some time been trying to acclimatise Tasmanian
crayfish in N.S.W., recently discovered that the
much-desired crustacean had been thriving on the
South Coast all along, disguised to suit the climate
and to elude Jack Want and Frank Farnell. Mr.
E. J. Paton, a keen ex-Tas. fish enthusiast, now
in N.S.W., recognised his retiring and back-
sliding fellow-islanders, and got Commissioner
Farnell to investigate. With a little judicious
protection and careful transplanting along the
coast, the new and unexpected find should grow
nto a valuable asset for JST.S. W.
Labor representation in the Vic. Parliament
will probably be stronger after the present elec-
tions than at any previous time. Over 300,000
persons will, directly or indirectly, suffer through
the lapse of the Wages Boards, and the Labor
crowd should stand on velvet with the people who
have been so miserably tricked by Irvine and
Shiels. Also, it has been estimated that the
public servant influences one vote in every
eight, and, as the majority of these are in Mel-
bourne, Ballarat, and the other cities, they can
give a very solid vote. Bromley and Prendergast
and the rest, who persistently denounced any
cutting down of salaries, will probably secure the
votes of practically the whole service, including
judges, departmental secretaries, and many other
people who never before entered into even their
most sanguine dreams. One of the big fights of
the election will probably be against the Syme-
hated Trenwith, at Richmond, but the great
David’s pet candidates have not shown to much
advantage of recent years; witness “Jimmy”
Munro, who opposed poor old Gillies ; and Kemp,
who fought Frank Madden at last election.
