The Manufacture of Spurious Public
Opinion.
Civio apathy, besides being responsible for the
selection of improper persons for the discharge of
functions actually allotted to them, is also a cause
of the usurpation by such persons of powers not
allotted to them. Such usurpations of power,
acquiesced in by the weary citizen, and used by
small classes of the people to further objects un-
attainable by them in the ordinary process of
polities, and to grind axes otherwise inaccessible,
give small and cheeky minorities an undue share m
the legislation and administration of the country.
Civic apathy is the cause of the evil. In Victoria,
for instance, glaring usurpations of powers not
allotted to them are common with Municipal
Councils, Justices of the Peace, and School Boards
of advice. Municipal Councils are composed of
persons with a rating qualification of £2O annually.
Their functions are to make roads and to pass by-
laws for certain limited purposes, and to pay
the cost by rating the lands in their districts.
They are chosen solely for these purposes, and are
not at all chosen, even by their restricted con-
stituencies, to represent the views of the electors
on any questions whatever ou'.sideof road-making
and other such local matters. Yet, civic apathy
allows these councils regularly, in season and
out of season, to voice their views on all sorts of
political, industrial, and social questions. They
profess to do so officially—just as the inspector
of nuisances might lend the weight of his official
authority for the removal of bad smells and dead
goats, in support of the abolition of the monarchy
or the annexation of Polynesia. Among subjects
that have been unduly engineered in Victoria, m
these ways, may be mentioned the bitter anti-
Labor programme of the PATTERSON government,
opposition to the extension of the I actories Act,
to inspection of boilers in country districts, and to
the improvement, by legislation, of wages, hours
of labor, or conditions of labor in country districts.
Also, country local bodies, elected to look after
road-metal, have meddled with the working of the
Factories Act, even in Melbourne ; have opposed
old age pension legislation supported
the unconstitutional action of the Peacock
Government in the Tocsin episode ; and have
meddled officially with questions of railway con-
struction, woman suffrage, proportional represen-
tation, reduction of members of Parliament, the
constitutional relations of the two Houses, re-
trenchment of the public servants, and so on. The
complaint is, not that the individuals composing
the councils influence public opinion and legisla-
tion in these matters, for as individuals their
voices would be those of a few lone ones crying in
the wilderness for more thistles, but that they use
the organised municipal machinery and taxes of the
country, for the purpose of attaining non-munici-
pal ends. Justices of the Peace, chosen, in a kiss-
ing-goes-by-fayor fashion, by a Legislative Council
Minister with many members to placate, for the
purpose of administering the local police-court
and Small Debts Acts, similarly usurp functions
not given to them, and by their assumption, un-
ctieeked by public opinion or Ministerial reproof,
have actually repealed many wholesome Acts of
Parliament. Their mode of operation is to deliver
ta-ades against the laws they are sworn to ad-
minister, and to punctuate their contempt for
them by imposing penalties of one shilling, with-
out costs, on the fraudulent, unscrupulous, or at
an y rate deliberate breakers of these laws. The
Milk Adulteration Act was long a nullity because
of this tendency, and so was the Short-Weight
Bread Act. Manifestos of open rebellion against
the Factories Act are the order of the day in
many suburban courts, and tend greatly to sup-
port the employers’ clamor that the Act is a
failure, and competent paid magistrates are
hustled from certain courts by usurping Dog-
berries, who swarm on the bench to refuse, in
face of the clearest evidence, to punish the breakers
of certain laws that tend for the benefit of the
workers, and to coarsely revile the laws themselves.
In like manner School Boards of advice are elected
by certain ratepayers, for the purpose of advising
the Education Department on repairs to schools,
and a few other matters such as holidays, conduct
of teachers and the prosecution of negligent
parents. But instead of confining themselves to
these functions, or even doing them well before
they meddle with outside matters, the members
continually use their positions as elected persons,
to pass resolutions on extraneous subjects, ranging
from the inculcation of drivel about “loyalty”
and the Empire, to the abolition of the compulsory
education clause in the interests of cockatoo slave-
drivers. This tendency to leave their own work un-
done while attending to every other kind of work,
sin, foolishness and bosh arises through popular
apathy. The people allow these things, so these
things happen. Almost anything of a nefarious
nature will happen if it is permitted to do so.
Two more returned swaddies, who before
they left for S’Africa were of good reputation,
have been sent to gaol for brutal and cowardly
assaults on a constable at Burra (S.A.). The,
cohentingenting was going to be “ a valuable
training for our police force.” It is certainly
giving the police force plenty of exercise, and a
good deal of training.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.

Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearl,? and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send tl-eir parcels direct to him. The firm has

London Times’ pompous talk about the
“ vice-regal magnificence” of the Commonwealth’s
Governor-General does not spring wholly from
British ignorance; part of it comes from taking
toady Australians, social and political, as voicing
the national sentiment. The short way to kill
sucli toady misrepresentation is for the Common-
wealth Parliament to state distinctly what kind
of a man it wants. Where is the member who
will move to some such effect as this :
Australia desires its Governor-General to be a man of
ordinary information and decent cmduct; more informa-
tion will not be objected to, but he must have the dis-
cretion to keep it to himself when its expression would
conflict with the Constitution Act; occasional or free
drinking will not he criticised, but he must not be a sofe;
the £lO,OOO he draws as salary will be considered wholly
his ; he can dine, if he likes, with the hangman, the arch-
bishops, or the-l’remiers of the States, but if he does he
must pay the score and notcome begging to the Treasury
afterwards ; he can do what he likes (within the limits of
decency) with his overtime—fish, or raise fowls, or collect
stamps, but his work is to do what his Ministers tell him,
within the Constitution, and the £lO,OOO is the pay (and
very go >d pay indeed) for that service.

Chronic Constipation results from a lack of
moisture in the residual matter of the food.
It becomes so dry and hard that the muscular
coating of the int 'stines is unable to expel
it. Two of Chamberlain’s Stomach and
Liver Tablets should be taken to begin
with and after that one each day. When
taken just after supper they will insure an
operation of the bowels on the following
morning. In most cases this lack of moisture
in the faecal matter is caused by an insuffi-
ciency of water being taken into the system
and cannot be cured unless more water is
taken with the food or drink. If taken
with the food, unless in shape of fruits
(apples and bananas), it is apt to interfere
with the processes of digestion; but if a regu-
lar habit is established of taking a full glass
of water as soon as you get up in the morning,
about two hours after each meal, and just be-
fore going to bed, it will in most cases correct
the disorder—not at once, but in time.
Chamberlain’s Stomach and Liver Tab=
lets are not intended for habitual use out
to give temporary relief until a cure can be
effected by taxing more water into the system
and establishing a regular habit by having
the bowels move every morning immediately
after breakfast whether there is an inclination
or not.

Unfulfilled Desires.
The men who die regretting that so much of
the work they purposed to do is left undone are
generally those who have achieved the most.
Charles Dickens was a marvel of industry, always
working or planning future work ; but he died at
58, almost with the pen in his hand, an unfinished
novel on his desk, and with the earnest wish to
see Australia—the country where one of his sons
made his home—unfulfilled. Cecil Rhodes, the
man of millions (using the phrase in a double
sense, for among the millions of Britain’s sons
we look in vain for the like of him), dies at 49,
regretting that he has accomplished so small a
portion of the task he set himself to perform. It
n tragic when Death stops the worker. When
Sickness postpones the work it is sad enough,
th jugh in that case Hope is always near by. Mr.
August Jansan, of Karabury Station, near Hay,
N.S.W., wrote, on January 20th, 1902:—“My
occupation (that of station carpenter) compelled
me to spend part of my time exposed to the heat
of the sun during the extremely hot summer of
1399, which caused me much subsequent suffer-
ing It began with sickness at the stomach and
a, distaste for food. Then 1 was alarmed by acute
pains in the region of the heart. The bowels,
too, were irregular, co3tiveness alternating with
purging. Each week marked a change for the
worse. Sick headaches and pain at the chest,
with want of appetite, added to my other troubles,
made me nervous, depressed, and irritable. From
patent medicines, and a doctor whom I consulted,
i derived no benefit whatever. Mine was a case-
t>i acute indigestion, and a friend, who had used
Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup with much benefit,
persuaded me to try it. I agreed, and found
relief from almost the first dose. By the time I
had taken two bottles I knew that I was fairly
on the road to health. My strength returned
much quicker than it had departed. Befoie the
sixth bottle was emptied I was restored to a
thoroughly healthy and robust condition. 1
could eat, sleep, and work without distress or
e/en discomfort. It is now six months since my
care was completed, when I gave up taking the
Syrup regularly; but, as a precaution, 1 still
take a couple of doses every week. Many oth i
persons in this district have taken it on my
recommendation, and in no case has it failed.”*
Tlie Townsville Mudbanks.
“ Sirhap ” writes about some tilings up North :
Premier Philp, of Queensland, is one of the member?
for Townsville, and he is very good to it. It is an arti-
ficial seaport, lying at the moutti of a small creek which
runs into a broad, open basin very shallow at its edge.
Money will make anything, and i& has made a harbor foi
Townsville. Of course, the h trbor is always crank, and
needs constant pouring in of State money to keep it
decently going. It is the port for the Charters Towers
and Ravenswood goldfields, and for the great western
pastoral industry. It has a railway running to Winton
(350 miles), the chief north-western pastoral town. This
line cannot now do justice to the production of its tapped
country, and after the drought has ended —assuming that
its railway from Hughendon to Townsville lives through
the big flood which follows every big drought up there—
it will need a duplicate set of rails ; practically another
line. Now Bowen is probably the best harbor in Queens-
land, except Gladstone, and there is but one gap of 55
miles between the end of its toy railway at Wangarattn
Creek and Ravenswood. The last has a branch railway
of 24 miles functioning with the Townsville main line, so,
were this gap filled up, the west would have the best
northern port to operate on ; and a duplicate set of rails
from the Ravenswood junction to Hughendon or Winton
would be the means of opening up for g’eneral develop-
ment (mining, agriculture and pastoral) 70,000 square
miles of good country. A better way is to pull up the
Ravenswood branch—it’s about done and connect
Ravenswood directly with Charters Towers, 45 miles dis-
tant, passing through mining country all the way. This
country is the mining hereafter of Charters Towers and
Ravenswood goldfields, having now six gold camps. But
this is all a dream as far as Piiilp is concerned. Of course
he cannot last much longer, and he has to thank the
drought for its mercies as regards his political position.
But when the floods come, and no railway line to speak
of between Hughendon and Townsville, and that port
blocked by silt, and the low-lying part of the town hasten-
ing out to the Barrier Reef, another king will arise and
fresh departures will take place. Queensland has lost one
million in two years through excess of expenditure, yet
far more than that worth of assets is lying idle near Bowen
through the want of this gap being railwayed. District
teems with city and country crown lands, and, given a
direct communication through Ravenswood to Charters
Towers, either by Ravenswood Junction line or a new
direct line through the Kirk and five other gold camps,
the million would come to hand in a few months, and
many thousands annual revenue to follow. But then Philp
is member for the shallow harbor of Townsville, and its
mudbanks return him to Parliament regularly.
The “Triumph of the New TestamenV
When the Boer war was in progress one fatuous
English Bishop said that the issue was really
between the Old and New Testament—between
the Boers, on the one side, with Israelitish ideas of
enslaving the men of the native races and seizing
the women for their harems, and the English on
the other with modern Christian ideas of freedom,
morality and enlightenment. That sort of fudge
was talked pretty commonly, too —not quite in
that absurd degree, perhaps, but still to the extent
of representing England as fighting for the good of
the native races and for the moral improvement
of the country. It will surely then come with
something of a shock to the pious people who were
such warm supporters of this war of Cohen
against the little farmer Republics, to learn from
recent cables that the conquering British power
in the Transvaal has imposed a head tax upon the
Kaffirs to force them to work in the Rand mines
(i.e., to enslave them), and that polygamy has
been established as a State institution, provided
that for every wife (except the first, who is exempt)
the black husband pays a yearly license of
£2 ! What will Exeter Hall say to these first fruits
of the triumph of the New Testament ?

How to Abolish Titles.
LABOUCHERE writes that during the reign of the
late Queen Victoria, Birthday Honors were dis-
tributed as follows Each department furnished
a list. The Prime Minister cut it down. The
Queen cut it down again. The men selected were,
in the main, those who had subscribed largely to
the party funds. He goes on :
No one in her day could be certain that he would gst
his title for his money. ... I see no reason why titles
should not be sold. For instance, I would obtain all the
money that the Coronation may cost by selling titles to
those willing to buy them. The number would be so
large that after all the expenses of the Coronation had
been paid, there would remain enough over to enable us
to meet the military expenditure that is now to be pro-
vided for by taxing the bread of the poor. It would be
far better that there should be a few hundred more
wealthy plutocrats with a prefix to their names than that
poor women and children should have their starvation
allowance of bread reduced. A good deal of money, too,
might be raised bj' giving existing peers a higher title
than they at present bear. To us humble individuals a
lord is a lord, but to lords it would seem that the differ-
ence between a marquis, and an earl, and a baron is a
matter of supreme importance. Another promising mode
of raising the wind would be to sell titles to wealthy
widows and spinsters.
Now, as the object to be achieved is not to raise
money by the sale of titles, but to abolish them, it
is worth while recalling the old scheme of a
Bulletin correspondent, “Ignotus,” namely, that
every good republican should at once assume a
title and use it. There is nothing illegal about
this, and if there were you couldn’t prosecute
thousands of offenders. “ I feel sure,” wrote
“Ignotus,” “that if I were always addressed as
Lord Wellington (the town where I live) and
you were Viscount Sydney, and so on, the tabula;
would soon be solvuntur risu.”

“Alderman’' writes :
It is to be hoped that the N.S.W. Amending Municipal
Act will speedily come into force and bring our municipal
law more up-to-date. In some country municipalities
queer things are done. Know of one alderman, elected
for three years, during which time he filled the office of
Mayor, and who was actually not a ratepayer during the
last two years of his term. A clause might be added that
when an alderman is six months in arrears with his rates
his position should be forfeited—that the mere fact of
being in arrears should make his seat vacant. The
accumulation of back rates is very often the result of
aldermen themselves being on the defaulters’ list, and not
pressing for the recovery of rates for fear of exposing
themselves. Another vexed question which ought to be
settled by the new Act is : Whether church proper.y
otherwise than churches should be rated ?
Maoriland’s recurrent leper turned up
again recently in an Otago gold mining district.
All his fingers had disappeared and some of his
feet also ; yet he contrived to carry on gold-mining
and earn enough to go drinking and gambling.
He doesn’t seen to have had a special pewter
kept for himself at any of the pubs either, and the
ghastly fact is causing a slump in the bar trade in
the district. Most of thecustomers take it homed
a bottle at present... The Chinese missionary and
several European doctors have certified that it is
leprosy, but there are a number of people in thedis
trict who declare that the man is only sufh ring from
frostbite, and when the Govt, medical officei
ordered the Waihemo Council t.> isolate the Chow
the Council decided that he was not a leper but a
frostbiter. However, the medical authorities
threatened to set the law in motion, and the
opposition in the Council pointed out that if the
medical officer said the disease was leprosy, it ws
leprosy, and that ended it, and leper or frostbiter,
he must be captured and isolated. The Council,
it recent advices, was diligently searching for a
itrong man, a man of nerve, who would do tin;
capturing. They have a great dread of leprosy

! “ Hermes " hears the fag-end of a tragedy :
The little supper room with its white-and-gold-papered
walls. Outside the roar of King-st. at 11 p.m. ; inside
the low hum of conversation and the buzz of the electric
fan. The stolid, fair man talked earnestly to the man
who sat beside me, and latter nodded his head wearily
in approbation while he ate his oysters. The tall,
dark man who talked so vehemently bad boiled fowl and
oyster sauce on his plate, but he wasn’t eating it. In-
stead, he held the drum-stick between his finger and
chumb, and emphasised his points by tapping the other
;nd of it on his plate and sending little spurts of oyster
muce over his dress-suit. But as it was his own dress-
iuit no one said anything. Suddenly he ground his teeth
—he was dreadfully excited, and leaning across the table,
shook the drum-stick at his friend, while his voice rose like
the wind, a:id the room stared with well-bred astonishment,
:orit was a dress circle crowd. The man who was eating
oysters said never a word, but regarded him with a fixed,
iphinx-like expression, and went on eating oysters, while
the drum-stick waved about and the oyster-sauce dripped
lown on to the starched cuff and the diamond links. The
rising voice had reached a roar, and become audible over
the gen .ral conversation : “ I tell you I don’t blame him
tor taking advantage of her being stony-broke ; it’s the
game ; I’d have done it meself, so I forgive him that.
But, damn him, what I hate him for is that the cow made
her love him, and forget me. Yes, by G , actually
dislike me, d’ye hear me, made her fairly hate the sight
of me, him!" and in his excitement he banged his
stolid friend on the head with the greasy bit of fowl.
“Lookout, you fool, that hurts,” snorted the injured
listener. “ I know it does, I intended it to, but I wish to
the Loard it had been him. Let’s get out of this ! I want
to break something large or upset a tram-car.” So they
went.
Robb. Brothers on Grass Tree Nob (where
Sydney Art Gallery Columns are to come from):
Grass Tree Nob is 20 miles from Kempsey on the
Macleay River. It is five miles beyond Timagog, which
is a place where about two farmers live--beyond that is
desolation. It is a large hill of solid marble, from which
21 columns are to be cut for Sydney Art Gallery. The
stone extends in a belt that is a mile long and half-a-mile
wide and heaven only knows how' deep. There are miles
and miles of marble. The lime-burners have got lime
from it for the past 30 years or more. You could build
the Pyramids with Grass Tree Nob marble if so minded,
all of it first-class stone. It obtains its rich chocolate and
occasional sea-green tint from the oxide of iron lode
which w'alls it in on one side. You see perfect fossil
shells in it, and wide streaks of kalsite, as crystalline in
some place as diamonds. The seams nearest to the lode
are deeper and richer in colors and each seam is lighter
in shading as it is further removed from the iron, till it
becomes a crystal white streaked w'ith pale reddish brown.
Experts say th it this marble takes the polish better, is
easier to work (cutting as easily as freestone) and is more
durable than any other stone they have come across. The
fossil shells are most unmistakably marked. You see
those fan-shaped shells that are so plentiful on Takapuna
beach in Auckland harbor, and cockle shells of a very
large size. The Nob is chambered with large caves ; the
entrances like wombat holes. One cave is 60 feet
deep and is walled in on all sides with marble ; it has not
been thoroughly explored yet. There are a few splendid
stalactites in it, one like a large head of Medusa, a hun-
dred tentacles hanging downward. The quarrymen are
expecting to open up caves that may equal Jenolan or at
least come up to Naracoorte.

At Poverty Point.
The first night of “ Robin Hood,” at the Adelaide
Royal, Langdon Bonython’s Advertiser, for reasons un-
known, jumped on Lauri and Hugh Ward by ignoring
them altogether. Lyding and Thorley got their good
word, then Florence Young, and Maud Ohetwynd ; also
Leslie and Bantock ; after which, “ The minor parts in
the cast were all satisfactorily represented.”
The dangers of the unfamiliar metaphor. S.A. Register
has got itself much disliked by calling the star performers
in a literary societies’ tournament “ the culls of all the
societies.” And it was trying hard to be complimentary 1
“Bole Armenia”: Re par. in B. 6/9/’O2 dealing with
comedian Hugh Ward’s lip make-up in “ Robin Hood,”
and his performance of the old “ Blobs ” in Floradora, the
lip-building idea, and all other face graftings, are as old
as the grandfather of Roscius. Ask any experienced
actor. As to Ward’s being judged an old man in private
life by his stage senility in “ Floradora,” who ever saw,
off the stage, such a sparkling-eyed, nimble-footed, elas-
tically trim-figured old man with such a bright-toothed
smile as Ward’s Cyrus Gilfain ? Of Ward’s many excel-
lences, delusive make-up is a long way from being on top
of the bundle. Some actors can lose themselves in make-
up beyond the recognition of press, public, and even
fellow mummers “in front.” But to achieve this, and
bob up differently in every role, means never becoming
familiar to audiences. If you would live on the stage
beware not to triumph that way, but thump your own
fiersonality into the public heart. Even Brough’s popu-
arity in character parts was partly due to the fact that he
played them all exactly alike. [Did he?—Ed. B.]. Versatility
is art’s own pet offspring ; but every actor on top of the
heap has his own home-made individuality as pronounced
and unchangeable as North Head, Sydney. [North Head
is steadily crumbiing.—Ed. B.].
It seems to be probable that Henry Irving is coming to
Australia under Musgrove’s management, for it is
reported that Musgrove is providing a new London theatre
for the actor, and is taking him, generally speaking,
under his wing.
The funniest experience of Donald Macdonald as a
lecturer occurred when he went up to a town in Western
Victoria and told a few of his war yarns. Donald had been
received in great style by the municipal magnates, and
the Mayor rose to the occasion with many drinks. As
soon as he had started his lecture at night, Macdonald
caught sight of the Mayor gesticulating wildly to him in
the wings. Thinking there was a fire or something else
amiss, he went quickly across, only to be met with a
whispered request that he should come and have anothei 1
drink. Mac. refused, but hadn’t gone on for two minutes
before His Worship again entreated him in a loud voice to
have another drink. He took no notice, but the Mayor
was not to be baulked, and sallied on to the stage to urge
Donald to have another drink. Mac. had to postpone his
narrative for five minutes while he humored the chief
townsman by having another drink. He finished his lec-
ture with one foot resting on the prostrate form of a
Mayor who had fallen headlong on to the stage in a final
endeavor to get him to leave the post of duty and have
several more drinks.
“M.M.” writes from Townsville “ Maggie Moore,
with a ‘ complete ’ cast, ‘ new ’ scenery, and ‘ gorgeous ’
dresses (the latter chiefly red shirts, guns, and top-boots),
has just finished her Townsville season to moderately
fair business. I saw her last in ‘ Struck Oil,’ at
the Melb. Royal, in eighteen-seventy-something. She
was a slim charmer then. Now a walk twice
round Maggie Moore’s waist is all the exercise I
want in a day. In spite of advancing years and
embonpoint , Maggie still holds her own. I saw her in
‘The Eureka Camp,’ which, for unadulterated trash, I
have never seen equalled ; and she knelt down without
capsizing.”
“ S.S.” : It makes one sad to sit in the theatre and
notice the things the people laugh at. In Melbourne, for
the time being, shrieks of Gargantuan merriment are
being evoked by the old, old dodge of robbing a man of
his pants, and leaving him in a public place
dressed in the tail portion of an abbreviated shirt.
Every time the skirts of that shirt bob into
view from behind Bailey’s log, the women in the
Royal audience emit one unanimous, roof-raising yell,
and rock themselves in convulsions of merriment. At
the Princess, the only hearty laugh “ Mice and Men ” can
raise is provoked by the hero’s reply, “Go in none,”
when Peggy asks what dress she must go to the ball in.
There are smartnesses in “ Mice and Men,” but they are
wasted on the audiences, a little subtlety rendering a joke
absolutely useless for stage purposes. The drama is not
going to the dogs; it has been there and has left the
dogs again, and is now speeding towards the next animal.
Women in particular haughtily refuse to recognise new
jokes, and only appreciate the nude in comic art.
“ Melb.” : According to the bills, the incidents of the
play, “ A Woman of Pleasure,” occur in the year 1900.
Yet in the Transvaal scenes the gush is all about the
King! Victoria is not in it. “ God Save the King ” is
sung by the soldiers as they go to the bottom in the
foundered steamer.
“ Ami.” : Newspapers at large have copied the mistake
that the late Richard Stewart made his first Melbourne
appearance under Barry Sullivan’s management.
Stewart’s first appearance at Melb. Royal, under Edwards
and Fawcett, was in a drama called “Second Love,” and
the farce of “ Binks, the Bagsman.” This was two years
before Sullivan began as manager.
“ Melb.” : Not to contradict the scribe who says that
Miss Bishop acted Juliet here, to Oates’ Romeo. May be
she did, after the production I saw', when Miss Reid w'as
Juliet to the Romeo of Oates, at the Opera House. The
scenery was Irvine’s, well worn with travel. M'Mahon
was manager. Talking of worthy, long-lost Oates is a
reminder of the notorious “ Romeo ” Coates. He was a
rich fool, who amused London by acting the part.
When Bert Bailey is masquerading as a girl in “A
Woman of Pleasure” he remarks that he is not Jane
Oakebread, which allusion falls flat. In the London pro-
duction it always raises a laugh. Jane Oakebread is the
champion drunk and disorganised of Fogtown, and has
been arrested by every policeman thereabouts, save the
very newest. Policemen who have run in all the very
old-established and historical lady drunks of London say
Jane is a difficult lady to capture, owing to a dead and
helpless wav she has of collapsing sideways and becoming
like the Leaning Tower of Pisa while on her way to the
lock-up.
“Melb.”: Re “Mice and Men.” The youth of Miss
Stewart is astounding and unaccountable. I now under-
stand Moliere’s actress, Mdlle. de Brie. The public
insisted that, w'hen she was 60, nobody could act a girl of
16 w'ith such reality. When Miss Stewart will cease to be
a girl cannot be prognosticated. Of course, the whole
may be made up of stage tricks, but, if so, they' are
hidden to perfection. Look at all her movements. How
intensely girlish. But her lucky fane is her fortune. It
is the same I beheld for the first time in the dress-circle
of the old matchbox Princess’s, when Bandmann and his
wife—Milly Palmer—were also visitors there. A critic
seeing Miss Stewart for the first time in “ Mice and Men ”
might well say, “ Ah, here is the unsophisticated genius,
with no knowledge of the stage.” All is so delightfully
natural—that is to say, deceptively artificial.
During a “ turn” at the Princess Theatre, Bendigo, one
of the Florenz family of acrobats had a narrow escape.
He was somersaulting from the shoulders of one comrade
to another, then to the floor. After a revolution In the
air he landed at the right wing, when the flooring collapsed
and only the carpet saved him from going through to the
basement. Sandow appeared at Bendigo only a few days
before : it would be as well, perhaps, if an inspector of
stage-flooring followed up the Strong Man’s tracks, as his
weights are dumped about a good deal during his per-
formance.
“T.D.” : The extraordinary is getting to be much too
ordinary in stage variety business. A few years ago the
sensation act given by the family at Rickaids
would have- stirred a furore by its combination of
strength, dexteritv, and courage, and everybody would
have talked Florenz for nine days. As it was, they passed
away almost unnoticed. With such a blas& multitude to
cater for, the sensation artists a few years hence will have
to outdo the feats of the whole heathen mythology.
Typical of ill-luck—the going out of J. H. Guenett,
musical critic and teacher, just as his most famous pupil,
Melba, is approaching these shores. He probably held a
better record in the way of bike accidents than an.v other
Austral an of his years. Once he was busily infringing some
traffic bylaw or other, when the constable on duty at
Princes Bridge called on him to stop. But the musical
one had the pace on and attempted to ride through or
over the Law, with the result that Chaos supervened.
Picking himself up, the bobby, all dust and swears,
wrathfullv inquired why the etc. the cyclist didn’t stop
when told to. ‘ ‘ Does a locomotive stop for a rabbit ?
said the fallen musician.
A Melbourne pressman:
The Conservative Irvine Government has played, tb
Victorian democracy the dog’s trick the electors migh»
have expected, if they were not too well satisfied with th«.
pennyworth of opinions they buy daily from the big fac-
tories run by Syme and M ‘ Kin non to bother about fabri-
cating an idea of their own. The greediness of that little
tin Sphinx, Irvine, for a dissolution, is a most suspicious
thing. There was absolutely no call for a dissolution,
unless there was a desire on the part of a Tory Premier
with a Tory following to bow to extremely Tory agitation
and wreck the Factories Act. Irvine says he had pro-
mised the country to carry his retrenchment scheme, but
he also promised, on his most sacred honor, to save the
Factories Act. Only a trifling detail of his retrenchment
policy was threatened by the M‘ Donald amendment ; all
the Vic. factories legislation since 1896 is wrecked by the
dissolution, and Fatrnan, Esq., is abundantly glorified.
As an example of straining at the midge and swallowing
the dromedary, Irvine’s action is a record.
The Mighty Muscie Maker.
jt. alking with Eugene Sandow in his after-luncheon
mood, at Menzies’ Hotel, one learns many things
about physical culture that were not discussed by
the Strong Man when ne bared his muscles to a
crowd of invites at Melbourne Opera House. The
“ private demonstration” was just a Sandow show,
with the heavy weignts omitted, and a few ques-
tions and answers thrown in. People went away
feeling content to be ordinarily unmuscular and
out of condition. They saw in Sandow an
abnormal human fact, and they wondered how
long he was likely to live, anyhow. The light
was bad. He had the look of an overwrought
professor. “A man to be respected, but not
imitated,” was the general verdict of that rather
elderly gathering of select citizens from Collins-
street.
* »
Off the stage, Sandow is calculated Co convert
any fat infidel to the new religion of Physical
Culture in about five minutes. He looks years
younger than Sandow who posed at. that matinee
demonstration. The lines across his forehead are
incidental to his type of countenance, and would
have been deeper had he taken to the Law or
become ordained to subdue the flesh in clerical
broadcloth. Sandow’s clothes are built to dis-
guise, as far as possible, the fact that he measures
48 inches round the chest in muscular repose, and
62 inches when he chooses to expand. “If the
tailor didn’t fix me up carefully, I would look
about as broad as I am long,” says the mighty
German.
* * *
His length is The tailor’s triumphs are
of thin material, but the close fit makes them
warm to the wearer. He indulges in a little
muscle play and his coat sleeve is like a woollen
arm-skin, or his thigh seems all the same as a bar
of iron in an elastic wrapper. Sandow is always
tightening muscles, unconsciously, or with cul-
tural intent. The bracing-up of oneself here and
there in a casual way is a part of the system that
soon becomes second nature to a Sandowian man.
“ You find that many old athletes preserve their
condition wonderfully after they have ceased to
work. The habit of setting their muscles keeps
them firm,” remarks Sandow.
• • *
The question has been asked, “ Where are the
samples of the splendid results of the Sandow
teaching?” They are popping up everywhere,
and Australia may expect to produce its own
Sandows anon. “lam only five years old,” ex-
plains the Professor; “ that is to say, my classes
are only five years old, and you must wait a while
longer for extraordinary specimens ; but already
there are many very successful followers of the
system.. I get letters—hundreds of letters —from
people I have never seen, calling themselves my
grateful pupils, and so forth. Very funny letters,
also some very interesting letters, and some of
them awfully stupid. Nearly two-and-a-half
million copies of the book, ‘ Strength, and How
to Obtain It,’ have been sold. I have
appeared in many countries. England (which I
call my home) America, Italy, Holland. Physical
culture is practised as it never was practised
before. Yes, the book has been a good thing for
the London publishers and for me. I get Id. on
each copy. I ought to get 2£d. perhaps.” Thus-
far Sandow has not paid a professional visit to his
native Germany, the land of gymnasiums.
m * * , *
“ Many of the famous singers and musicians are
friends of mine,” says Sandow—“ Padetewski,
the De Reszkes, Gerardy. Ben Davies was a
pupil of mine. If he sees me sitting among the
audience at a concert he expands his chest. They
all expand their chests when they see me. It
is a greeting.” The sight of Sandow is a, signal
for chest expansion with the staff—especially the
small boy section of it—at Menzies’ Hotel. The
lift-boy makes the most of himself when the God
of Strength enters the cage. Sandow, “ the chap
what grabbed a lion and made it owl,” is a walk-
ing exhortation to brace up.
* * * *
Talking of muscular matters, Sandow admits,
under pressure, that some people have told him
he ought to have been a good singer, getting 62in.
notes off his chest. But he has not cultivated his
voice to any extent. It is very big, a basso or
heavy baritone, but still in the rough. He has
given more time to music. Like Cinque-
valli, he writes the incidental music for
his performances and lifts . heavy metal
to his own tunes. He has composed marches.
His “Marche des Athletes” is a well-known
thing. Among his very few friends in the “show”
business, Cinquevalli is the one for whom Sandow
feels a brotherly regard. They are kindred
inventors. Paul Cinquevalli will always be known
as the founder of the new school of jugglery,
wherein he has dozens of clever imitators, but still
no equal for all-round ability. Eugene Sandow
asks only to be recognised as the Father of modern
Physical Culture.
* * *
Mightier men than Sandow will arise, but
none of them will be the first to demonstrate
the greatness of little exercises in the per-
fecting of the human frame. All the credit
for introducing muscle-culture into the every-
day life of commonplace people must rest
with this genial gentleman, this thoughtful,
sprightly athlete who eats “ anything that comes
along,” but doesn’t eat too heartily of it ; drinks
whatever pleases him, and smokes rather more
freely than the average citizen. He takes a glass
of chartreuse and a cigar as he chats about the
benefits that all men and women may derive from
the “System”—which is firstly and mostly
“grip” dumb-bells of, say, 21bs. weight. The
Sandow “developer” is subservient to the dumb-
bells in its usefulness. Intelligent brain work—
concentrated thought—is essential to the best
results. Turkish baths, vapor baths—he does not
believe in them. The women who attend his
schools are noted for clear skins. If they
are not all as beautiful as Mrs. Sandow it is
because the System doesn’t originate complexions
and figures, but only improves them. “ Does Mrs.
Sandow use the dumb-bells every day ? She takes
15 minutes exercise every morning as a matter of
SANDOW (OFF THE CHAIN).
duty to herself. My maid servants attend the
school. The charwoman who washes the floor, she
objected at first to take the exercise unless she was
paid extra for it, but after a while she said,
‘Why ! Mr. Sand-how, I’d pay a bit for it meself,
rether than miss it, if yer’ll believe me.’ She is a
real cockney lady, that charwoman.”
* * *
The Strong Man, it seems, doesn’t put all his
strength into his feats. He limits his card-tearing
feat to three packs, though he can actually tear
four packs with those wonderful hands of his—BJ
size in gloves. He can tear one pack at a time
into sixteenths, if required. In the Tomb of
Hercules feat, the six men and several bar-bells
that rest upon his body are not half the weight he
is capable of supporting ; he has borne 53001b5. in
the same way. And the strength to do all this,
or a good deal of all this, may be acquired,
Sandow says, “by steady application in little
exercises that make one feel much better !” _ A
Warrnambool man who sent along a portrait of
his beauteous naked back is only one of many who
write to Sandow in the spirit of converted Sal varmy
sinners, rendering thanks to a physical General
Booth. Upstairs, in his sitting-room, Mrs.
Sandow, the tall, divinely fair personification of
all the virtues of the System, spends much time
in answering some letters and not answering
others.
* * * »
One youth writes from Melbourne city: “ About
eight months ago I read of you in a boys’paper,
and was so interested I bought one of your
developers. lam only 16 years of age, but since
using the developer my measurements have in-
creased rapidly. I can lift a bag of oats weighing
2001 b. from floor to shoulder.” A grateful disciple
writes from Port Melbourne: “Four years ago,
at the age of 27, I had the pleasure of reading one
of your books on strength,- and then I started the
exercises you advocate. At the time I started I
was just the usual scrag." The particulars given
by the writer of this letter were sufficiently
remarkable to get him an introduction to Sandow.
He has developed from the “ usual scrag” into
a local champion at weight lifting. “ The letters
look like puffs for patent pills, ” says Sandow, ‘ ‘ but
they are interesting to me —some of them.
The gentleman who writes for _ advice as to
eye gymnastics and nasal drill is not so in-
teresting. I don’t understand drilling noses.”
Having lodged this mild complaint, the patient
Sandow parts from his interviewer with a splen-
didly-developed air of friendliness, and prepares
to take his wife for a drive in a barouche. It is
quite a soft thing to be a Strong Man. Sandow
doesn’t even need to worry about walking exer-
cise. Lolls back in the carriage, sets the muscles
of his legs now and again, by accident as it were,
and continues to measure fully 18in. round his
calf. What could be easier ?
EDUMND FISHER.
At Launceston (Tas.) Criminal Court, the
other week, a farmer was charged with indecently
assaulting his 18-year-old step-daughter. The
damsel admitted, under cross-examination, that
she trumped up the charge out of spite
against the prisoner, who wanted her to go out
into service. Case dismissed. Nothing hap-
pened to girl, as per usual.
Spring 1 Time Cleaning.
Spring has arrived, and now the careful housewife
goes through the annual Spring-house cleaning.
The Summer will soon be upon us, and at this
time of the year we should take care to cleanse the
whole human system, and fortify it to withstand
the enervating effects of the Australian Summer.
The increasing hot weather of this period has an
extremely debilitating effect on the system if it is
not in perfect working order all through. It will
be thus seen that a medicine which will regulate
and cleanse the interior mechanism of man
is especially valuable at this time of the year.
Such a medicine is Bile Beans, and no better
preparation can be found for toning up the
digestive organs, cleansing the blood, and invigo-
rating the liver. Spring generally brings with it
am assortment of ailments, such as spring fever,
tired feeling, loss of appetite, etc. For these take
Bile Beans, which are also a specific for bilious-
ness, indigestion, constipation and debility, which
are also prevalent at this season. As a purgative
Bile Beans are acknowledged without an equal in
Australia. Their price places them within the
reach of everyone, and no one should run the risk
of being in ill-health through the summer, when a
short course of Bile Beans would effectually purify
the body and keep it in thorough working order.

Re the“poor shepherd” (8., 19/4/’O2). An Australian
parson of my acquaintance, at my suggestion, started
reading, from the pulpit, names and amounts paid by
members of the flock towards his stipend. Also, he read
—in a particularly loud voice—the names of those who
failed to contribute. After about three years in the
parish, he had quite as much money as he needed, and I
was good for a tenner from him at’any time. If Brown
gives £5, Jones—as good a man, anyday—will give more
than two-and-six, or break something; and the parson
scores every time. The dodge has been practised on
O’Brien, Moran, and 00. for generations, and has never
been known to fail. Call their names, by all means!
Appeal tb their passions, or their sense of dacency, or
whatever, you like to call it, and gel the money l

“A Weary Politician ” :
The practically forced levy upon a N.S. Wales for
subscriptions to his-electorate’s associations and organisa-
tions is had enough but it is nothing like such a burden
as the time-tax levied by the people who solicit high for
billets. -As things are, with'the Public Service Board.in
the ascendancy, M’s.P. can seldom obtain positions for
electors, but constituents will not believe that, and insist
on his trying all the same. “I know if .you’ll <put in a
word for me with O’Sullivan it will be all right,” is the
formula; “You‘know I’ve always supported you—and
always hope to.” Members must submit or go out of
public life.
The Fiery Cross, the Edinburgh publica-
tion of our old friend Theodore Napier, is a
“Scottish Nationalist” paper, believes in Home
Rule for Scotland and .objects to the present
dynasty. It prints without a smile this sugges-
tion from a correspondent :
The ruling family in Great Britain was chosen simply as
a matter of expediency. The only conditions of succes-
sion were “ the royal line and Protestant religion,” and
the choice was merely Parliamentary. Being such, it can
with as much ease be set aside as made. A fresh choice
would bring to a happy end many of the dangers that
threaten the Empire. The chief arise from the
discontent of Ireland, the fresh Ireland erected
in South Africa, and the just indignation of the
Scots against the false and obnoxious “ Seventh.” Now
when the Boer war broke out it was pointed out and
explained that President Kruger was ■ descended from
Rob Roy, the famous Highland chief ; but when we come
to inquire into the ancestry of Rob Roy, we find that
through his mother he was descended from the Campbells
—his great-grandfather being Sir Duncan Campbell of
Glenorehy, known as “ Duncan with the Cowl.” This
Duncan was eighth in descent from Edward 111., of
England, through Joan Beaufort, and also seventh in
descent from Robert 11. of Scotland—the first of the
Stuart kings—so that President Kruger.js of the blood-
royal of both England and Scotland. He was of the royal
line and Protestant religion. Let Parliament make him
King. Ireland will be pleased, the Boers will rejoice in
union with the Empire, and Scotland will be happy in a
foul stain removed; nay, America, lost to us by the
bungling Hanoverians, may in the best sense again’offer
“ hands across the'sea.”

PERSONAL AND PERTINENT. —Those who have Dot met Herr Rassmussen, and
who have not given his Alfaline Herbal Remedies a trial, may find it beneficial to become
acquainted with the good work which he has done in the interest of suffering humanity for
the last twenty years.
ALONG BACK IN THE BEGINNING OF THE EIGHTIES
Herr Rassmussen introduced his now world-famed Alfaline Herbal Remedies to the Australian public at 547 George
Street, Sydney. Australia was then flooded with American importations and proprietary remedies, manufactured
by doubtful persons with assumed names and fictitious addresses, who took the people’s money without
responsibility, and very often without giving them fair value for same.
HERR RASSMUSSEN BROUGHT ABOUT A REVOLUTION
by straightforward and fair dealings with the public, offering to stake his reputation and future if his remedies
were not as efficacious as he claimed them to be, accepting all responsibility, and willing to abide by the people’s
verdict. •
THE HAPPY RESULT IS KNOWN TO EVERYONE.
Over twenty years have passed away, hundreds of remedies have come and gone—many of them were forced pn
the public in numerous designing ways—thousands of pounds spent on advertising same with the assistance of the
world's greatest experts in laying traps for the unwary; but the public would have none of them, and they have
disappeared as suddenly as a flash of lightning.
“THE ALFALINE REMEDIES EXCELL ALL OTHERS,”
and are well known to be more popular to-day than they have ever been. They have not only obtained, but
have retained the goodwill and confidence of the Australian public, and have become famous throughout the
civilized world. The first Sydney Office not only yet exists, but offices have been opened up for their sale at
Melbourne, Brisbane, Adelaide, Wellington, N.Z., and in West Australia at Perth, Fremantle,.. Kalgoorlie and
Boulder City, with Agencies throughout the world.
LET THE PUBLIC JUDGE FROM THE FOLLOWING:— .
The Alfaline Remedies have held their own for over twenty years in Australia, and Herr Rassmussen has
been awarded the Parisian Aeadatnies’ Gobi Medal and Diploma of Honour; Gold Medal, Sydney, and several Silver’
Medals; honourably mentioned in almost every newspaper in Australia; received many thousands of unsolicited
testimonials—every one of the originals open for inspection at his Offices. The Alfaline Remedies have never been
known to fail to cure in a single instance. ■

Out of a column of “ Country News,” the
other day, no less than eight paragraphs published
this sort of thing : “ Councillor Dash is in a critical
state of health;” “Presentation of Bible to Miss
Sunday School,” “Ditto of purse to Rev. Mr.
Balderdash,” “ Mailthan broke his leg;” “Coun-
cillor’s son fractured his arm,” and so forth. One
has perforce to wade through a column of common
or oaten chaff to get at one small grain of wheat.
Beyond occasional tragedies, the only country
news of general interest refers to the weather and
the crops; the public has absolutely no interest
in the appearance of a pimple on Councillor
Smith’s nose, or the breaking of a Mayor’s wooden
leg through falling off a clothes-horse.

“ Solomon Smith ” to The Bulletin :
If there is one person more than another who ought to
arise and bless Richard Seddon it is the Maoriland
publican. Not content with granting them every con-
cession asked for during his reign, Richard, ere leaving
for the Coronation, proposed to abolish licensing-com-
mittees and leave it all to the S.M., but his idea perished
for want of support. The S.M. in country districts is
often a person without home or habitation beyond the
pub, and is obviously of no more judicial use than a swag-
man, who naturally enough “ lays up” to the publican
who gives him a shakedown. It will soon be impossible
to convict a publican of anything less than wilful murder,
and the police, knowing this, don’t try. In cases of
breaches of the Licensing Act, such as Sunday trading,
supplying drink to children, &c., the beak usually speaks
strongly on the matter, commends the action of the
police, and reserves his decision. Next court day the
case is dismissed—very often with an apology from the
bench. The licensing ballot at the general election, con-
taining three issues—license, reduction, or no license—is
the nearest approach to the three-card trick so far —
the chances all in favor of the publican, and hedged round
with so many conditions that if every one voted no license
it would probably turii out illegal. When the downfall
of Richard comes to pass, the publican will most likely be
the first to desert him ; and the cold-tea and anti-
monopolist parties will treasure up these things against
him.

“ Veteran ’’ ..writes :
“Koorawattia” (B. 30/8/’O2) is quite right about Roberts
in S’Africa. Roberts did begin the farm-burning business,
and it was all the more inexcusable because, in his two
books, “ The Rise of Wellington ” and “Forty-one Years
in India,” he has incidentally shown the folly of such
measures. In Afghanistan, he'always carefully protected
the villages, and. had he not done so, he and his division
would have perished with cold and hunger at Cabul.
Roberts gained nothing, and lost a great deal, by changing
his earlierand saner opinion. Heneitherstopped’“ sniping”
nor train-wrecking—he didn’t even check them—bv
burning and blowing up farms. He lost the supplies o'f
odds and ends of produce they would have furnished had
they been let alone, and his army suffered in health
accordingly. He also lost the little scraps of information
about the commandoes that would have been picked up
from the people at the farms. Farm-burning employed a
great many men and horses, who were thus diverted
from the prosecution of the main business in hand—
catching and beating the commandoes. Kitchener
stopped train-wrecking by placing blockhouse-lines along
the railways. They made it too dangerous to tamper
with the lines, and could, and did, transmit warning
signals to trains. But Roberts is emotional, and there-
fore has a tendency to be cruel from passion, whereas
Kitchener’s cruelty is that of a machine which crushes
what comes in its way. He would probably not have
bothered with farm-burning and concentration camps if
Roberts had not bequeathed the ideas to him.

THE FIGHT UNDER THE SCAFFOLD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The road from Conroy’s Flat to the saw-mills is
steep and red ; the bush that hangs over spur and
cutting is tarnished with the powdered earth,
bruised up by the hot hoofs of innumerable cattle.
In wet seasons the flat becomes navigable, and
the drover, who jests according to the language at
his disposal, cries aloud for Government transports
to float his cattle off the land. His curse is clear
and bell-like when the moving slime climbs to the
bellies of his mob, and grips him stirrup and
girth.
There is a cutting on the Castlereagh known as
“ Murphy’s Leap.” In the early ’so’s, one Phidias
Murphy lived alone on the flat. All his life,
Murphy had been accustomed to the nerveful
clamor of Limerick town, and life on the Castle-
reagh, with its alarming silences and scarcity of
traffic, tapped his nerves by day and poisoned his
mind by night. Also, the strange resemblance that
Thursday bore to Friday began to prey upon him.
It was the dreadful procession of Sundays which
hurt most. He could discover a slight difference
between the Mondays and Saturdays, but the thin
grey line of Sabbaths became maddening fixtures
in his life. Each Sabbath heralded the same far-
away church-bell, the drone of the far-away ser-
mon, the dreamful rhythm of the far-away dog-
fight in Doughboy Hollow.
One morning, when Tuesday was sliding into
Thursday. afternoon, Murphy took a long rope,
fastened it to a tree overhanging the cutting,
slipped the _ other end round his neck, and
moistened his lips. Away on the flat was his
homestead—the distance lending it the quaint
blueness which tinged its mortgage-papers.
Murphy* drew a long breath, and gently launched
himself into Space. At that moment Space was
recovering from an attack of indigestion, and it
did not feel equal to swallowing a selector
named Phidias; Space closed its mouth, and
the Irishman’s knot holding the rope to the bough
slithered and gave way. The cutting was 30ft.
deep as a stone falls, but Murphy managed to fall
in circles .which, according to, measurements of cir-
cumference and diameter, gave him a drop of
100 ft. or thereabouts. For three hours he re-
mained voiceless, immovable, a blot on the land-
scape, until Brannigan came along with a dray and
removed him to the hospital at Conroy’s. The
attendant medico diagnosed his case as one of acute
dropsy and stated that Murphy would be unfit
for any kind of intelligent work thereafter. Six
months later Murphy joined the police.
«*• • '
Black-tracker Harry Weeks rode beside the
coach a.s it pulled up outside the “Swag and
Pannikin.” Publican Primrose hurried from the
bar as the two policemen jumped from the coach
holding between them a man with blood-shot eyes
and a hanging jaw, the handcuffs on his wrists
jangling like harness. They shoved him forward,
stiffly pressing him to a sitting position on the
verandah. “ Give him a drink, Primrose.” One
of the police beat the flies from his own hot face
and sidled into the shade.
The man with the hanging jaw humped his back
like a hurt beast. “Be crise, if me arrums ,wus
free now, if me arrums wus free I’d brake both
yer crawlin’ spines ! ” Hot sweat swam on his
brow. A crowd of loafers came up from Branni-
gan’s Hole.
“Give him a drink,” said the trooper again.
“ Beer, rum, anything he wants—any thing to shut
his mouth.”
The prisoner accepted a pannikin of beer—took
it in gulps with his eyes fixed on his captors.
Publican Primrose stood near the police staring
hard at the empty pannikin in his hand. “ Cattle
duffin’ ? ” he asked, politely.
“No;” thepolicemanintheshadeturnedsharply,
the sun seemed to be burning his eyes. “ Murder,”
he said, and looked at the ground. “ Got him in
the gully back of the sawmills. He bit like a
dog.”
“Ye got me asleep.” The prisoner with the
hanging jaw writhed. “Bare-futted ye came
upon me, orl three of ye . . . stuffin’ three re-
volvers in me face. If I had me arrums free
neow—if I had me arrums free. ...” Froth
dripped over his black beard in his rage.
“Let’s have a room and a couple of chains."
The trooper in the shade half-turned as he ad-
dressed the publican. “We start again at day-
break. Ready, Frank ? ”
The two police seized the prisoner, neck and
heel, and bore him to a room at the rear of the
hotel. ‘
Towards night the hotel front had the appear-
ance of a race-meeting. Buggies and saddle-hacks
lined the road, for the news had spread along the
Castlereagh that Black Donnelly, the murderer, ■
lay chained to a four-post bed at the Swag and
Pannikin. Publican Primrose served out the beer
with a beaming eye, his two wet hands working '
like a conjuror’s as he gathered up the inrushing
sixpences. “A good murder,” he said toone of
his customers, “ improves me business ; a circus
takes it away. A little murder now an’ again
doesn’t do heny harm ; besides, Black Donnelly
on’y killed a p’liceman.”
Constable Murphy strolled up and down between
the lines of buggies, stopping at times to pass a
word with the women seated alone in the carts.
Talk of murder ran from tongue to tongue ;
half-drunken cockies nursing some single hatred
in their breasts—hatred against a squatter, an
inspector of stock, a storekeeper—drew aside in
groups and discussed the best means of killing an
enemy without being found out. They harangued,
gesticulated, cursed at the sky. Yet in sober
moments they were shy, peaceful men, who said
“Good day” to their enemy somewhat timor-
ously, and looked upon a mounted trooper with
reverence and admiration. Several of these men
brought out drinks to Constable Murphy, who
accepted them at first somewhat stiffly ; later he
unloosened his belt and placed his helmet on the
verandah. Someone hinted that Murphy’s ex-
{>erience in the way of murders would be worth
tearing. The constable accepted a proffered beer,
eyed the froth somewhat kindly, and then divided
it into three gulps.
It was a hot night. Constable Murphy looked
around at the group of respectful figures before
him and wiped his mouth. “ ’Twould not be news
to many of ye to hear that I was a warder wanst
in a gaol that shall not be mentioned by me. Any-
how, I dhropped into the billet. ’Twas about a
week afther me appointment that a murderer
fiamed Bailey was sintenced to be hanged. A
pure divil he was, with the head of a gorilla an’
the stoop av wan, who passed the day jeering at
the warders an’pretendin’ he was mad. Passin’
his cell wan day I put me head in at the dure an’
gave a look at him. ‘Bailey,’sez I, ‘there’s a
poultice for your neck somewhere in the hemp-
room.’
“ ’Twas against the rules speakin’ to the con-
demned, but he had the tusks av a wild baste an’
the mouth av a cat. ‘Go away, Fenian,’ sez he,
‘ there’ll be somethin’ wrong wid your own wind-
pipe before long. I’m a plumber an’ can see by
the shape av it that it’ll be choked some day. Go
away, Fenian,’ he sez.
“I walked from the cell in a cowld sweat. A
few days afther, the deputy-came to me an’, sez
he, ‘ Murphy, go under the gallows an’ clear out
the thruck an’ rubbish that has been allowed to
accumulate. Bailey will be hanged to-morrow,
an’ ’twould be a pity if the mahn didn’t have a
clear dhrop. Clane it out at wanst,’ sez he.
“Well, wid me moind on wan thing an’ me
hands on another, I forgot his instructions en-
tirely. I was new to the thrade, an’ the frequent
throubles wut prisoners was disthractin’ to me.
“Next mornin’ I was up early to relieve warder
Finnerty whin, divil take it, I remimbered the
deputy’s instructions about the thruck an’ rub-
bish under the scaffold. By the clock it was a
quarter to eight. * Begob, ’ sez I, ‘ Bailey will not
have a clear dhrop afther all.’ I tuk a shovel an’
fairly jumped into the ‘separate’ depot yard,
triangular in shape, an’ given over to the hang-
man an’ reporters.
“Itwasdarruk under the scaffold an’ full av
odd things that Connolly an’ the rest had thrown
in from time to time—ciment barrels, a load av
bricks an’ a cookin’ sthove, right beneath the
thrap dive an’ fair in Bailey’s way. ’Tis a wonder
that such misconductions are allowed !
“ ‘ The curse of Crummle on the thruk,’ sez I,
hurling the sthove into the yard. ’Twas a warm
day, and the sweat stood on me as I threw out the
bricks. ‘ But there’s time enough, ’ sez I,
‘before the hangman takes Bailey’s measure,’
an’ I heaved the cement cask fair at a re-
porter’s shins. He was shtandin’ wut wan eye
fixed on the hook under the beam, bitin’ a bit av
a pencil. Three more reporters came past the
soup-lift an’ stood under the wall. I worked
harrud at the bricks, an’ devil a wan seemed to
notice me. Then I heard footsteps above me.
‘ ’Tis the governor,’ sez I, seein’ that the way is
clear. ‘ Plenty av time,’ sez I, an’ threw out
another ton av bricks. A mimber av Parly-
ment wint past, an’ the smell av his
whisky breath put new loife into me. Wut me
back bint so, I troo out orl the bricks. I was
spittin’ on me hands, thinkin’ av Mary Casey,
away in Bungaree, when the thrap-dure av the
scaffold opened, and Bailey shot down, strikin’ me
fair betune the shoulders. ‘ Good mornin,’
Bailey,’sez I, spittin’on the flure, ‘you’re down
early.’ I stipped away to get clear av him, but
his two feet kicked me sthrait in the back. ‘ Bad
luck to ye,’ sez I, ‘is that fwhat you’re after ? ’
an’ I gave him wan on the chest wit me shovel.
But, quick as loife, he swung at me his
two thick knees, doubled. ‘Howld, Bailey,’
sez I, duckin’, *ye have me in the corner;
but, howly saints, I could see that he was grinnin’
under his cap, grinnin’ he was as his two heels
took me full in the face. I crawled to the flure an’
was around the other side av him before he could
swing back an’ I gave it him wut the shovel on the
chest." ‘Be still now, Bailey,’ sez I, ‘ an’ die aisy.’
An’ because he hung still for the part av a
second I wint close, foolish loike, an’ believe me,
men, he kicked wut the strength of a stallion ;
’twas on the bricks in the middle av the yarrud I
found messilf all bruised and cut.
“The deputy came an’found me so. ‘You’re
dhrunk, Murphy,’ sez he wut hell in his eye. ‘ I
will recommend your dismissal.’ ‘Pardon me,
deputy, ’ sez I, ‘ ’tis sthrugglin’ wut Bailey I have
been, fightin’a hangin’man under the gallows.’
‘ Rubbish, fiddlesticks, ’ sez he, ‘ there’s too much
whisky at our executions of late. Go to your
quarters. I will recommend your dismissal. And
dismissed I was because I towld the thruth in a
gaol that was a Babel of misconductions.”
Constable Murphy wiped his hot brow and
drank deeply from a glass of beer, with his face to
the sky. “Warders an’ drovers are alike by
thrade—the wan drives men, the other cattle.”
“I’d sooner take me chance with the cattle,”
said a Monaro man, from the verandah.
“Thrue.” Constable Murphy put on his belt
slowly. “Nothin’but hate can live betune the
walls ov a gaol—hate and damnation.”
The crowd watched him as he strode down the
dark bush road, helmet in hand, swathed in an
atmosphere of beer and officialdom.
Away at the back of the pub. was a small group
of cockies peeping through a crack in the weather-
board at the prisoner with the hanging jaw, tossing
on a mattress.
ALBERT DORRINGTON.
A Burnt Sacrifice.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A fluttering of golden wings, a wild burst of
trilling, and the bird paused, nonplussed at its
master’s preoccupation. But upon his young life
had come a new idea, a great cloud that swamped
all the reason of his years. He had just dis-
covered that he was, as yet, Unsaved.
His aunt, under whose guardianship he had just
come, knew all about the movements of God and
the devil, and only last night she had told him the
story of Abraham being commanded to sacrifice
Isaac, and of the Flood. It was all new matter to
him. She told him the story of Jacob, and his
eager, rapt race filled her heart with affection.
“ I like more fair-ee tales,” piped the little boy.
“Tell me ’nother.”
In her shock, aunt’s spectacles shattered them-
selves on the floor. She explained that she had
not been telling fairy-tales, but truths from the
Bible, but his young mind scorned belief; and
therefore she set out to explain to him the mystery
of God, and the penalties attendant on the preser-
vation of the soul, according to her ideas.
It fell like a cloud on his life. It terrified him
to imagine that he was damned for ever on account
of the apple Adam had taken, and the peaches he
himself had taken and of which he had not re-
pented. He had been brought up before without
any knowledge of these things, and only now he
knew he was Unsaved.
What did the Bible say ? Adam and Eve had
stolen apples, and he had stolen peaches. Evi-
dently stealing fruit was a terrible crime and had
a heavy punishment. And now to find redemp-
tion. Joseph had been saved by being his father’s
favorite son. Jacob had been saved by running
away and having a dream. None of them showed
him a way. Abraham, however, did something
to earn salvation. He sacrificed, or would have
sacrificed, for that purpose what he loved best. It
seemed hard on Isaac, but the method was prob-
ably the right one. The boy wrestled with him-
self.
*•* ‘ *
His aunt awoke from her nap to the realisation
of a peculiar smell of burning. In the garden she
found her nephew, on his knees, beside a, curling
string of smoke.. He was weeping bitterly.
“ 1 loved it ve’y much, please Dod, but I don’t
want to go to Hell,” he was sobbing.
The smell was of burned feathers. His pet bird
had been made a burnt offering.
And the little boy wept over his salvation.
FAVEN.
“ Malle ” offers a social theory which has
at least novelty to recommend it:
There has been much printed grief over the decline of
the authorised birth-rate and the infrequency of mar-
riage, and it is proposed to legislatively lift the stigma,
&c., and thereby hall-mark an increasing proportion of
the population who would otherwise be social Brumma-
gem. No practical remedy has been suggested, except a
tax on bachelors, which, except financially, would be a
failure. Women are continually entering fresh indus-
tries, decreasing the demand for males, and making
matrimony an impossibility for them. Numbers of
women in good billets can, however, afford to support a
husband and provide his daily beef, and a large section of
tired Australian men regard the gospel of Mantalini with
approval. Something should be done to bring these
factors together, and I humbly suggest a revision of the
marriage laws, and the introduction of a system under
which a woman can hire a husband if she yearns that
way, it being understood that in country engagements
fare shall be paid one way if the elusive party in question
stays six months. Govt, might encourage the innovation
by starting a bureau for unemployed husbands. The
great trouble will be fixing the remuneration, therefore
both parties must form unions and come under the juris-
diction of the Arbitration Court. The blushing temporary
bride of 20 will have a big pull over the yellow, sun-dried
ancient. As there’s not enough husband to go round,
and polygamy is looked on with disfavor, this seems the
only way out of a very big difficulty.

The Great Conversion.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The young apostle came
Before the Elders grey—■
His eyes they were aflame—
But smiling-sour were they.
His eyes they were afire,
His heart a burning coal
“ And what is your desire ? ”
“ To die—and save a soul.”
“We hear the Heathen call,
We hear the Pagan groan,
Your mission is—to All,”
Said Elder MacElhone.
The young priest shook his head—
“ Your views should larger be;
I wish, dear sirs,” he said,
“ You had some charity.”
The Elders’ smiles were sad,
On cold, blue, bloodless lips—
The irony they had
Would plate five battleships.
“ Your work.is in the East—
There is your field,” they said.
The pale, young mission, priest
He shook again his head.
t ... ...... . „
“ I have a larger plan—
I have a finer scheme :
You aave the soul of man—
And what is it ?—A Dream.
“The heathen fills his place ;- -
God knows, but doesn’t care.
HE strolls around through space
With comets in His hair.
“ Eight "hundred million men
Are pagans. What says He ?
‘l’m busy now ’ ‘ And when ? ’—
‘Say next Eternity.’. .
“ Your missions bear their fruit •
The ends of earth you’ve trod ;
I go tp strike the root,
And make a saint of God.”
The chairman, with a sob,
Said—“ Brother, you can die :
You have a bigger job
Than ever we would try.”
CREEVE ROE.
The Barmaid on Trial.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This is the plea of the Barmaid, called to show
cause why she should not be deprived of her
livelihood* and struck off the rolls of those doing
decent and honorable work. It is not the plea of
one, but of a dozen; not of an individual, but of
a class ; and it is not set forth quite as it is given
—much luxuriance of straying reflections, by and
large, being trimmed off. The barmaid is but
human (though not worse than human), and
minded to give scratch for scratch, and hiss for
hiss, which is excusable enough, in truth, but of
no interest to the Court of Public Opinion. As
counsel for the defence, then, having collected
diligently what the Barmaid has to say for her-
self, this scribe omits all that is not pertinent to
the issue, and even ventures here and there to put
a thought in briefer, if less graceful, language
than that in which it was expressed :
“ It is said that we are a moral danger to men ;
that our calling endangers our own morality; that
we induce men to drink more than they should ;
that we might, with advantagb-to ourselves and to
the community at large, give up the bars and take
to dpmestic service-. To all this we say ‘ No,’
and with but one reservation —that you under-
stand a Barmaid to be a legitimate Barmaid, the
woman employed to serve out drinks at the
average decent hotel. There are others who call
themselves Barmaids so as to be conveniently
something else—just as there are nurses, actresses,
waitresses, church-workers, whose nominal occu-
pation is only a blind over the window of their
real one. The Barmaid at some of the waterside
hotels you might name is not an argument for the
abolition of the Barmaid, but for the abolition of
those hotels. It is well at the outset to define
terms.
“ The suspicion that there is definite danger to
men’s morals in a Batmaid’s calling is a quite suffi-
cient explanation of the anxiety of other women for
their abolition. Every married woman is a self-
constituted guardian of the virtue of all married
men—except the married man, or men, she is
interested in herself. Marriage is an institution
designed for the protection of women; and women
are combined to guard its integrity ; only a strong
degree of self-interest will relax their sense of duty
in this respect. Even the average unmarried
woman (whose views as to the future generally
embrace a marriage) has a prospective interest in
keeping men ‘good.’ Generally speaking, then,
women are united in preserving what we will call
monogamy. This is partly woman’s instinctive
goodness, partly her desire to see that no one else
gets what she is not getting.
‘ ‘ But is the Barmaid’s calling immoral ? We
come on duty at (say) 10 a.m., and work until 11
p.ni., with arrangements for a weekly half-holiday
among ourselves, when (as is usually the case)
there is more than one at an hotel. All that time
we are under public observation, so to speak.
There is hardly a moment when we can rely on
being alone with anybody. We are, as a rule,
too busy even for a chat—a public chat—of any
length with a man whom we might feel inclined
to make a victim to our wiles. - Ask those ladies
who cry out for our abolition if they have ever
tried to Seduce a man from his duty in a public
place liable to constant interruption. Some of
them, you can reckon, have had some experience
in the matter, but they wouldn’t try their wiles
under those circumstances. A man has no temp-
tation that he cannot escape thrown at him in a
respectable bar. Can the same be said of every
conservatory and drawing-room ’
“On the other point—the supposed facility
given for tempting us ? Well, the same argument,
in a way, applies. The man who wants to lay his
snares before a Barmaid has to put up with a deal
of inconvenient interruption-; he would find much
better opportunity with the average society
woman. A young woman can go through her
day’s work at the bar with as much freedom from
insult or suggestion as she could go through a ball
at Government House. Coarse language ? Well,
occasionally, just about as often as one hears it
from passers-by in the street. Now and again a
cad will bellow, his oath or obscenity so that all
who have ears may hear. But it is not more often
heard in a respectable bar than in the street; and
the hearing doesn’t kill a woman’s virtue; else
there would be none, however cloistered, pure.
“ Compare our occupation with other callings—
the stage, the shops, or domestic service. Take
the last, by choice, for comparison, because it is the
suggested alternative of employment for us when
we no longer serve at the bar. The great dif-
ference between the Barmaid and the chamber-
maid is that the former can ‘lose her virtue ’ if she
chooses to; the latter often does so without choosing
to. If the citadel in the one case is carried the
garrison capitulates with the honors of war and on
set terms. It is a matter of affection or of nego-
tiation. In the oth&r case it is too often
because of mean advantage taken, of dastardly
surprise. The chambermaid, especially if em-
ployed in a Temperance Coffee-Palace, has, in
many cases, to face what it would be euphemism
to call an attempt at ‘ seduction ’ as part of every
week’s work.
“This too (let it be here explained that the
witnesses didn’t say this in so many words, but it
was clear from their hints they had it in their
liearts)-r-the Barmaid doesn’t learn to hold herself
cheaply. She is well-paid, and well-housed, and
sees enough of men to get to a fairly accurate
judgment of them. She is not foolish enough to
throw herself away.
“ They say wo induce men to drink. Well, some
of us reokon we get men to take more drinks than
they would otherwise: some of us don’t. There’s
a conflict of opinion on the point. But we are all
agreed that we.don’t encourage men to get more
nearly drunk. We claim that the most drinky
bars in Sydney aro those run by men only.
Clearly, if men who come to our bars have any
friendly feelings towards us, or any respect for
womankind generally, they won’t want to make
beasts of themselves in our presence. Men may
pay up for small lagers, or small shandies, which
they don’t want, and on which they will get ‘no
forrader,’ for the sake of a nod from a smart girl
(we are smart girls, and we know it, and the other
ones know it, and that’s the real trouble); on that
point we Barmaids differ, but we don’t encourage
drunkenness ; in fact, it is otherwise.
“ It is said we can easily find more honorable
employment as domestic servants. Now—though
this may cast a shadow of doubt over our whole
evidence, still it is honest truth—the keen demand
for good domestic servants is a lie. The woes of
unimpeachable mistresses who ‘ can’t get their
work done for love or money’ are bogus. If a
woman treats her servants decently she can’t drive
them away from her. There’s not a post in any
house where the woman in charge is ‘ all right, ’ is
a human creature with human sympathies, but
would have scores of applicants if it were vacant
to-morrow. The trouble is that the average
woman hasn’t got very much human sympathy.
Her main object in keeping a servant is ‘to keep
the creature in her place,’ just as one of the great
joys of being a Barmaid is that it riles the other
cats so much. They can’t invade our domain, but
have to trust their precious men out of their sight
in oUr bars.' "If we went out to seek work as servants,
most, of us could only get it, however willing,
however skilful, by drinking to the dregs the cup
of humiliation from the hands of another woman.
I don’t think they mean it, mind you. But
woman doesn’t know how to treat a subject
woman as a rule. There’s a class of men who
come into our bars whom we don’t know to be
married men (because they don’t look to be either
scheming a fib or dreading a nag when they call
in for a whisky before going home) until we over-
hear them say one day: ‘ Oh, my wife never has
any trouble with servants.’ Then we understand;
the wife is a good ’un. As Barmaids we are free
—in service only to men, and that’s natural ser-
vice and not slavery. We can put on a good hat
when we go out without fearing that a mistress
will see it and subject us to some special humilia-
tion for our impudence.
“We are not angels. But we don’t make men
drunkards. We don’t seduce men from their
allegiance to other women to any special extent
because, for one reason, we don’t get the leisure
or the opportunity. We aren’t seduced by men to
any marked extent, because we know thoroughly
well how to take care of ourselves, and our occu-
pation teaches us how to resist, rather than leads
us into, temptation.”
Such is the case for the Barmaid.
F.R
A Post-and-rail Fence.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A post-and-rail fence ! White, withered tree
skeletons, holding out gaunt arms to clasp hands
in the moonlight. My private shrine, wrapped in
the incense of tobacco-smoke, is that old post-and-
rail. I can shut my eyes and see it as it looked
that night, when, under the old bull-oak, I made
a discovery. Do you remember how obedient you
became ? From queen to slave —that is a descent
only a woman in love can make.
How vividly I recall it all ! The weariness of
the wait at the sliprails; the hundred possibilities
of your not coming ; the fear of some break in the
chain of circumstance. You were late. There
was a shade of annoyance in my greeting of you
on that account. I lied to myself—said you were
flirting, amusing yourself at a country lad’s ex-
pense. I questioned : Had you kissed, or been
kissed ! I gave the lover’s verdict, with a lover’s
bias, when I looked into those good, true eyes as
you said good-night.
We walked slowly to the old bull-oak and you
talked. I thought—not of what you said, but of
what I wanted to say, and your choice of subjects
never gave me a chance to connect. Then you
told me you were going away. The dead barren-
ness of.life without you spread out like the plain
before me, and the old bull-oak sighed my senti-
ment. I clasped you. “ Kiss me !” I said. Did
you ever obey a rough command so quickly ? I
“ crushed your lips till you almost cried. Oh, lass,
the memory of that first rush of passion brings the
mist to my eyes, and the vision of the post-and-
rail fence shimmers like a mirage.
We were hours going home, my arm around
your waist. Every few panels we stopped and
kissed. I laughed as you sneaked round the house
for fear of rousing the dogs. The echo of that
laugh is in my heart still.
Then—Creed and Greed took a hand, and Love
was over-matched. My love-story !If my wife
knew ! Bah—perhaps she, too, has a shrine in
some post-and-rail fence.
L.M.D.
